The Red Page
Australian
It is National Efficiency that will not onl.v
win this war but preserve the Empire.
• series of lectures upon this highly-importan
■subject was delivered by R. F. Irvine, Pro
lessor of. Sydney University
Meredith Nckinson, of the same University
and Professors W. Ilarrison Moore and W
A. Osborne, of Melbourne University, a'
Melbourne, at the invitation of F. Hagel
thorn, Minister for Public Works, Vic
These lectures are now printed under the
title, National Efficiency. R. F. Irvine ex
plains the misconceptions of the public o
the subject. “The average business man
thinks National Efficiency is identical witl
more business, higher profits, and a laboi
force that works according to his standards
and refrains from striking and making things
otherwise uncomfortable.... The working
man, knowing what is commonly in the mine
of the employer, scents danger in any talk o]
efficiency. It seems to suggest merely a
new device for over-driving, speeding up anc
getting unpaid work out of human muscle. .. .
When any or all of these are masquerading
as the ‘General Public,’ National Efficiency
is discovered to be a thing in which all gov-
ernment services are woefully lacking.” R
F. Irvine’s definition is: “The most efficient
adaptation of means to procure the highest
welfare and civilisation of a people and to
ensure its survival against internal diseases
and the attacks of other nations.”
In Australia there is a natural distrust of
monopolies and little comprehension of the
fact that combination makes possible a highei
grade of organisation. Irvine believes in
Trade Unionism as a vital factor in our evo-
lution. “To organised labor we are indebted
for a far nobler conception of what the
economic aims of a society should be.” But
he is compelled to admit that organised labor
has so far confined itself to purely protective
action. It has aimed to secure a better dis-
tribution of wealth; but it has done little,
except indirectly, to secure an ampler pro-
duction of wealth. But it is a basic truth
that neither the employer nor the employee
can work efficiently without some stimulus
in the shape of reward.
R. F. Irvine’s opinion of the business man
and the civil servant is scathing. “Business
men here are, I believe, as conservative as
civil servants. Although they travel a good
deal, my impression is that too often they
carry with them exceedingly self-satisfied, un-
trained and unobservant minds....ln cities
like Melbourne and Sydney, where the price of
electricity is very low and the cost of petrol
very high, the introduction of electric traction
is demonstrably still more economical than
it has proved to be in America and Europe.
Yet our business men still hold off without
being able to give an adequate reason. The
electric handling of heavy goods on wharves,
in stores, factories* and on railway stations
is now becoming universal abroad, but we
still persist with the laboriously slow and
very expensive hand-barrow. In this respect
privately-conducted industries are no more
adventurous than public enterprises... .1 am
amused when I hear city men talking of the
need of applying ‘business principles’ in pub-
lic administration, and in the operation of
public enterprises. In nine cases out of ten
they are speaking in complete ignorance of
the conditions under which the civil servant
has necessarily to work.... Also, I know that
among ‘down-town’ business men there is a
large annual crop of failures. It has,
indeed, been calculated that about 90 per
cent, of the new enterprises started every
year fail owing mainly to bad management.”
As for the civil servant, Irvine finds that
he adopts a hostile attitude to the public,
and regards liis business as an end in itself
and not as a rendering of the best possible
service to the public. “When he gets down
to this stage of limited vision he becomes a
singularly inventive creature. He has a
wonderful knack of manufacturing little bits
of useless procedure, of check and counter-
check. and so, like a coral insect, of building
up huge official ramparts which impede busi-
ness and lead tb over-staffing and needless
expenditure.” This curious insect has an
excessive regard for precedent and routine
experience. He comes to think that his par-
ticular knowledge and experience is of
supreme importance. So he gradually begins
and continues to do obsolete things in an
obsolete way. “The system is such that he
loses or fails to acquire that spirit of adven-
turousness without which progress is im-
possible. .. .Wisdom to him is too often
measured by the number of years he has sat
in some official chaiy. When such a man
reaches a position of responsibility he is
careful to tone down originality in those
below him. He is suspicious of ability, and
frowns upon initiative and ambition.”
Irvine illustrates by our Sydney tram-
system. From an electrical and engineering
point of view it is highly efficient, but it
fails to provide a convenient and comfort-
able service. The cars are so high that a
step-ladder is needed to get into them. They
are so constructed that passengers are con-
tinually disturbed by the entrance and
departure of other passengers and by an
obsolete and cumbersome method of fare
collection. Their construction is responsible
for a maximum of noise, dust and draught.
But the management is not wholly to blame.
We are, because we possess so low a standard
of comfort that we put up with our trams.
The remedy suggested by this alert Pro-
fessor of Economics is the establishment in
each State (why not Federal?) of a Bureau
of National Efficiency and a General Staff
acting in conjunction with it. Germany is
completely organised ; we are not. Irvine’s
Bureau is not a public library—which he
characterises as a mausoleum of dead know-
ledge, but a department for collecting know-
ledge of all devices for improving
equipment, organisation and methods of
other countries, all the thinking in this and
other countries, ana not merely classifying
them but publishing them, in the press and
in the Cabinet.
For the General Staff, Irvine confesses
that what is wanted is a brand of men that
this community has hitherto had no machi-
nery for producing: the trained and ex-
perienced efficiency expert, who will not
regard businesses and industries merely as
profit-making mechanisms, but as a co-opera-
tion of forces to increase welfare. They
must be free from “the diseases of official-
ism.”
The other essays treat this difficult sub-
ject with almost equal interest from other
points of view. The little book is cordially
recommended to those most in need of it:
the business man, the civil servant, the
trades-unionist and the politician.
li. E. Boote, who is “Touchstone” and
also editor of The Worker, collects his
weekly feature, A Fool’s Talk, in yolume
form (The Worker Trustees, Sydney, 3s.
6d.). The book is charmingly produced, and
the illustrations by C. A. Emery contribute
much to its appearance. “Touchstone” dedi-
cates his book “to the fools of the world,’ 4
but not in the hope of getting a large circu-
lation ; for the author is probably the only
fool in the world who would admit his fool-
ishness. His idea is that the only way to
get a truth accepted by the world —or at any
rate the least personally inconvenient way
—is to tell the truth in the guise of a joke.
Boote reveals himself here as a clear thinker,
with a neat literary touch and considerable
originality of thought; but the book suffers
because, while the contributions to his paper
were individually sufficiently interesting,
here they must be taken in bulk, and the
reader gets tired of too much attempted bril-
liance, and wearied by the repetition of cer-
tain overstressed ideas. And that of this
war as solely a capitalists’ war is one of
them.

“In the world of turfdom the name of
Christie Kenny was one to conjure with. It
caused the hearts of backers to beat with
elation when they saw it hoisted alongside
the number of the horse that they had
backed. At sight of it bookmakers shortened
the odds, and cursed this too popular rider,
whose mount everyone wanted to back.”
That is how Under a Cloud begins. And it
ends with a telegram: “Batlow first Tatt.’s
Cup. Good win. My name is clear. Leav-
ing here to-night for home and Elva.
Darcy.” What more could the inhabitants
of “the world of turfdom” want than this,
the seventh Australian racing story by
Arthur Wright, and the 19.746,392,387 th
shilling volume, or thereabouts, issued bv
the N.S.W. Bookstall. Sydney?
Startling war news comes to band from
Althea, whose information is now given to
the world by Motor Id. Ivroemer in his
work. Why this World Crisis? (W. K.
Thomas, Adelaide Register, S.A.). When
the war broke but the writer formed an
occult class of four members; and through
Althea, a medium, the class received a flood
of light upon all the aspects of the great
crisis. The class, it is encouraging to, note,
has now a membership of eight. It appears
that there is war in Heaven, and that the
whole solar system is affected. The spirit-
ual ruler of this world needed reinforce-
ments, and appealed to the God of Venus.
Chapter Three is entitled, “Glimpses of the
Planet Mars—Tremendous Volcanic Emo-
tions Observed.” Full descriptions are
given of the inhabitants of all the other
planets; and each planet is apparently en-
gaged in warfare rather like our own. In,
Mercury people are not born: there are no
children there. And they are able to visit
the earth. Another chapter describes the
mobilisation of the solar system; and in
another we learn —as we somehow sus-
pected—that the Jupitarians do not approve
of a White Australia. Of course, the writer
fully believes the absurd fake about the
Angels of Mons, but fails to offer any
proofs. He believes that the alleged angels
were inhabitants of the planet of Uranus.
But these were only the advance guard. A
still more glorious army is preparing to
descend and take a hand in the solution of
the world’s difficulties —the Ruler of this
world with his angels, supported by legions
from the sun. Also there are going tp be tidal
waves. ‘T felt the water rise and rise and rise,
and then it seemed as if it gave one big
‘woof,’ and it seemed that half a continent
was submerged.” One-half of the tidal wave
broke over Holland and the other over
Egypt. Now, did the wave say “Woof 1” or
“Spoof?”
At last we have an Australian poet to
sing the war. The Sony Story of the Great
War, by J. B. Fulton, Casino, N.S.W., price
6d., or. if taken in quantity, 4d., has now
reached its fourth edition, completing 8000
copies. This poem was written before break-
fast on February 5, 1015—surely a date that
will become historical! The author admits
that he has had assistance from various
people in perfecting the proofs, and so
modest is he that he “allowed the man in
the street to decide which of two words
should be used.” However, “there is no com-
pulsion to sing all the piece through. The
first part may be read, recited or sung, but
the choruses should be sung by the com-
pany.” And our national war-poet is “not
out for money”; all he wishes is to help the
Empire along.
Liege’s long-defended fortresses,
And Antwerp’s chain of forts ;
With well-trained gun and mortar piece
Are giving no reports.
In case this should be ambiguous the poet
adds a footnote: “I mean that the forts were
silenced or taken.” It is a pity that other
classical poets have not been equally elucida-
tive.
The cruel German legions all
The Vosges valleys past;
And aged men are learning now
Of war the deadly blast....
Oh, Treitzschke, war-mad Treitzschkel
You cause the mothers’ tears;
At Jesus pure Beatitudes
The crack-brained Nietzsche sneers....
And all, remember, written before break-
fast!
Health and Longevity, with an account of
the work of the Sydney Child Studv Associa-
tion (Epworth, 238 pastlereagli-street, Syd-
ney; ~s.), is something more than a report
upon this valuable voluntary organisation
devoted to the prevention and remedying of
the ailments of children, though a sane State
would surely include this work in its depart-
ment of education. A number of useful
articles dealing with the value of various
foods, and two directories, one of dairvmen
supplying what the book calls “Child Study”
milk, and the other of bakers supplying
“Child Study” bread, give the book a per-
manent value to the home.

“If the European War brings in a Confed-
eracy, of the Nations, then know that the end
of the Age L- at hand. Parallel with its
inauguration look for a great church scheme
to Christianise mankind... .Testimonies:
authentic history, wonderful Bible predic-
tions, recent , wonderful astronomical discov-
eries, mathematics, etc.—an irrefutable, con-
centrated force expressed in convincing, logi-
cal English easily understood. Accept not
the _ statements herein and you must fight
against the stars in their courses, refute his-
tory and chronology, disprove mathematics,
throw overboard all reason and intense pro-
bability. No man can do it.-. . .Examine late
English and French newspapers for signs
of this coming supreme delusion and death-
trap.. ..The last lap has now been entered.
iSo place for human foresight and sagacity.
It’s high time to take notice. This booklet
is....put forth as a gold-mine of saving
knowledge. Trained in business and verse
with science generally the writer is critical
analytical, comparing; is against blind, fana-
faith, but appeals to reason with facts
stripped of all superfluity and vain imagin-
ings. In it you’ll find boiled down consider-
able study and research after a careful exam-
ination of leading views and deductions, an
analysis and comparison of reasonings and
objections, and the elimination and
of all that is grossly imaginative and heter-
ogeneous. One who is watching.”
One Who Is Watching is Arthur
Chmo (12-14 O Connell-street, Sydney; 7d.).
Eondon Athenaeum, the most solid and
severe of English weekly journals of literary
ci iticism, will henceforth appear as a
monthly.
Mary Gaunt, author of The Uncounted
Cost, and Alone in West Africa, furnishes an
interesting “scrap of autobiography” to her
new collection of stories, Australian and
overseas, entitled The Ends of the Earth
(Laurie). “In collecting together this book,”
she says, “I seem to be going step by step once
again through my own life.” And it has
been an interesting life. She was a baby of
six when the wanderings of her parents took
her to Gippsland, where she first remembered
seeing the Australian aboriginal, and heard
tales from her father, then a goldfields
warden, of the early days. From these tales
later she constructed several of the stories
in this volume. The little band of children
as they drew up scattered literally to the
ends of the earth; and from one of the
brothers she got the materials for one of her
stories. Her brother “corrected it for me
and did the seamanship.” From him, too, she
got the incident upon which she founded her
story, “When the Colt Jammed”—the Colt
being a revolver. “And then, to my amuse-
ment, the Colt people wrote to me to say that
the Colt never jammed. It was impossible,
and if I would visit their warehouse they
would demonstrate why. They evidently
thought I was interested in munitions —I
who did not know one end of a gun from
another, and shouldn’t have recognised a
Colt if I had met it.” On her marriage the
author went to live at Warrnambool, and
there “made the acquaintance of the gentle-
man who ran an illicit still.” And then she
was widowed, and found herself in London
earning a living by her pen. “I found nothing
inspiring in the London streets, so of neces-
sity I fell back upon the incidents of my
youth for material; and as I succeeded, and
money allowed, I wandered farther afield.”
So, one tale reminds her of “a weird night I
spent alone on Anum Mountain, and the
long trek by night from Ho to Palime in the
heart of that Togoland we have just taken
from the Germans.” Another memory is that
of drifting down the canal outside the walls
of Peking,,with two companions. “Together
we three worked out the story, lunching in a
grassy graveyard, the people, courteous and
unwashed, who hoped to occupy that grave-
yard in the future sitting round us in a
ring waiting patiently for our scraps.” Then
she went further inland, where no one but a
missionary would go, and “met the man who
told me the incident of the old gun in the
gateway and the armed men hanging threat-
eningly over the city walls.” And north she
went, and saw “lying in the mouth of the
great Amur River the sealing schooners that
my brother had told me of when, years
before, together we wrote” another story.
With such a preface any book of stories
should inveigle the reader. Mary Gaunt’s
stories are good stories, though she is not a
Kipling; and, had she not so generously
given her secrets away, the reader would
have wondered how a woman could have
written stories so essentially masculine.
Russian Literature
Another of the tremendous novels r
*dor Dostoevsky, The Insulted and
has appeared in English translation hE** *’
maim, per Dymock’s Library) Tt l Ueine -
Dostoevsky’s faults and
time the novelist appears in person
how he has any chance of writing g i*
w th so many exciting even! '
whole of his (time, it is difficult tn ; • e
The Picture Dostoevsky leaves is tfiW «
man rushing from one melodramatic scene
another, taking the burden of every SVa
and injured character on his shouldeSS3
contriving to be present at almost every con
versation that takes place. And these Z
versations are of stupendous length • and S
the characters seem to have nothing X
do but to discuss, with a profound psvdiol
ogy, themselves and everybody else ‘ '
The list of characters is long and varied
and the plot is hard to summarise within
reasonable limits. A simple youth, unstV n
ft 11 * yet with the pure heart’
Dostoevsky’s belief, covers a multitude
puerilities, falls in love with a pure amt
splendid girl. She goes to live with £
because she loves him, not because he
promised to marry her. He is the son of .
super-scoundrel, a. clever and depraved
prince. This personage schemes to part the
lovers, since he wants his foolish son to
marry an heiress. Incidentally, he ruins the
girl’s father. Meantime the novelist rescues
an epileptic child from a woman of notorious
character; and the mystery of the identity
ot this strange mite is curiously mixed un
with the plot. But there are a hundred other
sub-plots, all handled with a masterly crafts-
manship. But it is not in the dramatic con-
trasts so prodigally provided that the in-
terest of the novel lies. It is in the extra-
ordinarily acute analysis of strange charac-
ters, in the novelist’s love for the despised
and rejected of men, and in his vivid descrip-
tions of every side of Russian life, that
Dostoevsky proves himself a genius. All that
need be said is that if you like Dostoevskv
you will like The Insulted and Injured—
though not so much as you will like The
Brothers Karamazov.
J*
Mimi’s Marriage, by V. Mikoulitch (Un-
win), is the first example of the light
Russian novel to be translated. It is the
slightest of stories, merely the account of a
foolish, empty-headed but pretty young girl
—her engagement, her jilting, her marriage
of convenience to an ugly old General, her
meeting with the usual handsome young
lover, the inevitable consequences, and her
return to the boredom of her unsuspecting
husband. But the picture is more than a
sketch, though it is painted with the lightest
of strokes. The author has a talent for
genial satire. The mind, or what passes for
the mind, of the useless but charming Mimi
is brilliantly analysed ; and the portraits of
the other characters are drawn with a light
but incisive sureness. The love affair and
its “correct” ending are delightful comedy.
The reader unacquainted ivith Russian liter-
ature would do well to begin with Mimi.

Of C. J. Dennis’s The Songs of a Senti-
mental Bloke, three editions, aggregating
over 12,000 copies, were sold within three
months of publication. As this is certainly
an Australian, if not a world, record for
poetry, it may be expressed in a more strik-
ing way. Counting in Sundays and holidays
the bookseller’s average day is seven hours.
As Dennis sells at least 133 copies a day.
that works out, roughly, as the sale of -0
copies every hour the bookshops are open.
So a copy of The Songs of a Sentimental
Bloke is being bought, somewhere in Aus-
tralasia, every three minutes!
“Few authors know how many markets
there are for a book or play,” remarks an
English literary agent, in his li‘Ue persua-
sive pamphlet. The manager mentions the
case of “a certain novel .of universal inter-
est" managed by his linn. Already he has
sold 26 separate rights in the book, and he
is still discovering other markets for it. _ The
list is interesting: American serial rights,
English serial rights, Australian serial
rigir*. the corresponding book rights in these
countries, and a heap of European book
rights, including the Continental rights in
the English language. Russian rights
(though there is no cvnyri/ht in Russia, and
the sale amounted y to £5). dramatic
rights in six count.: - world-film rights,
second serial right- Great Britain, six-
penny and sever ./ lights in Great
Britain, and 50c >» r.ghts in America.

Cartridges
For ' Rifles, Revolvers or Pistols
The most accurate shooting rifle, revolver
or pistol made will do unsatisfactory work
unless the cartridges used in it are as accurate
as the arm. For that reason it is important
when buying to get cartridges that have an
established reputation for quality. Win-
chester —the W brand of cartridges in all
calibers from .22 to .50 are unexcelled for
accuracy, penetration and uniform shooting
qualities. They cost no more than inferior
makes and are for sale everywhere. If you
want to get the most satisfactory results with
your rifle, revolver or pistol, always use
Winchester ammunition. Do not simply
ask for “some cartridges” when you buy,
but be sure to specify Winchester make
and look for the bold W on the box.

The Helping Hand
To-day, some poor, suffering soul has been
turned away from St. Vincent’s Hospital
turned away in anguish and despair because
your helping hand was not outstretched in
time. “There is no room!” was the only
answer that could be given to that poor
soul’s appeal. Can you withhold the
Helping. Hand any longer?
A million shillings are needed to provide
150 more beds. Let one shilling at least
come from you. Hand it in to any local
collector and receive a coupon in exchange.
Twenty shillings or coupons entitle you to a
ticket in a draw for an Engaved Solid
Gold Plaque, value One Hundred and
Fifty Pounds sterling, and the entry of
your name as a member of
The Million Shilling League

the central office.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beach Thoughts.
Friend, strong friend, you were there with
me,
That grey day on the sounding beach ;
” hat was the message the great sea gave,
What did the moving waters teach?
Low clouds hung in the heavens above,
Massed and sullen, and dull and grey;
Little by little the tide stole out—
Friend, was it Hope that ebbed that day':
Sinoke-drifts moved on the distant verge,
Seabirds hovered above the wave ;
How could the mind take golden thought,
How could the heart in that hour be brave?
Spindrift, born of the breaking seas,
Wraith-like drifted across the sands;
No child, girdled with foam, cast back
Gold, wet tresses with hasty hands.
Ebbed the tide, on the lone sea-beach,
Inch by inch till the black night fell;
Dull, grey hearts and a dull, grey s^a —
Friend, O friend, they were mated well.
Tides have risen, and tides have ebbed
Since that hour of the leaden scene;
Many tracks on the sands washed out
Tell the tale of the days between.
Friend, strong friend, we are side by side,
This gold day on the shining beach ;
What is the message the great sea bears,
What do the moving waters teach?
All is azure, and gold, and pearl—
Wave sounds beating like triumph-drums ;
Little by little the tide moves in—
Friend, it is Hope that hither comes.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Edge of the Sea.
Were we forty? Both of us,
Were we seventeen?
How the little wavelets fuss
And the rollers green
Rise like some pellucid hills
In a flow that never stills.
Were we old and sad, alas!
Were we gods, and young?
How the wind among the grass
Mocks with golden tongue
Shivering blades on creamy sands
In between our clasping hands!
Were we married? Honor bound?
Were we free to woo?
How the waves’ tumultuous sound
To a paean grew,
Rivalling the rapturous breeze
Choiring in the clasping trees!
Little crabs along the sand
Wrote with scurrying feet
Something gods can understand,
Subtle, warm and sweet.
Here and there the sand was stirred
By a trident-footed bird —
Bachelor piper of the beach,
Or the wooing gulls:
Llere a fish flashed out of reach —■
Boat with silver sculls;
While the sea wrote on the shore
Notes of an orchestral score.
Never there again for me!
Something blots across the scroll,
That is made of wind and sea
And the harping of a soul.
What has fallen from my hand—
Love and youth.... or only sand ?
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Regret.
When darkness cloaks the feet of Time
Then I wide-eyed, devoid of sleep,
Will listen to the ghostly rhyme
That drums my ears since one did creep
To me from Life’s cruel battle field—
My brother, sorely bruised that day—
Bowed low his head, and soft appealed,
And I, alas! I turned away.
Could we recall the chances lost,
The heedless, callous things we did,
That laid remembrance under frost —
Oh me, what pain from life we’d rid!
Each hour there pleads to deafened ears,
To eyes that look yet do not see,
A brother’s wound, a sister’s tears —
Ah, seize the opportunity!
N.S.W.
J. B.
The Saving Sense.
The dull-faced gaoler looked at me and
grinned.
His eyes said this : “Come in. my fool, and
stay,
While the slow years
Painfully pass you like a weary wind,
And I will rot your soul the prison way
With shameful tears.”
He placed about me shapes obscenely
wrought,
Faces like masks grotesquely eaten deep
By venal crime.
Cold, dying minds exhaled putrescent thought
That battened on the clinging things that
creep
In prison slime.
But in the safe discreetness of my cell
Ever an imp of laughter sat to kill
The day’s disgust;
His gift o’ nights a bright dream caravel
Of tossing sails the free wind loved to fill
With starry dust.
Star dust of mirth! And now that lam
free,
Sane and untouched by all that loathsome
net
Of prison snares,
Not unto any priest-taught deity
But to the laughter god I pay my debt
Of wrinkled prayers.
MAORILAND.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
. (In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every iveek for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding tivo columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. Tiie Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted , for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

don, after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs.
Gordon and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.

THURSDAY, MARCH 2, 1916.
THE DECORATIVE JOB AND THE
MAN WHO COUNTS.
Armageddon has its hilarious aspects, any-
how, and .John Bull in sorting himself out
and seeing how many of him there is for
achieving the victories of peace and war and
disposing of himself in khaki and munition
factories is finding humorous interludes at
unexpected places. John has , two main
considerations in view—one to raise an
adequate army and the other not to cripple
any essential industry by draining away
therefrom the labor that keeps it moving.
To achieve the first he lately adopted a
diluted form of compulsion; to ensure the
second he provided numerous loopholes
through which escape from service might be
perpetrated. Exemption is allowed in the
case of unmarried citizens who, between the
ages of 18 and 41 years, are
1. Wanted in national employment.
2. The support of other persons.
S. Possessed of “conscientious objections,” such as
Quakers.
To make the first ground of exemption
clearer many industries have been specified
by proclamation, being in the main those
callings from which the output forms the
backbone of the national exports. Also local
tribunals have been established all over the
country to settle claims for exemption, and
it is from these bodies that weird and in-
structive gleams are thrown on the situa-
tion. All sorts of fantastic claims to escape
the ranks defending the nation are being
advanced. There was a yarn the other day
about a footman who adds to the dignity of
the Lord Mayor of London ; the heart-break-
ing case of a man employed by a missionary
society in “packing Bibles”; and the woes
of a butcher who was threatened with the
loss of an indispensable assistant. But
behind the comedy, of the footman, and the
man with Bibles, and the butcher —all in
its way emblematic of the people and typical
of the times —there are points of deadly
seriousness.
Who is the indispensable man when a
lunatic is trying to haul a machine-gun
through the front gate and his mate is in the
clouds dropping explosives on the perambu-
lator? The comfortable conceptions of every-
day life are turned upside down the
moment the riot starts and a transmutation
of values takes place which brings things
down pretty close to bedrock. Shams are
stripped of disguise, and society, if it didn’t
see the truth before, discovers in short
order how heavily it has been cumbered with
the dead weight of accumulated foolishness
- —how vast a superstructure of futility and
injustice it has reared on the foundation of
national life —how inverted all the standards
of service and reward and usefulness may
become when civic worth finds expression in
one form .only, that of the dollar. Modern
society is a complex thing, mainly because
it has loaded up with exploiters and idlers
and interest- and rent-mongers and multi-
farious commercial brigands; because it
decorates Lord Mayors with rabbit-skin,
adorns them with flunkeys and makes a joss
of the tall hat; because it gives its greatest
rewards and material comforts to successful
landsharks, and food adulterators and mono-
polists and Siiylocks ; because the parasite
prospers and its host grows thin. And on
the big parasite another parasite fastens;
on it a third, and so on after the manner
of the parable concerning fleas that are in-
finitum. Therefore is the world full of
social inequalities, and success at the game
of grab is the test of personal worth and
social value, until somethitig happens and
a colony of rats are smoked out after great
labor and uproar. Whereupon other rats
get busy on the cheese, and the hunted
animals bolt to a fresh lot of holes
and start business at a new address.
Such, too, is the confusion in stan-
dards of judgment and estimation that
the more definitely parasitic men or
women may he, .the more strenuously they
push their claims to be recognised as the salt
of the earth —to be regarded as privileged
to browse in peace and plenty and be exempt
from the obligations, trials and services of
the common herd. The old regime of France
degenerated into a state of mind from which
it was only divorced by losing its head.
Royalty was and still is in some places
convinced of its divinity. The British House
of Lords fought like a regiment against even
the nominal restriction recently placed upon
the. parasite House. The Lords claimed to be
indispensable on the ground, chiefly, that
they had condescended to be horn and had
inherited shooting-boxes and piles of cash.
* * * *
The Indispensable men to every nation are
the men who can fight and produce.
Cut these assets out and the nation
collapses. Cut the others out and the
nation would still he virile, having
all the elements essential to future wealth
and strength. It would gain something in
grace, too,, by pruning away the dead
wood. This is as true in times of peace
as when the enemy arrives to find out how
many indispensable men there are on the
premises; and it is the nation which has
allowed its public policy to lose sight of the
fundamental test of social worth and to
stimulate the growth of parasiticism and
fuinkeydom that finds itself with the greatest
volume of trouble on hand when the trial
begins. Australia would probably not have
any footmen applying for relief from service
if the call came, nor anyone packing up
Bibles to consider, nor a butcher at 17s. 6d.
a week to laugh over, on the ground that
he was indispensable. That is because
there has not been time to get them aboard.
But they are in the offing. The con-
tinent shelters an altogether excessive
number of individuals whose activities could
he dispensed with on sight to the great
advantage to the nation’s strength and pro-
ducing power; people who evolve nothing
hut the high cost of living—indispensable
adjuncts, in their way, to a system of intro-
ducing railways from shindykits and acquir-
ing telephones with a p.n.—but absolutely
parasitical if Australia lived on what she
produces and man got the value of his toil.
THE TREASON OF CONGESTED
BRICKS.
Patriotism, which is sometimes another
nnme for the right to plunder and sometimes
a passion of such an exalted sort that it
smashes across the world’s trail with the
force of high-explosive bombs or flames up
into a light so brilliant that it dazzles the
eyes of men for centuries after the flash is
gone, has an economic basis, and gets its
force and power from commonplace condi-
tions. Like any other factor it is bound by
the law of cause and effect, and can no more
be made to order at the blast of a trumpet
than prosperity can be made to spring into
existence at the snap of a statesman’s jaw.
To expect patriotism of a slave is absurd;
to expect it of a multi-millionaire is equally
futile. The would-be millionaire may run to
patriotism of the brigand sort; but it is only
the. free man who, standing on his own
native soil, and laying a rugged hand on a
hairy chest, declaring that it is good to be
alive, that we can look to for the
genuine article. The adventurer, to
whom life is a great confidence trick
and the world a thing to be taken
down, may. feel an impulse which to the noil-
critical mind looks like-enough patriotism;
but his feeling' for any country and his
loyalty even to a desperate cause is no more
than enthusiasm for the team he plays with.
The real patriotism is a deeper sentiment
with one root around a grave and. another
touching a baby’s cradle and a thousand
smaller rootlets fingering all the wholesome
and fair and honest things of life. In a
word, patriotism is an impulse that springs
from the home, and where there are no homes
there can he no patriotism.
* * * *
The miracle of old republican Rome is ex-
plained in this, the only possible, way. A
few brigands of different tribes and "races
established themselves in a Latin village. To
hold that village itself against the force of
the big and greedy surrounding nations
looked too elevated a job for the first cut-
throats who built their huts on the Palatine ;
but within seven centuries imperial Rome
had its grip upon all the world it could find.
Not only was this the case, but the huge,
early. Roman patriotic impulse was of such
magnitude that, when it smashed itself on the
rocks of bad statesmanship and luxury and
booze and admixture of Asiatic and nigger
blood and Christianity, the corrupt and tan-
gled and generally degenerate Empire tot-
tered for fully 400 years before it flopped to
extinction; and even then its influence re-
mained, with some faint echo of its old
power, right down to the days of our grand-
fathers. The key of the whole Roman posi-
tion was the citizen’s home. In those first
days by the Tiber every brigand stood. for
his own show and made his life-conditions
the best possible. Then the community of
brigands rose to the point of recognising the
rights of each individual cut-throat to live a
clean, wholesome life, and get the best for
those dependent on him. The result was
that Rome, and not the individual hut on the
hillside, became home to the citizen; and
when the Etruscan overlord or any other
person who counted his strength by the mul-
titude of his spears made a dead set at the
rising city, lie was met by such a fury of
patriotic resistance that he went away nurs-
ing his sore pride and wondering what sort
of kerosene those Romans drank to enable
them to put up a scrap like that. And the
real explanation was that the child in the
Roman cot was cleaner and better fed than
the Etruscan youngster, and that the Roman
meant to keep him so. Out of such patriotic
struggles against odds grew discipline, mili-
tary system and world-conquering possi-
bilities.
* * * *
If patriotism- -the genuine article that
might do for a continent what the spirit of
Rome did for a single city—is to he fostered
in Australia it must have its foundations in
a good, honest regard for home. Without
any sort of disparagement of a single Aus-
tralian now at the Front, or getting ready to
go there, it must be admitted that our men
have rushed into the tumult from a variety
of motives, and that to lump them all under
the general title of patriotism is to misuse
terms. Some heard the call because khaki
was the passport to the road of high adven-
ture. Many rushed the recruiting offices
because, with the story of enemy crimes fresh
in their ears, they felt it was their duty to see
that the weak and helpless had decent pro-
tection —may they strike a good blow and
return in honor to those who understand
their motives. Others —and they not in the
majority—faced the shells sternly and grimly
because they saw in the European conflagra-
tion a menace to home and all that home
meant. What it did mean is not so much
the point. One saw himself as a subject of
a far-reaching empire; another as a citizen
of a great Commonwealth yet to be. The
true note struck was that home was worth
toiling and dying for; and the home idea,
traced to its source, narrows to the actual
dwelling.
* * * *
Australian patriotism is dependent upon
Australian homes; and the reason it does
not burn with more flame is clearly to be
found in the fact that so big a proportion of
the population is homeless. You cannot
make a home out of bricks alone. The man
who merely rents a house does not neces-
sarily possess a home. In congested
areas of Sydney, where the decencies of life
are lost amid the corruption and foulness of
closely-packed hovels, it is unreasonable to
expect patriotism. What is there in the
grimy, airless confines of four miserable
walls, where a bedraggled woman herds a
swarm of squalid children, that a man should
be called to die for? Through the misery of
his home-coming he views State and Com-
monwealth and Empire, and who can blame
him if he despises the great causes which
thrill the souls of other men who feel that"
they have something to defend? Sydney City
Council, The Bulletin observes, is keen on
the erection of workmen’s dwellings—bar-
racks mostly—in the spots where the worst
of the slums have been abolished. Alderman
} LRNON struck the right gong when, speak-
ing for the worker, he demanded that the
homes should be erected on the fine park
lands rather than in the close and sordid
places proposed.. Sydney needs the parks,
needs the breathing spaces that are as lungs
to the city; but the needs of her workers are
urgent that the nation may be benefited. The
party of congestion forces certain classes of
workers to remain near the places of their
toil, yet gives them no opportunity to make
homes. The real remedy is to be found in
decentralisation, the opening of neglected
ports, and the creation of new and more
wholesome cities ; hut while the whole force
of the Sydney congestionists is exerted to
prevent the development of such a policy ,it
devolves upon those congestionists to show
how the workers can live as white men
should live without going beyond the bloated
city’s bounds. It works back to the clear
point that the overgrown city in denying
proper homes, to citizens of the Common-
wealth, and in thereby pinching the true
spirit of patriotism among those citizens,
is itself about as serious an enemy as we
could be called upon to fight.
LIGHT BREAKS UPON THE DARK-
NESS.
Dear Bulletin,—At the time of its pas-
sage eveiy student of sociology who was not
temporarily immersed in consideration of
the pending disturbance between Messrs.
Darcy and McGoorty realised that through
the War Precautions Act Australia had
stepped a long way further than she knew.
Of course the Act made the Referenda argu-
ments merely humorous, and luckily for
himself and for the democracy Prime Min-
ister Hughes detected this in time to per-
form the most difficult of all political feats
—that of diverting humor into a channel
where it becomes transformed into great
seriousness until soaked into the sands of for-'
getfulness. Hughes, who is naturally familiar
with the history surrounding the Xominides
period, was likely to see that the new Act
woukl probably become in the hands of Aus-
tralian statesmen as. effective an instrument
as a measure of similar import promulgated
fiL a'i™ the lounger and used by
him and his successors for a period of ove?
two hundred years, during which the people
ot Salomines rose to their greatest heights
of material prosperity, comfort, freedom and

Salomines by proclamation and Salomines ac cJ“
him as a Giver of Law by consent. When theTw
crisis arose about the marriage bonds he acted with
ruthless decision declaring that all men who d d
not take unto themselves wives should cease to be
beneficiaries under the wills of their mothers-in kw
and carried Ins reform into law, despite podS

In a masterly address to the people he nro<W,i

erished by reckless testamentary bequests of worn™
to men who were unknown to them, and ingeniously
argued that this was an interference with rights if
relatives and friends and likely to cause serious dis
orders Subsequently the village councils rescinded
their hostile motions, and the Court declared its
judgments ultra vires. The immediate result was
that the Councils and Courts became obsolete and
Salomines emerged from a period of thraldom to
hasty resolutions arrived at round village pumps.
This is a very striking illustration of what
may be accomplished by a democracy just
as the. Australian War Precautions Act
exemplifies the unbroken traditions of
Demos and proves how firmly rooted in the
human mind are the irrefutable principles
preserved in stress and strain through cen-
turies of time. Australia is beginning to
see now that this Act may be the medium
of great national redemption from the errors
of the past—may be expanded to embrace
the- future in its beneficent operations.
Obviously there is no reason why it should
lapse with cessation of the war. There are
going to be other wars—some day, some-
where. Australia may be interested in them.
Australia must be ready, and it would be a
monstrous betrayal of the public if anything
were allowed to occur that would interfere
with the prosecution of those wars, witli
enlistment or with public order, or would
permit any possible enemy to learn what we
were doing.
* * *
The War Precautions Act must be
made permanent. The signs of the
times cannot be disregarded. The price
of living is already causing unrest. Wharf-
lumpers have stopped earning Is. 9d. an
hour because a 21b. loaf costs 4d. Other
influential citizens have threatened to strike
because chops are two a shilling and a
roast of beef is £2. 17s. 6d. Suppose that
the army goes on strike and invades Mel-
bourne, looting bakers and butchers’ shops
and passing legs of mutton out to the popu-
lace. Under the War Precautions Act all
the meat-shops and bakeries can be closed
and order restored by not permitting them
to open their doors more than six inches
for half an hour every morning and giving
the army the sack. We are safe enough for
the present. The danger is that after this
war is over and before the next one begins
the State Governments might allow the
butcher to stay wide open all day, and thus
provoke people to rush into his emporium
and tear pieces of steak from under the
cleaver. All the State Government could
do in such a case as that would be to hit
the butcher’s assailant over the head with a
baton, which would clearly be an interference
with enlistment, as the person thus
woodened out might he prevented from
volunteering. Moreover, the censorship is in
danger of falling down dead if there is no
Act in force when the war is over and the
Administration harassed with criticism in
respect to its plans for prosecuting war and
buying horses, and its policy of acquiring
asperin and tentpoles be the subject of
disorderly criticism in the newspapers. In
addition to which, remarks may be made
about appointments and bungles and general
messing and trifling, all of which would
plunge the country into strife when the
duty of everyone is to do as he is told (vide
Hughes) and refrain from saying anything
that would interfere with everyone devoting
his energies to prosecution of the struggle
before him.
* * * *
But above every other consideration of
expediency there is this, that by a vva
Precautions Act this country can be nia
a nation instead of six nations and anot
one inside —that by this short _ cut
Commonwealth can achieve sovereign pove •
and the States be given the knock. In a
matters relating to defence the Comm
wealth has absolute power. Therefore
the broad, general ground that Australia -
preparing for defence the Act can witi
violation to the Constitution be made P
of an enduring public policy. If it does
late the Constitution the Constitution
talnly cannot violate the Act. Keeping
in view the hour of redemption ca

shattering events being prepared,
the war stops let Hughes and „ .

phrase calling for definition. But can any
democrat who supports one or both of these
fail to see the expediency of having as a
preliminary to democratic progress a full
and ample measure for the conscription of
nublic opinion and private habits? —Yours,

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Digging Dug-outs for Monkeys.
1 rom The Bulletin’s chained Zoologist;—
It is pleasing to report that, despite the war, one
of our great natural primary industries is beiro-
developed at full speed in Sydney. Hundreds of able-
bodied workmen are employed, at able-bodied wages,
m preparing a great acreage of otherwise waste land
for occupation. This vast stretch of country has
long lain in a state of nature, covered with bush,
within a mile or so from the General Post Office. As
a site for dwellings it is superb. It will make an
ideal working-mart’s suburb. It provides a splendid
view in all directions over the harbor, and it has
hitherto been reached by a ferryboat within 20
minutes from the Quay. But the roughness of the land,
and, possibly, the absence of a wayside pub, have
hitheito retarded settlement. Now, however, that the
land is being put to its full use, the need of better
communication has been patriotically recognised by
the State. A new tramway is being built through
the heart of the bush, and a new wharf is being
constructed within a few hundred yards of the present
wharf. The land is being energetically cleared for
occupation, and care is being taken to lay out the
estate in an artistic manner, by planting avenues of
native trees and constructing elaborate gardens,
stately ponds and broad and sweeping pathways. The
steepness of the land has necessitated tremendous
work in making properly-graded roadways ; but, con-
sidering the patriotic purpose for which the estate
is designed, no expenditure can be grudged for the
huge embankments, the costly fibro-cement flights of
steps, and the generous lavishness of the whole
scheme. In a few months’ time the inhabitants of
this area will feel the benefit of this splendid scheme
of closer settlement, though it is hardly likely that
any public expression will be made by the new
settlers because they will be chiefly monkeys.
Already some of the new inhabitants are installed
in the new Zoo; and, judging from their demeanor,
they are quite satisfied with the provision made for
their aesthetic sense and their physical comfort. There
is no doubt that if the inhabitants of the estate
could express their gratitude at the polling-box, the
Labor Government, which conceived the scheme,
would receive a considerable accession to its
strength; and it may be necessary in the near
future for Premier Holman to consider the advisa-
bility of giving the baboon, the elephant and the
wombat a vote, and making the iguana a J.P. or a
Lord Mayor. Certainly the elephant would welcome
a vote. He is housed in a palace which, with the
laudable desire of preventing him from feeling home-
sick, has been constructed in an Indian style of archi-
tecture. Many a navvy now constructing roads for
the elephant to stroll along, and many a worker who
built Jumbo his nobly-proportioned private bathing-
place, would be satisfied to become the tenant of such
a desirable residence; but, fortunately for that
worker, the City Council and the State have built
working-men’s homes elsewhere. These are not so
elaborate, nor have they the charming site on which
the elephant is housed, and it is quite possible that
the worker may have to pay more in tram-fares than
the elephant needs for his ferry-trip; but then an
elephant is an elephant.
Considered from a financial standpoint the ener-
getic development of the estate conclusively dis-
proves the assertion, made by evil-minded beings,
that we are not exercising the public economies
required by the British Government in these perilous
times of war. The lavish expenditure, the huge
bodies of men employed, the speeding-up of the work
so that the canary will cease his complaints about
the stuffiness of his present residence, or the llama
will stop spitting with disgust upon Randwick, all
afford a proud advertisement of our financial stabil-
ity. The State shows that it can prosecute the war
and encourage enlisting, and at the same time see
to the aesthetic ideals of the jaguar and the happi-
ness of the baboon. Consider the value of the adver-
tisement to the world and the consternation of the
Kaiser when it is learned that the same people that
produced the Anzacs can build roomy dugouts for
the monkey, construct palatial headquarters for the
elephant and dig front-line trenches for the platypus.
For what becomes of Kultur if a nation lacks a
Zoo? It is true that on the only occasion the writer
ever visited the old Zoo, the only persons he saw
there were his own and other similarly-misguided
children, who took a painful interest in the home-life
of the gorilla, a bodyguard of nursemaids with
babies too young to take an intelligent interest in
the anatomy of the anteater or even to understand
why the green monkey got its name, and a number
of Lascars who seemed to be the only persons who
were really enjoying themselves. The stranger looked
in vain for the crowd that? dotes upon the details of
the Tasmanian devil, pores over the profundity of the
camel and finds sermons in snakes. Still, if anyone
asked the Lascars and the nursemaids what the use
of a Zoo was, they would unanimously reply that it
Filled a Great Public Need.
N. S. Wales, in its noble ambition to possess the
finest menagerie in the world, gives but one more
proof of its high, ideals of civilisation. No expense
must be spared to complete the new monkey-suburb
at the earliest possible moment. The British Empire
needs every penny to preserve its entity from destruc-
tion ; and in diverting into menagerie-construction
the money that otherwise might go into the manu-
facture of munitions the State is merely showing its
wisdom. All our energies and all our cash—doubt-
less, for such a patriotic cause, Norton Griffiths
might be induced to oblige again, on the usual com-
mission—must be devoted henceforth to the para-
mount necessity of settling every available monkey on
the land.
There may be carpers who are not enthusiastic
about native bears, and are not even partial to
platvpi, who feel that this care for ill-smelling
fauna is being overdone at this crisis. Perhaps they
are right. There are times when The Bulletin
almost comes to the conclusion that all Zoos should
be closed by the Minister for Defence at 6 a.m.

The Horrors of Peace.
One of the best surviving English war
authorities insists that when the truth be-
comes known the world will be amazed to
learn with how few troops the enemy is
holding the Western Front. The Germans,
in a word, are putting up an immense bluff
with armor-plate and machine guns, and
this war on which so many honest Britishers
and others are depending for a living, may
collapse any day. It is a terrifying prospect
for any person who hates a perfect but job-
less peace. The world has forgotten how to
pay its way and learnt how to exist like a
gambler and a spendthrift since the con-
fused days before the declaration of war.
While the England of 1914 was still won-
dering if the diplomatic tower of cards was
really about to fall, one of the foremost
English financial sages upended himself on
the housetops and prophesied as follows:
If we are entangled, great financial houses, great
merchants and manufacturers will go down like nine-
pins. Capital will perish, mills will close, shops will
become empty, advertisement orders will cease, and
wages will fall.
lie forgot or ignored the fact that England
had a long stocking and almost limitless
credit. But the misery and ruin he foretold
is only held over for the time when the Vul-
ture of Peace will spread its wings and
throw the shadow of unemployment. Battle,
Murder and Sudden Death are paying wages
that are in many cases two and three times
as much as English workers ever got out
of Peace and Goodwill. When the armies
disintegrate and come home to look for
peaceful work it wall be like a second Crea-
tion—Chaos trying to evolve into Order.
The outlook for the future is made greyer
by the suspicion that Britain hasn’t had half
a chance to recoup its losses by going out to
grasp a firm hold on the deserted world-
markets of beleaguered Germany. The big
job of getting a firm hold on Germany’s
windpipe at the BTont is using up all the
power and time; meanwhile trade has to
hobble along as best it can. War is and
must be the world’s only trade for the
present; and it is so very profitable for the
people who are not actually being shot at
that they are beginning to dread the horrors
of peace.

The Sobriety of Beer.
When Charles the Second came back
from obscurity and exile to be merry
in his own peculiar fashion at White-
hall, it is recorded that all England got
drunk in his honor. Early closing wasn’t
in fashion even among the Puritans; and
the beer foamed furiously in a mighty wave
from Dover to the Tweed, where it dashed
itself to a loyal finish against the whisky
wave which had rolled down from Cape
Wrath: With slight fluctuations the drunk
then started went on seething and eddying
for more than a century, and that century
was the soberest time in English history. No
historian has hung bouquets round the fact,
but, paradoxical as it seems, the remark-
able phenomenon will stand investigation.
The tendencies of nations to go on periodical
jamborees and tear the moral machinery out
of civilisation or constitutions or churches
has often been remarked upon; but few have
taken the trouble to note that these are
always dry sprees, and that in times of great
and boisterous beershed the political horizon
is generally of a soft opal tone, merging into
delicate shades of wattle yellow and jaca-
randa blue. When Constantine hauled
down the eagle and set up the cross in its
stead, on the ground that the bird was out
of date and that a peaceful emblem was more
effective in securing a proper slaughter of
the enemy, the Church from east to west
went on a tremendous burst that shook the
Empire to its last gladiator. The East
drank Arianism —the mildest-looking theolo-
gical liquid in the world ; the West consumed
great draughts of the brew of Athanasius
—a mixed sort of punch composed mostly of
words and curses. Nobody in the whole
ruthless rage and tangle, during which hair
and whiskers were flying all over the Medi-
terranean, is known to have consumed a
single pint of beer; and yet the drunk was
of such magnitude that it took the world
centuries to recover from it and more cen-
turies to get the roar of bursting bishops
out of its ears. Similarly, in the time of
the Tudors, the grand English theological
drunk which cavorted round Parliament and
Cathedral, and frothed in the Six Articles
and the Thirty-nine Articles and the fires
of Smithfield and promiscuous hangings all
over the kingdom was a dry spree in the
most literal sense. Yet aproned bishops
and fuddled kings went on a howling burst
at the mention of a word, and danced and
prayed and wept and fought and argued as
if each were in the habit of consuming a
stable-bucket of rum before breakfast. More-
over, they changed their theological liquors
frequently, and each new tipple seemed to
set the reeling, rocking, raving commotion
going at higher speed. Martyrs died —some
of them, if our best historians of the period
have got a right grip of the facts, from no
particular principle, but simply because
they’d see themselves damned before they’d
drink out of the other fellow’s glass. So
they saw and heard themselves damned and
the orgies went on. The French Revolution
was a dry spree, except for the blood that
made the roadways sticky. The ho\vling
madness of the Crusades was equally free
from suspicion of liquor. The roaring burst
of alleged patriotism, in the midst of which
C. J. Chssar was knifed, was conducted on
water alone. Through the whole Middle
Ages the fighting drunk roared like a bull
and challenged the police to arrest him ; and
yet liquor had no part in the riot. In fact,
from first to last the pub vied with the mon-
astery as the place of quiet, and the booziest
person who clung to its mug-ringed bar was
a gentle, sober proposition compared with
the raving baron who went by cased in the
trimmings of the garden roller, and eager to
break anything, from the Ten Command-
ments to the jaw of the handiest king. The
Spanish Inquisition was dry; Luther and
John Calvin were as unsoused as the
N.S.W. Alliance; the Turks and Moors were
beerless. Yet around all these the jamboree
hiccoughed and staggered and refused to go
home till morning and asserted that it wasn’t
drunk until the non-alcoholic Saturnalia
affected whole nations and empires. View-
ing the history of this remarkable world
through the calm spectacles of history, beer
looks about the soberest agent it ever em-
ployed. The facts of the case appear to be
that when somewhat civilised man has to
bottle up too long his own distended soul he
is apt to go unhealthily mad and rage round
like several tick-bitten bulls. Beer appears
to supply the safety valve. In a quiet,
unenthusiastic way the common, moderately
sober drunk gets rid from time to time of the
accumulating insanity in his system and keeps
a steady grin turned to the world. If Aus-
tralia is out to try experiments in 100 per
cent, sobriety the overwhelming national
drunk will take place to scheduled time.
Already the anti-drink person looks a good
deal less sober than the average citizen who
buries his whiskers in alcoholic foam. Of
course, it may be interesting to shut down
the safety-valve and let the madness accumu-
late. The revolution which follows may
be picturesque and of fine historical interest;
but, if we value the quiet life, it is as well
to. give a little scientific attention to the
mollifying sobriety of beer as it affects the
national tendency to burst at intervals.

A cloud of Yankee doctors is now hanging
over Maoriland. Apparently the news got
abroad that there was a serious shortage of
medical men in M.L., so a wide-awake U.S.A.
doctor-mill started to write Notes to Premier
Massey about a consignment of its students
to man the brass plates of the men who
have gone to fight. The latest news was
that Massey had reached hurriedly for an
axe. Of course, there is a shortage of medi-
cal men, chiefly because of the system that
allowed medical students, many of whom
were in their final year, to drop their studies
and dash off to the Front. Many of them
were killed at Gallipoli, and Maoriland is
now wondering what it is going to do when it
feels ill. But whatever happens, it has decided
that it isn’t going to recognise qualifications
guaranteed by institutions that it isn’t sure
about. In the circumstances, this is a
wise decision; but it doesn’t overcome the
original difficulty. In countries where
conscription operates the indispensable
specialist isn’t allowed to hurry away
to the wrong job and leave the nation
short-handed. The specialist, like the sol-
dier, is placed where he can be of most use.
But, of course, Australasia, as a free nation,
must be very careful about conscription.
It is far too orderly and systematic.

Compare the reasonable, business-like ex-
hortations of Matiiews, M.H.R., at a Foots-
cray (Melb.) recruiting meeting, with the
optimistic soapsuds of a John Madden who
predicted in May last that the Germans
would be hopelessly beaten within two or
three months. Said Mathews
He did not believe the Allies were “bound to
win.” If he had been told two years ago that he
would ever have advocated recruiting he would have
vailed his informant a liar ; but he had made a
mistake in his original conception of the state of
affairs, and he was manly enough to admit it.
Where optimism of the Madden sort merely
serves to blunt the edge of Endeavor’s axe,
the pessimism of the man who denies that
we are bound to win serves as the grindstone
on which the axe is sharpened. It is to
resolute pessimism, not to swaggering confi-
dence, that victory will come in this war.
The doubts which have lately put new life
into Britain will be the death of Germany
in due course.

If the annual conference of the Vic. P.E.C.,
which meets in Melbourne at Easter, means
real business it had better begin sitting at
once, there being enough proposals on the
notice paper to 'provide platforms for a
dozen parties. For example, the Clerks’
Union wants membership of the P.L.C. re-
stricted to hand and brain wage-earners,
which would shut out men like Hughes or
the late Jack Arthur ; while various
branches and unions want Parliament called
together immediately, to pass a fresh lot of
Referendum Bills. But the really impres-
sive list of amendments sought comes from
Geelong, which wants, among other trifles,
to “make interest, rent and profit on wealth
illegal; prices boards for everything; the
automatic nationalisation of all real and per-
sonal property immediately upon the outbreak
of war; the financing of the present war by
means of a land tax instead of borrowing;
the immediate confiscation of all war profits
(these can be discerned by the X rays in
any fat man’s pocket) ; the annual handing
over to an organising fund of £25 per head
by Federal and £lO by State members; the
legalisation of a six-hour system.’’ Geelong
evidently doesn’t intend to stay at the tail
of the procession any longer, and proposes
to hitch itself to the Millennium by way of
a preliminary canter.

From a man who has been round the pad-
docks and listened to the arguments :■ —-
Cockydom in the Northern Vie. wheat-growing
acreages is in a bad way through the lack of hard
cash. This seems all wrong to the men on the
strippers, who estimate that the best harvest known
in the history of the district has been bagged and
sent to the seaboard. A host of sub-agents have had
the handling of the wheat, and if there is not a nice
muddle in the matter of tallies and qualities before
the final adjustments are made I am a poor prophet.
The average wheat lumper has more brawn than
brain, and how he managed to keep tally in the
rush of getting the loads trucked and away to the
ship’s side is a mystery in more ways than
one. Perhaps he is relying on the weigh-
bridge records. The quality of the grain delivered
was far from being f.a.q. (fair average quality) at
the beginning of the season, when the pinched, early
wheat started to come in, stripped in many in-
stances too soon because of the grower’s eagerness to
handle some coin. Then again there were heavy
summer showers that accounted for consignments of
wet wheat being sent away, only to be held up, and
rehandled to the sub-agents’ debit—all of which
means delay in the receipt of the longed-for wheat
certificates. Meanwhile Cockydom is practically
bankrupt, and the traders who (by the interest-6 d
grace of the wholesalers) have waited like Job on a
tombstone for over two seasons, are sitting crouched
over their ledgers and correspondence files wondering
how much longer the siege is going to last. To fur-
ther add to the joy, Cocky objects to paying
interest on his long overdue scores because be earned
nothing during the recent drought year. Promissory
notes which fell due early in February have
been dishonored by the banks, and writs are being
flourished at the end of every paddock. It is only
fair to add that the farmer in many cases is eager
to meet his liability ; there are others who will only
meet it in the Hereafter.
Every day it becomes plainer that the
anti-enemy trade sentiment wants crystallisa-
tion in a tariff that will prevent a lapse
When the war is over. At the annual meet-
ing of the Australian Association of British
Manufacturers someone moved that mem-
bers should pledge themselves not to accept
the representative of any enemy firm for at
least 10 years after the war; whereupon
another member arose and suggested that
such a pledge “would press very hard on
agents the bulk of whose income was gained
by dealing with German, and Austrian
goods.” Finally the motion was shelved by
a decision that it should be “printed and
circulated.” Now, if that is the sort of
thing that happens when war is in full
blast, what is likely to happen when the war
is over?

Gehenna Breaks Ont at the Old Address.
Ever since the Western armies disappeared
into the earth after the battle of the Marne
sections of them that were over beyond Ver-
dun, which is opposite Metz, have been nib-
bling at one another, for thereabouts is a
hinge on which swing the dispositions of
either side north and south. Possession of
Verdun was an advantage to France; to
collar it would be an advantage to the Ger-
man. And at the beginning of last week the
Hun threw himself at Verdun in large num-
bers and a violent hurry. At time of writing
he was continuing to hurl himself forward in
the same direction. Vague, contradictory
whispers about how things w y ere going were
all that drifted back from the conflict, the
only certainties being that a stupendous
battle was in progress, that the losses on both
sides had been great, that the German was
making a certain advance, and that the
French were shortening their line. The
comments by various newspapers and experts
cabled from London were, as usual,
more or less pointless and occasionally
mere drivel. However, we’re getting accus-
tomed to drivel. An example was the obser-
vation that a German seizure of Verdun
would be of no consequence. Obviously, if
this were so Joffre would not be arguing the
matter so strenuously, nor would the German
be making so tremendous an onslaught. Pend-
ing reliable information about the position,
the main thing sticking out of the gloom is
the warning afforded by this attack against
acceptance of the comforting yarns about the
enemy being somewhere near the end of his
tether. The war is going to be decided acre it
started, and if the campaign of last summer
revealed that the enemy line was hard to
break, the battles of Verdun and recent
happenings around Ypres show that the
enemy is still prepared to attack with a
heavy punch. It may be that his latest
effort is one of despair, or signalises an in-
tention to put everything to the hazard of
victory. The evidence of this is a
long way from being conclusive. What
has happened and is still happening
can more safely be taken as another indica-
tion of what has been clear all along—that
what the Allies need most is every man that
can be mustered, and what can give them
the least assistance is guesswork about the
other fellow. In the interval of waiting for
the result of the battle at Verdun
two big steamers have been lost close to
Dover; the interchange of Notes has reached
a stage in which ink and paper and speeches
and general remarks are mixed in one huge,
incomprehensible mass; and the Turk is
tightening up his belt and ‘wondering what
is going to be his next trouble.
“Marama” : It is just about time some of
the “authorities” woke up to the fact that
good Commonwealth gold is quietly sneaking
away to the Dutch possessions in the East.
Each vessel that steams away to the North
carries a cargo of sovereigns hidden about
the clothes of various persons, most of them
Australians, acting as agents for the wily
Chinese barterers of Singapore and Batavia.
Couldn’t the Customs people cease worrying
about old ladies’ kimonos, and apply a search-
light to the pockets of these low-down pil-
ferers of our greatest asset?
Thomas Edwin Brown, an orchardist of
St. Ives (Sydney), who silently moved off
to Yankeeland the other day, had a crowded
year or two of life and law not long ago.
In 1913, after Brown had been involved in
innumerable petty legal squabbles, a police
sergeant and constable went to his place
armed with a warrant of arrest. “All right,”
said Brown, and went inside to get his coat,
the Hops following. But Brown, in addi-
tion to his coat, got a revolver, and when
he had ended his preparations the
sergeant was shot dead and Brown was
charged with murder. At the first trial the
jury disagreed. At the next Brown was found
guilty and sentenced to death —whereupon he
appealed to the High Court, which quashed
the proceedings and ordered a new trial.
This took place before Cullen, C.J., and
accused was acquitted, mainly because of
doubt whether the shooting was accidental
or deliberate. As he left the dock he was
arrested on a charge of lunacy, and more
long-drawn-out hearings and arguments and
appeals supervened, the final stage being that
Brown was held to be a victim of paranoia,
which took the form of a fixed delusion that
a vast conspiracy existed to prevent him
proceeding at common law to recover 2h\.
or something. Thereupon after about two
years’ wrangling with statutes and specialists
and police Brown faded off the landscape
with a vast accumulation of documents. He
is about 50. of dark and sombre mien, and
looks as if he had signed a pledge in early
youth never to laugh again. Tp hear Brown
giving evidence on his own behalf was to
get a fairly useful lesson in the elasticity
of the word “paranoia.” He appeared abso-
lutely normal, which seems, after all, to be
one of the unfailing signs of the paranoic.

Metaphysicians and devotees to New
Thought will have an opportunity to dis-
cover what New Thought looks like on the
stage at the Little Theatre on March 2
when Frederick Ward is to produce “The
Victory,” by .Julia Seton, M.D., of N’York
where the New Thought conies from. The
dramatist, who is now in Australia on a
lecturing tour, is the founder and principle
lecturer of the Church of the New Civilisa-
tion. “The Victory” is a play with a re-
ligious purpose, and is said to "stand in the
same relation to the New Thought faith
as “The Sign of the Cross” and “The Passing
of the Third Floor Back” does to the estab-
lished Church: but the producer holds out
no _ hope that there are any hungry lions
in it. The scene has been localised in Aus-
tralia. and one of the acts represents an
open-air meeting in the Domain. Herbert
Ford, Eily Malyon and Agnes Keogh will
appear in the production. This play ought
to settle the questions whether the New
Thought is only the Old Thought with an
Amurrican accent and whether the New
Model Civilisation is a self-starter.

The beautifying of Branscombe’s Garden
Theatre, which is over the shoulder of Wil-
liam-street. Sydney, has become an obsession
with the management, and as much atten-
tion is paid to the appearance of the open-
air place of entertainment as to the prepara-
tion of the evening programme. The versa-
tile Green Dandies, with quick changes of
specialties are holding their end of the busi-
ness well.
** * *
During the week one of the biggest of the
series of carnivals which has been conducted
at the White City (Sydney) this season drew
an unusual crowd even for a White City
carnival night. The costume competition sys-
tems are the magnet of the big draw.
* * * *
Allan Wilkie’s interesting Hamlet will
muse, meditate and make things hum at
Melb. Princess’s until Friday night, when
the devastated Court of Denmark will
disappear from view and the Ghost will lapse
into silence as before. The performance,
which was good on the first night, has im-
proved in sundry details during its two
weeks’ run. The aforesaid Ghost, for in-
stance, was a trifle “fluffy” in his lines when
he first opened his misty jaws, and the voice
of the prompter could be heard in the gloom
telling the hesitating spook what next to say.
But now he is a practised public speaker
“FACT’RY ’ANDS."
They are a weird collection in that factory
of Spats’,
And Dyson says they’re Sydney girls, to
which we answer “Rats!”
Such girls may wield the paste-brush in a
village down in Vic.,
But Sydney fact’ry ’ands possess that price-
less thing called “chick.”
Some ladies paste the pages in the building
where we write ;
And they are smart in their attire and pleas-
ing to the sight.
In Dyson’s eyes the girls are but a target
for his wit;
Of sympathy and kindliness he shows them
not a bit.
Yet in that mob of foolish freaks there
surely must have been
One simple, sweet and loving girl like Den-
nis’s “Doreen” !
They are a weird assortment in that factory
of Spats’,
With costumes that are awful and an out-
size in their hats.
You’ll see them at the Palace, as they bicker
and they flirt.
And sling repartee at the boss, who likes a
bit of skirt.
You’ll see them on the ferry-boat and see
them out on strike;
But never will you notice one that you will
really like.
For Sarah is a smooger with a very fickle
heart,
Though Cilly wears “real poodle,” still you
couldn’t call her smart;
And in this wild menagerie no lady can be
seen
That looks at all like Dennis’s delectable
“Doreen.”
The men that earn a living in that factory
of Spats’
Are really much more life-like than that mob
of female cats.
There’s “Feathers” who is cynical and to
the ladies rude;
We’ve tried to be like him, and so approve
his attitude.
And Benno is the sort of bloke that we all
recognise:
A Don Juan in his own mind, a fool in
women’s eyes.
Billy the Boy, the printer’s devil; yes, we
know that chap ;
For in our office we have always one of him
on tap.
But “Fuzzy” seems to fade away from what
he might have been ;
And so we miss a simple tart like Dennis’s
“Doreen.”
FIRST NIGHT OF “ FACT'RY ’ANDS.”
Edward Dyson watches his “ Fact’ry ’Ands”
at work in the Sydney Palace Theatre.
No plot is really needed in this factory of
Spats’;
The audience is happy with the chatter of
the cats.
But suddenly across the fun there sounds a
pistol shot.
A melodrama murder-case is sandwiched in
the plot.
The hero shields the heroine —at intervals he
wooes her,
The rude detective smokes cigars and hur-
riedly pursues her.
But what this melodrama has to do in such a
play _
Is what this simple critic bloke is unprepared
to say.
There is no need for hero, ’tec, or weepful
herbine:
What’s needed most, it seems to us, is Den-
nis’s “Doreen.”
For Dyson’s wit and humor in this factory of
Spats’
This critic thanks him humbly, and he offers
his congrats.
But pistol shots, detectives and the heroine’s
disgrace
In this neat chronicle of tarts are rather out
of place.
They interrupt the fish-hook joke—of course
he put in that—
And they beguile the audience from poor
Cilly’s noble hat.
But still the trouble with this farce is that
the author seeks
To captivate the audience with a painful lot
of freaks.
And our advice is merely this, to shoot that
heroine,
And introduce some simple maid like Dennis’s
“Doreen.”

j n j t The bill for Saturday is “Twelfth
Night,” the least unfamiliar to modern Mel-
bourne of all Shakspeare’s comedies. This
revival will be the fourth —not to mention
Alexander Watson’s recital —within 14 years.
Wilkie is expected to beat the local record
as Malvolio, and Miss Hunter Watts has
every qualification for making Viola a nice
sentimental lamb in boy’s clothing. There
is not much of the mere bloke about Viola’s
costume, anyhow. She usually looks like a
glorified Pirate of Penzance at a fancy-dress
ball. .....

“It Pays to Advertise” is still mak-
ing good at the Royal. Two more
weeks are talked of, so there's time
for any enterprising manufacturer of toilet
fat to get a “Number 13 Skin Soap. Unlucky
for Pimples,” fairly on the market ere the
Yankee comedy has ceased to wash. For
five nights recently Beatrice Holloway
figured as typewriter to the King of
Soap, but Myrtle Tanneliill is now back in
that dollar-making job. A lovely easy job
is the typewriter’s—all dollars and kisses.
The thought of it would drive the most
flapperlike girl into business.
