The panto, has already passed away from
Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), and the Royal
Comics are about to resume operations at
the Williamson house. On Saturday they
revive “Gipsy Love” for just a week only.
After which comes “The Belle of New
York.”
The war-lecture season at Melbourne
Auditorium will be closed for another week.
Ashmead Bartlett has gone, leaving behind
him the impression of one who talked not
unwisely but too much, who was full of
matter but sadly lacking in the lecturer’s
art of personal appeal to his audience,
whose manner was dry and distant where it
should have been juicy and familiar, and
who was most casually fluent when he should
have been slowly emphatic. Bartlett has
the matter, but no idea of how to deliver it.
lie passes consignments from his know-
ledge-box as though he were unloading
bricks. The next war witness to give evi-
dence at the Audi, will be Fred Coleman,
lie talks of how he was “With the Allies in
France and Flanders,” and shows 300 pic-
tures of the Great. Mysteripus, Western
Front. Coleman starts on March 11.
“HAMLET,” AT MELBOURNE
PRINCESS'S.
Ophelia’s Dad.
Hamlet (Allan
Wilkie).
“The Waybacks” continue to be a matter
for laughter at Melbourne King’s. Dad
payback remains in charge of the proceed-
ings, giving advice or chiding his unruly
family in a voice that sounds like “interrup-
tion” at a public meeting. The broken
comedy is most humorous when it is satiri-
cal, as in Dad’s deputation to the Minister
of Public Works, or the controversy between
the two Balmain ladies over their back
fences; and weakest when the junior charac-
ters are pushing one another about. Most
of these small people are scant of human in-
terest and are otherwise unworthy of sym-
pathy or attention. As for the frequent in-
terloper that wears a monocle and a tartan
hatband and expresses his dense ignorance
of agriculture in some pitiable japes, he is
an apology for a human being.
All of which being said the fact re-
mains that the King’s was crowded on the
second Saturday night of “The Waybacks.”
Ihe audience seemed to think, moreover,
that the show was pretty much what the
highly-colored posters had led it to expect.
t
Saturday’s muster at Melbourne Bijou in-
cluded George Wheeler, an eccentric musi-
cian whose most arresting item is the perpe-
tratlon of popular tunes on dead marines.
Alphonso and King, two fresh programmers,
show themselves to be well informed on the
subject of trick-biking; but in the matter of
cross-gagging Desmond and Dalton are
hawkers of ancient history. Rose Lee Ivy,
clever and enlivening in songs, has come to
the help of the old brigade. Lending some
support is Leonard Nelson, the retailer of
machine-made melodies.

Ihe Follies, plus the Belgian Concert
aud two or three other items, are jus-
tifying their incidental flags and fixings at
the Melbourne Tivoli. Business is brisk
and the programme is the same disordered
onslaught of songs and trivialities as before,
home _of the trivialities break through the
first, lines of inanity at salient points, but a
hollies performance has one great advan-
tage over an ordered sequence of “turns.”
There are no encore numbers in it and no
pauses. Nobody reappears before a cheer-
less front-cloth to say “Ladies and gentle-
men, with your kind permission I will
now give you a bit more of myself.” when
nearly everybody in the audience wishes the
performer was dead.

Signaller Tom Skeyhill’s honest yarn of
the Australians at Anzac is to be repeated
at the Athenaeum Hall. Thomas will be there
again next Saturday and subsequent nights.
Malini, professor of magic, threatens to be
amazing for a week at the New Assembly
Hall, Collins-street (Melb.), commencing
next Saturday (4th). lie practises his
black art without apparatus.
This all-for-the-Empire-business may be
very well in theory when the importer
has a penny flag in his hand, but in practice
—well, during 1914 the Australian importer
introduced 42 million pounds of tea to this
country, as against 45 millions in 1915. Of
the 1914 importation, about nine millions
came from China and Java (non-British)
and 33 millions from British territory. But
in 1915 the tally was : China and Java, 12
millions; British territory, 33 millions again.
So in the year when there was the loudest
howl for the protection and extension of
British trade, importations from British
lands remained stationary while the foreign
product was favored to the extent of three
million pounds over the 1914 purchases.
There is a remedy, but it has nothing to do
with waving penny flags and talking in a
large, inflated, splendid, insincere way of
Australia's duty to the Empire.
A guarded word from behind the wheat-
stacks :—-
The cockies in the rich districts beyond Bendigo
were expected to contribute thousands of bags of
wheat to the Sick and Wounded Soldiers’ Fund; but
so far only a score or so of bags have been for-
warded from the sky-high stacks. Those cockies
who have been moved by a generous impulse are
the smaller men. The big growers have given little
in the shape of kin, much less in harvest-trove. As
an example to them, some struggling settlers on
Nestdown Estate, further north, have sent along
their 16 bags (over a ton) without a murmur. But
these are the glorious exceptions.
Tassy’s Marine Board had before it the
other day a proposal to abolish the signal
station at Mount Nelson on the ground that
ships carry wireless nowadays, and that,
anyway, vessels are reported from other
places before they are spotted from Mount
Nelson. As the abolition of the station would
have been a break with ancient history the
proposal was dropped. Governor Macquarie
planted the look-out on Mount Nelson when
he reached Hobart in 1811, naming it after
the Lady Nelson, the craft which carried
him from Sydney. Ever since there has been
a signalman with an eye cocked for vessels
coming into the Derwent. On a clear day
a ship’s flags can be made out 18 miles
away, and if a vessel is a well-known trader
they know all about her through the tele-
scope when she is 28 miles off or thereabouts.
AT POVERTY POINT.
C. J. Z. Woinarski writes :
Contributors to The Bulletin concerning the late
Geo. Musgrove cast an unwarrantable slur on his
memory in reference to the “Tambour Major” company
and its profits. It has been suggested that Musgrove
acted shabbily or worse to his uncle, W. S. Lyster,
in appropriating its profits. Not a penny of Lyster’s
money was in that venture. The money which
brought out the co. from England was furnished to
Geo. Musgrove by an Englishman named Ayers in
these circumstances. G.M. was returning from Maori-
land in May, 1880, with manager Lyster’s English
opera co., and on board the ship was Ayers (who
was a very wealthy man). He and Musgrove
became very friendly, and when George mentioned
certain plans he had as to his future and his lack
of capital Ayers generously offered £2OOO, with
the promise of another £lOOO if necessary, as a
loan. On arriving at Melbourne Musgrove
went straight to England and engaged the “Tambour
Major” co. with the money. Previous to his departure
it was arranged by Ayers that only if the venture
made good was the money to be returned, but if
it failed the lot was to be accepted as an absolute
gift by him to Musgrove. Lyster died before his
nephew returned to Melbourne from England. The
point I want to emphasise is that not a penny of
Lyster’s money was in the successful “Tambour
Major” venture.
“Pin” : Re late George Musgrove. There
are _ old heads that remember “Muzzy” in
1861-70 as a law clerk to old Win. Atten-
borough, erstwhile of Collins-street. The
office where “Muzzy” then ate his sand-
wiches when not writing blisters adjoined
the old Criterion pub, on the site of the
present Commercial Bank. It was from
Attenborough’s that G.M. went to Uncle
Lyster at the old Opera House to help keep
up the business end of things. By the way,
Melbourne’s recent reminder of the once-
important George has set me moralising on
the way of all worlds —theatrical or other-
wise. A picture of his funeral was filmed
at one of the movies. The coffin was showm
being carried out from a small cottage, or
villa, thence to a two-horse hearse. Two
black coaches suffice for the mourners, and
the rest of the “cortege” consists of two
motor-cars and a hansom cab. Fifteen years
ago the funeral of poor “Muzzy” would have
made an imposing show on the film screen.
This picture looked like a satire.

production of “The Country Girl” and popu-
larised “My Little Grey Home in the West,”
is now a widow. Her husband was comman-
der of HALS. Arethusa, lost the other day
by striking a mine off the British coast.
R. G. Ivnowdes, of music-hall fame, has a
few things to say about Australia in bis
book of reminiscences, “A Modern Colum-
bus.” “Nearly everyone likes the Australians,
for they are a most hospitable people, with
kindly feelings for all peoples in all parts.”
Knowles claims to have brought down the
house at Sydney Palace by asking, on his
first appearance, “If the man is in the house
who dug the harbor, will he please stand
up?” The “howl of delight” which greeted
this brilliant effort “came, I imagine, on
account of everyone over-praising Port
Jackson to such an extent that they
were pleased wdien someone came along
with courage enough to guy a little
on so serious a subject.” George Reid
is a favorite subject wdth Knowles, whose
stories never lose anything in the telling.
George, it seems, “established his reputation
in a single night.” Someone in “a great
throng” threw an egg which smashed on his
shirt-front; on which he solemnly thanked
God that “the Great Auk is dead.” On the
other hand, a joke of dubious taste, which
Reid generally gets the credit of, is told here
as having been scored against him. “You
ought to be dead,” said the man ; and Reid
obligingly replied: “Suppose I were dead,
what would happen then?” Whereupon
somebody snapped that the fat would be m
the fire. Knowles says he was in the audience
when that happened ! “There was no speech ;
I heard nothing of Reid—neither logic, ora-
tory, wit, humor, eloquence nor repartee.
Reid laughingly quitted, and so did the audi-
ence, howling with delight.”
“II.F.”: Your Melbourne report of my
Wayback play states: “Dad merely reflects
the crude and primitive nature of Dad Rudd ;
all Rudd’s fondness for sudden violence and
whiskers is carefully repeated.” Excepting
the whiskers item—-common to most bushies
—this statement is contrary to fact. Old
Rudd, a clearly-defined character, is an
opinionated, bull-headed and humorless old
settler of the Darling Downs. Dads Way-
back is an easy-going, humorous philosopher,
a native of the Hawkesbury, and incidentally
my neighbor for many years. Also date of
publication shows that The Waybacks came
to town years before Steel Rudd brought his
family to the metropolis. And it was as
bushies in town that The Waybacks struck
oil.
“Hamer”: 11. W. Varna, who figured as
petitioner in a Melbourne Divorce Court a
week or so back, came out to the William-
son firm to produce “The Darling of the
Gods”; and Sid Smith, an Australian who
had made a reputation as producer with the
Firm, refusing to play second fiddle, went
out. Later Varna stage-managed for the
Knight-Jeffries co. While Julius was in hos-
pital, and Plimmer Marcus Superbing—an
occasion when there was considerable
shuffling of parts—Varna played Nero. One
newspaper carelessly called the part “Zero.”
Hall Caine has manufactured a war
drama, “The Prime Minister,” which has
been censored in London. U.S.A. is to get
the benefit of the doubt.
Victoria has learned its lesson from last
year’s bitterly disappointing experience with
serum imported for the treatment of menin-
gitis. Lieutenant-Colonel George Cuscaden,
P.M.O. for the State, has published the fact
that supplies of serum, for coping with a
possible outbreak this autumn, are now
being made at the Veterinary College (Mel-
bourne University), and large reserves
thereof are being piled up against the day
of infection. The disease is being fur-
ther discouraged by more liberal tenting
arrangements for the men in militarv camps,
six to eight soldiers per covering being the
complement, instead of 10 as formerly. With
a few other precautions, such as arrange-
ments for early diagnosis and adequate
segregation, the soldiers should be able to
have a fairly healthy wet-season. It is the
civilian who will suffer, as before, with his
haphazard treatment by clashing authorities,
squabbling hospital-managements and indif-
ferent municipal bumbles.
The recruiting district of Heidelberg, part
of which is quite close to Melbourne, but
which extends into the bushy hills beyond
Diamond Creek on the north, contains about
40 square miles ; so the sergeant has to keep
at least one good horse. The other day the
local shire-council received a letter suggest-
ing that the ratepayers might patriotically
feed the animal. While the council
was considering the matter an old
Heidelberg dweller, Malcolm Macleod,
of Dalvey, arose and settled the busi-
ness by volunteering for active horse-
service during the period of the war.
Mac’s sporting offer was to provide
transport and to drive the sergeant any-
whither at any hour of day or night.
With the light of battle in its eye Sydney
D.T. threw the last bottle out of its new
skyscraper which occupies the site from
which a pub was torn and on February 17
roared that:—
The obvious course for Minister for Defence Pearce
to take.... if the State Government will not limit
the hours for consumption of liquor is to step boldly
in under the ample powers he possesses.
Whereupon Pearce stepped boldly in, and
the D.T. arose to propose his health with: —
At last! After irreparable and incalculable dam-
age has been done, Mr. Pearce has invoked his powers.
But on February 25 it appeared to the D.T.
that it was time to turn the usual somer-
sault :
The rights of the States to control their domestic
affairs must be respected, and it would be a dan-
gerous precedent if the Federal Government, by a
mere proclamation, insisted upon interfering with
them. The same power which by proclamation can
limit the hours of trading can extend them, and a
Minister on his own will, if left with uncontrolled
authority, might in this way make violent inroads
on the functions of the States.
Having got this off, the agitated organ of
Freetrade reckoned it might as well add to
the confusion by remarking a little further
down that: —
It is pretty plain that the Minister for Defence
will have to maintain a sufficient restriction on the
bar trade of hotels during the war unless the State
Government does its obvious duty.
So it boils down to this: that Pearce did the
right thing; that what he did was dangerous
and an “inroad” on the functions of the
State ; that he must go on being dangerous
and continue his inroads unless someone else
does what Pearce and the D.T. consider to
be the correct thing. It seems somewhat
mixed.

Tell him straight he was put in the pound,
And it won’t help to blaspheme and splutter,

NOT HER FAULT.
“Darling, these onions have a horrible smell.”
“They can't have. I sprayed them with Eau-de-Cologne before I cooled them.”
“Tito” : You don’t often hear a growl from
a barman, so here goes. I was recently
driven by force of circumstances to snatch
a job or position as bar-tender in one of
Melbourne’s leading hotels, and after putting
in three months was asked to retire on
account of my alleged appetite for imported
stout. During those three months I worked
GO hours per week for the tremendous weekly
remuneration of 355., or 7d. per hour. In
return for this imperial income I was ex-
pected to be honest, sober, smart, obedient,
muscular (to deal with refractory strange
drunks), a diplomatist (to pacify without
offence good customers on the spree), and
to address every sodden bounder that hap-
pened along as “Sir.” The manager took
such a fatherly interest in me and my
fellow drink-agents that he couldn’t let us
out of his sight, even on Sundays. On that
alleged day of rest we came in at 8 a.m. to
polish up numerous whisky bottles which all
contained the same fluid under different
aliases. To make sure that we wouldn’t go
away without seeing him he didn’t pay us
our week’s money till 10 a.m. on the day
of resurrection. It is time the men who
supply sore heads at an expensive rate drank
up and demanded shorter hours and a decent
Avage. It is a disgrace that men who
work till 11.30 six nights out of the seven
should be made to rise at 6 a.m. on Sunday
so that they may walk into town and arrive
there at 8 to put a gorgeous boot-polish on
the bottle with the blue ruin in it.

“J.P.”: An inventh'e farmer at Palmer-
ston North (M.L.) seems to have struck
something big in the shape of an electric
gun for hurling gigantic shells, containing a
high explosive, incredible distances. Six
months ago he sent complete models, plans
and specifications of this remarkable weapon
to the Inventions Board of the British Admi-
ralty. Contrary to its usual custom, the
Board sprang at the device, examined it
thoroughly and hurried it to the munition
branch for experiment. The inventor \A r as
recently advised that the tests were pro-
ceeding, that so far they had been satis-
factory, and that practically the only diffi-
culty to be overcome Avas the excessive
length of barrel required to ensure efficiency.
If the idea can be Avorked, a new horror
Avill be added to war, for dynamite and
dead cats can be safely hurled out of the
weapon.

“Carfax” salutes the book and tells the
story of a neglected opportunity :
That yarn published in Australian papers a week
ago about the mechanical device perfected by Ger-
man scientists for electrically warming soldiers in
the trenches during winter was another illustration
of the scurvy treatment received by men of inventive
genius in the Commonwealth. The device is really
the invention of a man whose copy has frequently
been published in The Bulletin. Of a strongly
philosophic temperament he had, long before the war
broke out, foreseen the collision and forecasted
the revolution that would take place in the
conduct of campaigns by land and sea. The
seizure of Bosnia and Herzegovina by Austria
about five years ago was so obviously a
trial gallop that he immediately wrote to
the War Office, offering to place at its disposal the
plans and specifications he had drawn up for the
equipment of soldiers necessary to meet modern
methods of attack and defence. At great expense
he had samples prepared, and these were sent to
London, but in spite of repeated letters inquiring
what was going to be done he got no reply. This,
with the announcement of the Germans having in-
A'-ented a portable warmer, points strongly to sus-
picion that the goods were forwarded by the post

the Australian is advised by counsel to proceed

“Vin”: I’m waiting and hoping for the
educational depts. to officially scalp the time-
waster who visits schools and gives a lecture
or operates a gramophone or magic
lantern at 3d. admission. The show is always
20 years behind the times; yet education
departments allow it while preaching
thrift to the kiddies. Some of the school-
showmen rake in five or six notes a week by
working their miserable fakes. I’m so im-
pressed with the possibilities that I’m think-
ing of throwing up teaching and taking
down the younger generation by machinery.
It pays better than working.

“Chinky” : The wet-farm areas in Northern
victoria are far ahead of the rich wheat-
farming latitudes in the matter of recruiting.
The irrigation settlement of Ballendella,
with its population of 30 families, has con-
tributed about 40 fighters to ' Australia’s
ranks, while cockydom nearby has hardly
contributed a man. There is, by the way,
a m ‘* l ’k ec J ! difference in the temperaments of
we f ‘‘dry” farming communities.
While the wives of the fighting settlers
tackle the work on their blocks, including
irrigating, cutting and stacking lucerne, and
generally work like good Australians to be
independent of the so-called “charity” of local
committees, the better-off cockies whose few
absent sons have blocks of the wheat-growing
closer settlement estates, have been beg-
ging for help to take the crops off. This
fatty degeneration of the liver that afflicts
the well-to-do is a terrible disease.
The man who boils down the cables got
to understandable, everyday hospital lan-
guage last week when he announced that
"the entire Servian army is now out of
danger.” Next caller will be told that it is
as well as can be expected, or is sitting up
in Corfu and taking nourishment.
* * * *
From the abode of Costs:—
Some divorce cases have lately illuminated the
drab business of life in Melbourne. In one the
husband, immediately after the honeymoon, con-
ceived the idea that his wife was a mere slave,
and whenever he was at home desired her to bog
into certain dressmaking work, the proceeds of which
he afterwards commandeered. When he went out he
locked the lady in the bedroom, which was lit in
the daytime by a skylight and at night by the
moon. As he was out a good deal and often till
midnight, and as he mostly omitted to leave his bet-
ter half any food the proceedings grew monotonous
and she left for a more crowded environment.
Nominally she had deserted her husband, but the
Law considered that he should have been deserted
earlier in the campaign and severed the bonds.
Another narrative, told by the petitioning wife,
astonished even the seasoned Justice Hood. it
concerned a moderately-rich English woman whose
husband occupied himself for 16 years in the Cold
land in living on her money. Then he engaged a
domestic servant and insisted that she should sleep
in their room. Petitioner acquiesced, as she did to his
nibs’s departure for Australia with the damsel. On
following them later the wife naturally found the
pair living together, but she took up her abode on
the premises quite ingenuously, although she didn't
approve of her husband preferring to sleep on the
verandah, where it happened the other lady slept.
It was a tale of almost incredible simplicity, but
his Honor dismissed his preliminary suspicions of
connivance and handed out a decree.
After listening for over two days to the
evidence in the original suit, when hearing
the appeal in the Norton v. Norton divorce
case, the N.S.W. Full Court took a long
breath (the rules unfortunately prevent a
Court taking a long sleever). The costs of
the first hearing are said to have reached
£2OOO, and, of course, Costs are still going
strong. While Justices Pring, Sly and Fer-
guson represented the Court’s fulness over
that case, Justices Street, Gordon and Har-
vey were wrestling with an appeal m
another case, wherein, also, the costs are
alleged to have reached the vicinity of
£2OOO before the appeal began. In that case
—Everingham v. the Municipality of Pen-
rith—the printed record of evidence reached
towards 300 pages. The land in dispute is
worth, at the outside, £l5O.
PARNASSUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The return railway fare, Christchurch to Parnassus,
is one guinea.
The ranges called Parnassus
Are leagues from Christchurch town.
There winds sing in the grasses
On hillsides golden-brown—
There cornfields glimmer yellow
And skies are mostly blue,
And Pegasus, old fellow,
Has hay enough to chew.
The old post-road to Waiau
Is steep; it never winds
By punga, pine or ngaio,
Nor sheltered hollow finds.
But over high Parnassus,
A Roman way, it goes
Where storm-kings clink their glasses
And drink to winter’s snows.
We took that road one morning,
’Twas Pegasus we drove,
And autumn’s rich adorning
Was colder than our love,
Along the high, blue ridges
We dreamed till sunset came—•
The mountain-tops were bridges
To cross a world of flame.
’Twas splendid on Parnassus—
We saw the wild hares flee—
And young gods seemed to pass us
With glances turned to see
Who came, with none to guide them,
By pathways of the skies,
With youth and love beside them
And laughter in their eyes.
Down dim and misty valleys
Good Pegasus made haste
Where twilight ever dallies
With shadows slim and chaste,
And even when the town lights
Were twinkling close at hand
We saw the blue and brown lights
That lit our golden land.
“Who’s going to Parnassus!’’
The guard cries, facing down—•
With every wind that passes
We smell the hillsides brown.
Two guineas, bright and yellow,
Two tickets, royal blue—
The engine’s deep-toned bellow—
“ Hold on, we’re coming, too!”
MAORILAND.

“Lou”: A returned soldier ran his fore-
finger across his throat about this: “We
were rusliin’ up a slope under a hot fire. On
me off-side was one of them Indian Silks.
All of a sudden a piece of shrapnel nearly
cut off one of his food hooks at the wrist.
Without stoppin' he screwed the paw off and
threw it away.” Well, he had no further
use for it.

About the time war was declared a Ger-
man tramp, loaded with some thousands of
pounds’ worth of Hun firebricks for the Pro-
prietary Steel Works at Newcastle, poked
its nose in at Albany, but someone passed
the word along promptly, and the stranger
bolted for Java. As there was danger of
delay in starting the works, and as it was
impossible to procure a fresh supply from
abroad (where all good things come from)
manager Delprat proceeded to test an
Australian fire-clay deposit, and with
such success that a far better brick was
manufactured in Australia. When, about
a year later, the German consignment was

Trade Union Ghost”: I wouldn’t care to
lie dead in old St. David’s Cemetery, at
Hobart (Tas.), where the monument of
Governor Collins and many another old-time
worthy defies the weather with more or less
success. The whole place is in a scandal-
ous condition, with gaping vaults and
di unken tombstones reeling in the moon-
light. According to the accounts, the bone
settlement paid its miserable way in
the revenue including a ' sum
ot £22 _ 2s. for rent of part of it
as a timberyard and an old cottage
once used by the caretaker. The
expenditure was only £8 7s. Id. If the
balance of £l3 14s. lid. isn’t spent on im-
proving the property, I am going to discon-
tinue haunting there.

An ancient mariner was fined £lO at
Parramatta (N.S.W.) last week for feloni-
ously acquiring cash from a Chow who
wanted opium. The old shellback told the
Chow he knew where to secure some of the
sleepy stuff and was advanced £9 10s. to
procure it, but as the goods were not deliv-
ered the Chinaman summoned the sailor
to court on a charge of stealing his money.
Opium-buying, like betting, is illegal, and
the general impression is that a citizen can-
not recover a bet at law. Seeing that the
Chinaman had been trying to evade the law
by conspiring with the defendant to commit
aa illegal act, the Court’s decision to fine
the latter £lO is a curious one. If the Law
wants to prevent Chinamen from buying
opium one of the best ways not to do it is
to offer protection to would-be purchasers.
Briefly the Law undertakes by this case to
say to the opium smoker: “Arrange with
who you like to buy the poppy drug. If
he delivers it, all li; if not we finem tenner.”
Bishop Long, of Bathurst (N.S.W.),
records that out of 15 men preparing for
ordination 11 have gone to the Front, and
two others are staying only because they
tailed to pass the medical tests. Further, only
one stipendiary reader remains in the diocese,
and he has returned from Gallipoli incapaci-
tated by wounds. “It is impossible,” sighs
Bathurst, “to fill the vacancies for curates.”
Who wants curates, anyhow? These are
the days of warriors.

A very wild man in pyjamas did a flame-
dance round his country home last week. He
had good reason for his excitement, and he
explained his troubles between leaps. Deep
in the heart of the furnace was a roll of 175
£1 banknotes, and as the Treasury hasn’t
yet started its asbestos issue, money was
burning as well as the- piano and the por-
trait of mother before she was married.
Most Australian notes look like dishrags and
deserve to be fired ; but it is one of the jobs
the private citizen can’t afford to do on
his own.

“True Bill”: A Government (address
strictly censored) recently informed a
General (name on no account to be pub-
lished) that there were 30 decorations for
distributions among his officers who had dis-
tinguished themselves. Would he send on his
recommendations? In due time 30 officers
sported ribbons, but to the astonishment of
the General and his chief of staff not one of
the men he had recommended received recog-
nition. Discreet inquiry was made, and it
was found that by the mistake of an officer
at headquarters a list of 30 officers who were
to be inoculated against hay-fever or some-
thing got into the wrong envelope ; also, that
30 heroes had been dragged off to hospital
and punctured instead of being decorated.
Whereupon the O.C, turned himself into a
Catherine-wheel and uttered wild, dishevelled
language.
* * * *
A Sydney boatbuilder who knew him
lately supplied a suitable epitaph for the late ,
Emil Bomholtz, who was drowned with two
friends while trying to steeplechase over the
bar at Laurieton (N.S.W.) in a motor-
boat. “An absolutely fearless man” was
the boatbuilder’s farewell, and he told how
Bomholtz once travelled all the way to Syd-
ney from the Richmond Biver in an IS-foot
open boat, with engine and sail. In the
same week that Bomholtz was drowned he
and another friend, while out in the lost
motor-boat, were smashed up by a sea and
barely rescued from a saturated grave. Yet
he went out in bad weather a few days
later. Fearlessness may be a commendable
quality, but it would be improved if those
who are addicted to it made a point of always
performing alone, and not involving others
in their dare-devil acts.

A CHOICE ASSORTMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All the air was, warm with laughter,
All the sun was bright with joy;
Time himself that pleasant after-
Noon became a careless boy.
(Sort of boy that likes to tarry
Having telegrams to carry.)
All the world was like a feather
Sailing down a summer wind ;
Both your eyes made happy weather,
And your lips were curved and kind.
(Sort of lips that mock the roses
Worn with pert, tip-tilted noses.)
There I think you called me Billy,
And I tried to guess your name,
For your neck was like a lily,
And your blushes like a flame.
(Sort of flame that burns its brightest
When the waist is squeezed the tightest.)
All the night with soft stars twinkled,
And the white 'moon rode in state
Over fields the dew had sprinkled
E’er we found the train was late.
(Sort of train that o’er creation
Crawls and stops at every station.)
Often standing sadly under
Skies no longer quite so blue,
I look back and mildly wonder
What the deuce became of you.
(Sort of you whose memory teases
While it stimulates and pleases.)
N.S.W.

“F.L.J.”: Major Dr. E. T. Brennan, of
West Australia, who won the Military Cross
for bravery on the field, is a Victorian, lie
was born in Hawthorn, and had some letters
tacked after his name at the Melbourne Uni-
versity. lie was practising in W.A. when
the war cloud burst, and he joined the lltli
Battalion as medical officer. He was at the
landing at Gallipoli.

A ivian de Crespigny, a very young bro-
ther of that steady goer on the Australian
Inky Track, Phil de Crespigny, has gained
his British aviation-certificate and the usual
flight-lieutenancy therewith. Another bro-
ther, Captain Constantine de Crespigny, who
at one time used to.toil in the Western Dis-
trict in the interests of the Willaura and
Glen Thompson sheeplutocrats, left for the
Front some months ago with an A.A.M.C.
outfit. Philip de Crespigny the elder man-
ages the Bank of Victoria at Melbourne.
Boucaut, the lately-deceased S. Aus.
Judge, rarely missed rapping back an answer
to an interjeetor. A continued criminal, con-
victed of housebreaking, with a long list of
previous convictions against him, was up for
sentence. The old man was about to com-
mence a homily when Sikes interrupted him
with, “Cut it short, Judge; how much?”
“No, I’ll cut it long,” said Boucaut. “Ten
3 r ears.”
H. C. E. Muecke, though one of Adelaide’s
most prominent business men, has found the
present clamor against German extraction
names too strong for him, and resigns all
his directorships. One result was a keen
fight for the seat on the Bank of Adelaide
Board. A. G. Itymill, managing director of
the Canowie Pastoral Co., was beaten by
Howard Lloyd in a contest in December, on
the death of James Harvey. This time,
however, he increased his 8500 vote to
12,700, and won from Leonard Bakewell
with 10,100. It was the biggest poll, and
the highest winning vote, in the Bank’s his-
tory. Muecke’S place on the Executor and
Trustee Co. has been filled by John Barker,
and on the Adelaide Steamship Co. by
Leonard Bakewell.

Napoleon H. Louwyck, who has been
appointed to superintend an Anglican col-
lection-plate at Yorketown (S.A.) worked
for years as a It.C. priest in the back country
oi South Aus. In 11)01) he speculated in
Anglicanism, and has since that time been
earning a crust as curate in Manchester
(Eng.). He is on his way from the Cold
Country to Adelaide.
George T. B. Ivyngdon, who passed out
in January at Treninnick, Cornwall (Eng.),
was one of the last survivors of the “Canter-
bury Association” which was formed to
found an Anglican settlement in Maoriland.
Kyngdon came as chaplain of one of the
four ships which sailed from England with
the expedition in ISSO. and became the first
vicar of what is now the Anglican cathedral
in Christchurch. He afterwards went to
Taranaki, and rounded up stragglers from
the Anglican fold in the bush country out-
side New Plymouth; but a breakdown of
health sent him back to the Cold Country
in 1861. Though pursued since then by ill
health, he won through to the 94th lap.
One of the oldest of Sydney’s ancient
mariners took up his moorings in Fiddler’s
Green when Frederick Bracegirdle passed
out last week. Born in Surrey (Eng.) 84
years ago, Bracegirdle was in the Medi-
terranean tra'de as a boy, and came to Aus-
tralasian waters for the first time in the
Samuel Enderby, which sailed from London
for Hobart in 1849. For years he traded in
the China tea clippers, and also to the West
Indies, but eventually he gravitated into
steam, and in 1866 joined the Panama Mail
Company, making seven voyages in the
Ivaikoura between Panama and Sydney. In
1869 Bracegirdle, then a master mariner,
signed up for the N.S.W. coastal trade, and
ran for many years between Sydney and the
Northern Rivers. In 1884 he climbed ashore
into a billet as assistant harbormaster at
Sydney. lie reached the age limit in 1901,
and settled down on the Balmain water-
front, varying the monotony by an occa-
sional job as an assessor in the Marine
Court. A son. who before the war was
district naval officer at Newcastle (N.S.W.),
is now in command of the Australian naval
bridging train in the Mediterranean, and was
recently • wounded and mentioned in
despatches from the Dardanelles. A daughter
is married to a flight-lieutenant (Watkins)
in the naval air service.
The enemy’s submarine frightfulness has
wiped out the family of the Rev. J. M. Mac-
donald, of Smithton (Tas.). His only son,
a lieutenant in the Navy, was lost when the
Hawke was torpedoed in the North Sea in
October, 1914, and his only daughter Enid,
who was on her way to India to be married,
was one of those drowned when the Persia
was sunk on December 80, 1915.
A Melbourne Methodist movement to com-
memorate the parsonic jubilee of Rev.
Fitchett reveals the fact that Rev. Joseph
Nicholson, the secretary for the movement,
has been the particular friend of Fitchett for
many years, Now, as Nicholson has for
long been the friend of Ikey Mo,
in his insidious warfare against the tote, it
would be a fair thing for the bookmakers of
Victoria to subscribe the £5OO which their
helpful brother is endeavoring to raise for a
memorial to Fitchett. The Federated Book-
makers’ Association ought to rejoice in this
opportunity to drop a “monkey” into the
Methodist hat.
Colonel Robert Wilson, dead in Egypt at
45, was a son of the late R. G. Wilson, the
best-known grocer in Melbourne. ColonJ
Wilson lived in one of the mellowest parts
of Toorak; but he was also a bush-lover.
The land now occupied by the Broad-
meadows camp belonged to him ; and he had
spent much of his time, for years past, on
his holding at Whittlesea, a beautiful and
verdant spot at the foot of the Plenty
Ranges. There, his name stands very high
in the estimation of the neighboring settler,
in whose company he was accustomed to
agitate for the usual district reform, and,
in a democratic manner, provide the cheque
for the usual good purpose. The dead
soldier’s only son is with the A.I.F. in
Egypt.
“Lud” considers the fruity past:—
A new biography of Queen Adelaide fills up some
old gaps, and, incidentally, shows what a lot of
anti-German feeling there was in the England of
that day. Dora Jordan, the actress who lived with
William Duke of Clarence (afterwards William IV.)
till 1811, had three illegitimate daughters before
she and the monarch became acquainted. This
batch was her undoing. William allowed her £2O
a week, and her stage salary was £3O, total £5O.
She was not extravagant, but had a horde of depend-
ants who fed on her. She broke an agreement made
in 1811 not to go on the stage again, and William
turned off her pension. The poor creature subse-
quently died in poverty in France. Meanwhile her
10 children by William, the Fitzclarences, were
reared in his household. After 1811 he fished about
for a rich wife. He was refused in turn by Lady
Catherine Long, Miss Elphinstone and Miss Wyke-
ham. So he bachelored up to 1817, when Princess
Charlotte died. lie then set about finding young
German wives for George lll.’s sons, Clarence,
Kent and Cambridge. For various reasons the four
others, George, York, Cumberland and Sussex, were
ineligible. In 1818 Clarence, then 03 years old,
married Adelaide of Saxe-Meningen, aged 26. Thev
had two daughters, who died in infancy. They, like
Charlotte, would have settled Victoria’s chances.
York, senior to Clarence, died in 1827. Clarence
became heir-apparent ar.d king in 1830. Adelaide
was much worried over the Fitzclarence swarm, which
William insisted on keeping in his house, and also
by public abuse. The Morning Chronicle boldly
called her “a nasty German flow.”
A FISHERMAN’S TALE.
Our torpedo boats met several British cruisers
which fled. Our boats pursued and sank the new
cruiser Arabis, and hit a second cruiser with a tor-
pedo.— (Merman Admiralty report.
The British Admiralty notifies that the cruisers
mentioned were four mine sweepers, of which three
returned sately.

“WERE BOUND TO WIN.”
H.O.’: C. J. Dennis’s “Sentimental
Bloke” is making himself popular in Eng-
land. The author has lately received letters
from H. G. Wells and W. j. Locke handing
him the praise that cheers. “You must cer-
tainly come to London when the war is
over,” writes Wells.
The change of associates to Justice
Hodges, of Melbourne, is an interesting mat-
ter of military significance. Wilbur Ander-
son, the Judge’s present associate, has en-
listed and is about to go into camp. Ilis
successor is James Borrowman, who was one
°f the original Australian landing force at
Gallipoli and lost his left arm in the fight.
Lawyer Charlie Davis, of Bendigo, chief
censor for Victoria, has exchanged the axe
for the sword. He is one of the officers
chosen to encourage Australia’s new army
of 50,000 to keep up the traditions of Gal-
lipoli.
“Chinkz”: The recent passing out of
John D. Mackay, at Leeholm, Kelso
(N.S.W.), revives a memory of Mor-
gan, who bushranged parts of north-
eastern Victoria and N.S.W. in the
early ’6o’s. Mackay, who was one of the
Mackay clan from The Grange, at Everton,
in the Beechworth district, took up the
Nangunyan station, in the Riverina just
when Morgan had the landowners along the
Big Trickle badlv scared. Morgan heard
that Mackay had a loaded gun waiting for
him at the Nangunyan, so at the first oppor-
tunity he started a bushfire near the home-
stead. When Mackay appeared the bush-
ranger held him up and invited him to
where the gun was kept. The fact that the
weapon had just previously been discharged
at a kangaroo undoubtedly saved the squat-
ter’s life. A few days later Morgan, who
merely stole a horse and provisions from
Nangunyan, shot a man in a cold-blooded
frenzy on a neighboring station. Before the
week was out he was killed at Rutherford’s
l’eeeheelba station, on the Ovens River,
near Wangaratta, where a station-hand
named Quinlan potted him.
“Jimmy” Borrowman, of Armadale (Vic.),
only son of that James Borrowman who
directs the fortunes of the Trustees and Ex-
ecutors Co., lost his left arm at the Gaba
Tepe landing. Since his recent return there
has been small occasion for him to wonder
what to do with his future, for several open-
ings offered. The most agreeable was that
of associate to Justice Hodges, and he has
been duly appointed to the billet. Shortly
before leaving for the Front the youth had
passed his law-finals at Melbourne Univer-
sity.
THE IMPORTANCE OF BEING JEAMES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sir Charles Wakefield, Lord Mayor of London, has
applied for exemption from military service of a
19-years-old footman at the Mansion House.— Cable.
Loud clangs the War God's brazen gong.
The call is to the young aHd fit,
For Freedom’s sake, to come along
And in the ranks to do their bit;
But still, of course, nobody deems
That such a call applies to Jeames!
Though Mars may thunder in his rage,
His status must Sir Chawles maintain ;
’Twould be a blot on Britain's page,
And fill each alderman with pain,
If to frustrate the foeman’s schemes,
The Empire was deprived of Jeames!
If London falls Jeames must be there —
Though sickened at the sight of blood—
The Kaiser to announce, with care:
“His Majesty is here, me Lud.”
In pleasant contrast to the screams
Outside will be the voice of Jeames.
VICTORIA.

The Menace of
Germany.
After the War.
This is a world-wide war, and it is
being waged for the purpose of a world
power. This is not merely for the pur-
pose of military power, although the
means of achieving world power are
necessarily military in their nature.
But the war is waged by Germany for
the purpose of achieving COMMER-
CIAL, FINANCIAL AND INDUS-
TRIAL DOMINATION OF ' THE
EARTH. This is the great purpose
for which she has devoted her tremen-
dous systematised energies for nearly
half a century
■ UNLESS. ...W E TAKE PRE-
CAUTIONARY MEASURES NOW
....we shall after a season of barren
inactivity be overwhelmed by an inun-
dation of Enemy Goods, swamping not
only our good intentions and the
possibility of giving effect to them, but
giving to those who have vowed our
destruction the fruits of our enterprise
and industry
Hon. W. M. HUGHES,
Prime Minister of Australia.
The Opportunity is NOW!
For nearly half a century the civilised world has
thought and dreamt of the menace of Germany’s
Military Might—a menace that has at last material-
ised in a world-wide struggle for supremacy.
To-day, with our National consciousness almost
wholly preoccupied with the waging of war, we need to
lift our heads above the din and turmoil of the conflict
to realise clearly the issues that are at stake.
Only so shall we avoid the danger of a peace which
is no peace at all —because it does not remove the real
causes of the war.
German Militarism, beaten and even humiliated,
will be no bar to the long-planned German war on our
Commerce and Industry.
Victory—even a decisive Victory—will be fruitless
unless it leads toa fundamental change in our National
business policy, and the golden opportunity is NOW.
Germany MUST BE. BEATEN
NOW or she never will.
Clearly, unmistakably we realise the National
need for economic independence ... our ignorance and
weakness of the past, in the needless barter for German
goods. But to-day, conscious and wide-awake to the
menace of enemy goods, we know our weakness —and
our strength.
We CAN beat Germany now
IF WE WILL.
For the sake of our Empire’s future
we must bar all Enemy Goods, and in
the preference for things all British supply
our National needs.
Australia and the work of Australians
demand preference first of all, and its
practice will banish the menace that
threatens after the war.

As the eye runs down that profit column it
gets a few bumps. For instance, as at Dec.,
13, when £54,765 was disclosed. But of
course some of that wasn’t made in the six
months. There was an old stocking some-
where, and it was emptied into the till—a
stocking saved up for some contingency that
didn’t arise. It has been a fine" office for
stockings, this—thanks to the hahit of going
without them at Manly. Surfing made the
co. Twenty years ago you could have had
the shares thrown at you if the co. thought
you were a solid enough person to put on
the register. In the brief table at the top
there is mention of three transfers to capi-
tal, totalling £123,207; and the capital even
now is only £211.232. So it has been con-
siderably more than doubled in less than
seven years, and the 13$p.c. dividend is
equal to 23 p.c. on the capital as it stood in
1009. Go back further than that, and the
results are even more astonishing.
And you can’t tell what the co. has done
merely by looking at the growth of dividends.
Seven years ago the co. had a surplus of
£20,118 of liquid assets. To-day, after pay-
ing for all repairs and additions made mean-
time, and putting more than £35,000 into real
property and workshop plant, etc., its liquid
surplus is £98,440. This paper doesn’t know
how much has been put into steamers mean-
time, but it seems to he well over £70,000,
representing the Bellubera, Kalgowlah and
Barren joey. Add £78,000 and £35.000 and
£70.000, and you get some idea of the money
the co. has made apart from what the share-
holders have had. It isn’t suggested that
every penny of this is net profit. These
vessels have been depreciating, and some of
the money has to be put by for renewals.
Btill the depreciation is not rapid, on account
of the really splendid manner in which the
whole of the co.’s fleet is maintained. Fur-
ther, out of the accumulations certain in-
definite amounts are to be earmarked to
cover various risks —including the very sub-
stantial risk of a disaster to one of the co.’s
boats through mishandling. Such a disaster
might easily cost the co. £200.000.
“The war has caused a decrease of revenue
and increased running exes.,” the directors
say ; but. as the next comparison shows, the
co. hasn’t been drifting perceptibly towards
the rocks: —
Dec.,'l4. June,’ls. Dec.,’ls.
Assets other than steamers,
freehold and plant ..£85,407 £96,207 £99,899
Liabilities 1,509 1,021 1,459
Surplus liquid assets. . £83,898 £95,186 £98,440
That’s £15.000 over and above the dividends
in the 12 months, and over and above any-
thing spent on vessels and plant. All the
same, the past six months’ figures are dis-
tinctly poorer than usual; and Sydney
hasn’t really started to feel the war yet.
Presently also the co. will have to pay a
trifle more for its monopoly of the ferry
traffic to Manly, the local Council having
struck against the hitherto quite inadequate
subsidy. But shareholders needn’t shiver:
the margin between earnings and dividends
is quite wide enough to shelter them.

This is a branch of the Savings Bank which
was created to displace the old semi-political
Advances to Settlers Board. The money is
raised by debenture loans from the Savings
Department: thus far £4.355.000 has
been borrowed in this way. and £120,050 has
been raised through funded stock. The busi-
ness is managed by the Board of Commis-
sioners which also controls the Savings De-
partment; but it is guided by various Acts.
For instance, there is a sub-department
established to finance borrowers under the
Closer Settlement Promotion Act. As this
measure provides that the whole of the pur-
chase money may be lent to the settler in
certain cases, and as that plainly is not legi-
timate banking business, the Commissioners
have a guarantee from the Government cov-
ering the bank against possible losses in
these particular transactions.
The whole business is organised in some
such way as this. First, there is the Sav-
ings Bank, which takes the public’s savings.
It invests’ in Government stock, deposits,
mortgages, and in these Advance Depart-
ment debentures. The Advance Department
in its turn finances the ordinary settlers
individually and also three groups of settlers,
which groups are treated as sub-departments
(1) Advances for Homes, (2) Closer Set-
tlement Promotion, (3) Irrigation Farms.
The total advances are as follows:
Advances to Settlers .. .. .. £2,514,150
„ for Homes . . . . . . 215,085
~ on Closer Settlement terms . . 1,546,734
~ on Irrigation Farms . . .. 13,403
Total. . £4,289,372
There are also a few thousands outstanding
for interest and charges.
The Homes and Irrigation Farms are new
departments. Parliament, w r hen it author-
ised the former, put a limit of £300,000 upon
the borrowings; and, though the books were
only opened on July 1, 1914, applications for
the whole amount had been already received
when the Commissioners reported at the end
of November, 1915. Its purpose is “to
enable any person w r ho is not the owner of
a dwelling-house wdthin the State (other
than that upon which the advance is re-
quired) to obtain an advance to erect, en-
large or purchase a dwelling-house or pay off
any mortgage already existing.” The de-
partment shows a loss for the year of £2759 ;
but this includes all preliminary exes. The
Irrigation Farms loans, like those of the
Closer Settlement Department, are guaran-
teed by the Government. Under that guaran-
tee the Act allows the bank to lend “upon
mortgage of any irrigation-farm lease, not-
withstanding that such holding is mortgaged
or charged to the Commissioner for Water
Conservation and Irrigation, subject, how-
ever, to the approval of the Minister.” The
loan may be “of any amount not exceeding
three-fourths of the value of the holder in the
permanent or prospective improvements on
the land.” other than those still owned by'
the, Commissioner. The loss for the year
was £129; but as in the case of the Homes
Department this has no significance what-
ever. The Closer Settlement Promotion De-
partment made a profit of £lO9B ; and it has
now its own reserve of £1987—-wretchedly
little, of course, considering the risks. But,
as before remarked, the department is run
for a specific purpose, and the Government
is prepared to accept the risk without cover-
ing it by charging a commensurate rate of
interest. And, by the way. to give the Devil
his due. it wasn’t the reckless Labor Govern-
ment that did it. either, for the Act was
passed in 1910.

In 10 years over £150,000 has been written
off, in addition to amounts charged in Profit
and Loss to cover ordinarv renewals and
repairs. This really means that £150,000
of profit has been spent in such things as
the new works at Pukueri, recently opened ;
or old works have been written down and a
corresponding amount put into stock or liquid
assets. The result is showm in the third
line in the following comparison :
1906. 1910. 1914. 1915.
Debentures . . £173,500 £150,000
Other liabilities 91,319 £81,619 £133,290 307,385
Property, plant
etc 369,647 297,723 253,899 273,522
Liquid assets. . 128,368 99,891 209,472 542,587
Very large additions have certainly been
made to the property, plant, etc., in the past
nine years; yet not only has the wdiole of
the cost been paid out of profits, but
£414,219 has been added to the liquid assets
(which include stock), while liabilities have
grown by only £192,566 and capital by
£7334. Put it another w 7 ay : assuming that
property and plant are worth to-day only as
much as they were nine years ago, the co.
would still show an undivided profit of a
trifle over £214.000 in that period The capi-
tal nine years ago was £165,920, and now it
is £173,254. Meantime a dividend of from 8
p.c. to 11 p.c. has been paid. Altogether, then,
the co. can be said to have done pretty well
—averaged perhaps 20 p.c. to 25 p.c. p.a.
Maybe it was more, it depends on the real in-
crease in the value of property and plant.
Just at present liabilities are unusually
heavy, but that is due to the financing of
exceptionally heavy “sundry debtors, invest-
ments, consignments unrealised and stocks in
works”—a miscellaneous heap which is
about £250,000 more than usual. Probably
because of the size of some of these items the
co. is building up a special insurance fund ;
but, apart from that, this paper wouldn’t be
at all surprised to presently find that, before
serving up the profits, it has carved enough
out of them to make a very decent contin-
gency fund.

For many years the capital was under
£60,000, and the dividend was 15 p.c. The
Act passed to regulate the price of gas very
properly permitted the co. to capitalise the
reserves, visible and undisclosed; in other
words, the co. was, with certain qualifica-
tions, limited to 10 p.c. on the actual value
of the undertaking, and to 7 p.c. on any new
ordinary capital got in subsequent to the
passing of the Act. The present capital is
made up as follows: —
“A” shares (10 p.c.), £2 paid ..£132,740
“B” shares (10 p.c.), 30/-paid .. 99,555
“O” shares ( 7 p.c.), 22/-paid ... 31,549
Total ..£263,844
Or. the reconstruction the assets were natu-
rally not valued on a basis which allowed
of an easy 10 p.c. Any business man would
of course value them as high as possible;
for that, though making the immediate rate
of dividend look small, would give the co.
a chance to benefit by any increase in the
ej rning capacity of the plant, etc. Thus for
the past half-year £BB2l is distributed,
whereas under the Act the co. can pay away
£12,500 without reducing the price "of gas
below the standard fixed by the Act. There
is thus room for a considerable rise in the
market value of these shares. Very likely
the management didn’t contemplate a divi-
dend as small as the co. is now paying;
exes, seem to have sprung up and upset cal-
culations. Only a year ago gas sales, etc.,
brought in £26,319 at a cost of £15,207;
for the past term the revenue of £27,012
was produced at a cost which had risen to
£17,935. However, the co. isn’t exactly
despairing, and is putting out a good deal
of money. This shows :
Dec.,’l4. June,’ls. Dec.,’ls.
Liabilities £17,737 £30,064 £30,259
Buildings, plant, mains.. 267,934 280,480 290,887
9,742 11,954 10,466

Apparently the co. has been going into debt
for plant, notwithstanding calls have
been made steadily on the “C” shares. Here,
by the way, is an anomaly in connection
with the Commonwealth Government’s pro-
hibition of the issue of new capital. The
wily and often very dubious concern which
has issued 20s. shares paid to Is., may go
on calling in capital, while its often more
solid competitor, which had its shares paid
up, can’t get in capital without an official
sanction that isn’t likely to be given.

Liquid assets are £21,003; liabilities not
thus covered amount to £241.421. Advances
and_freeholds total £379,392. There is thus

round w r ortli that much less than has been
advanced on them, assuming that business
has been carried on with ordinary prudence.
The greater weakness lies in the very poor
holding of liquid assets. The co. lias 111
hand only 8 p.c. of its debts to depositors,
etc. With an ordinary bank this would be
perilous; but presumably all these deposits
are for fairly long periods. If they are no
the co. might easily find itself in a ® IPSS ‘
The thing that mustn’t be overlooked m a
case of this sort is not that the co. must
pay, but that it must pay on a certain date.
That’s what mostly causes the trouble.

ALUMINOFERUTC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth. — Advt.

Efficient grinding is one of the many problems
that mine managers have to solve. The Hardinge
Conical Mills have proved to the Calumet and Hecla
Mining Co. that grinding is not such a difficult
problem ; they have installed 64 of them, and say
“The Hardinge Conical Mill is the most efficient
grinding machine we have yet gotten hold of.” If
they say this of the mills, it should be worth the
while of mine managers here to inquire about them.
The Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
sole representatives for them in the Commonwealth,
and will be glad to forward particulars.— Advt. [1

29 BRANCHES IN QUEENSLAND. E.C.
Agents and Correspondents throughout the World.
Thp net profits of three Broken Hill mines
year totalled £1,013,380, viz., Propri-
jL./ £139,228, South £291,101, Sulphide
Corporation £280,000. Almost the whole
,ir the shares in Sulphide ; three-quarters of
Proprietary and probably a quarter of South
a reheld outside of Australia. It is well
k noW n that large blocks of shares in these
■md other mines are owned by alien enemies,
and it would be interesting to know how
the Australian Government proposes to try
to stop them from participating in these
profits. It may be easy enough to com-
mandeer the interests held by the enemy
within the gates, but without the co-operation
of the British Government in the way of
confiscation the enemy without cannot be
touched. And it is a shilling to o granite
that British misplaced magnanimity will be
proof against such drastic action.
Arrangements are in progress for the re-
sumption of work at the Mount Hope Cop-
per Mine (N.S.W.). The surface plant is
to he remodelled to ensure economy and
higher efficiency in concentration. Finan-
cial success will largely depend upon
mechanical perfection, which was far from
being attained in former campaigns. Even at
the present shallow depth the mine re-
veals enormous quantities of ore which,
with copper anywhere over the £7O mark,
should be highly payable, provided that the
mechanical engineer and metallurgical
chemist are given a chance to do effective
work. This will mean the installation of
an oil-flotation plant. The oft-discussed
project of smelting the ore at Blayney has
been abandoned, so the ore will be sent to
Port Kembla instead. The Public Works
Department of N.S.W. persists in its
policy of running the Condobolin-Broken
Hill railway line 13 miles away from
Mount Hope. To the lay mind this is
inexplicable—it can see no practical reason
why the line should not run right through the
different mine properties.
* * * *
New Burragorang (N.S.W.) silver mine
has not had as successful a half-year as was
anticipated. Work has been hindered by the
shortage of explosives; the lode pinched in
the principal workings, thus reducing output
and increasing cost of mining; yet, in
spite of these disadvantages, a working profit
of £1046 is shown. There is now £2734 to
credit, but the directors wisely decide not
to make a distribution, as further shaft
sinking has to be undertaken. Melbourne
speculators who own the bulk of the shares
will be disappointed, but their patience may
yet be rewarded—the mine ought soon to be
drawn out of the category of being a splendid
wage-earner, which it has been for many
years. Yerranderie mine-owners may be good
mining engineers, but as political engineers
they are lamentable failures. During the
borrow-and-burst boom of the Labor party
they ought to have applied persuasive influ-
ences to get a railway built from Picton to
the field. Their silence has been as the dul-
ness of lead, whereas there should have been
much talk with the metallic ring of gold in
it. Such a railway would be an advisable
and a legitimate undertaking. Talk about
land for returned soldiers! With a railway
the Burragorang valley would absorb them
all and leave room for more ; a few market
gardens would be provided for the metro-
polis, and many mines which are now merely
tantalising “stringers-on” would become
payable.

The surprise of Cock’s Pioneer, Eldorado
(Vic.), has been the rich patches found among
the old McEvoy props and laths at 70ft.
What lies ahead therefore largely depends
on the area of the ground so left by the allu-
vial-man, plus the intact block higher up the
gutter. Six months’ work to November 30
saw the clean-up of runs 5 and 6, out of
which £10,843 was netted. No 6 run turned
over 100,000 cub. yds., averaging 11.71 d. for
gold and 3.84 d. for tin oxide per cub. yd.,
taking tin at £l6O per ton. On the working
account costs figure at 9.76 d. for 301.000
cub. yds. shifted. This includes an allow-
ance of 1.25 d. transferred to a suspense
account for barge removal, etc., so that the
real costs were as close to B|d. as possible.
This is stiffen than expected, but so long
as 15d. dirt show's up, the margin is safe
enough. But what does Moline mean when
he reports 3,000,000 cub. yds. above the
dredge as worth only a shilling, with a pos-
sible additional 1,000,000 cub. yds. of the
same value? If costs are to be 9|d., the
uncertainty as to what the McEvoy may
have left at 70ft. adds a gambling touch to
the show'. In the old paddocks worked by
•lack Dane’s plant the average w 7 as about
Is. 4d. If Moline’s average, half a mile
away, has been roughly Is. 2d. he doesn’t
run much risk wdien he expects sluicing
values to beat bore values. Meanwhile,
i*] 11 aD f ements are a head for duplicating
ie plant. The impulse to launch out must
ne the 10|d. results got from a line of bores
"av down stream. If these have been com-
puted on the same conservative lines as the
bores above No. 1 paddock. Cock’s Pioneer
400Q S (W| Ut k S ! S T et ing - If on the
_JuiuxMJ cub. yds. above the dredge a profit
° only 4d. a yard is got the return would be
£66,000, or if sd. a yard £83,000. Hence the
original capital of £lOO,OOO would he nearly
returned, leaving the big block below the
dredge as an asset to the good, for the
bores are over 2000 ft. west of the No. 1
paddock.
* * * *
Only two Daylesford (Vic.) mines are on
the div. list —Ajax Central and North Ajax
and probably neither has much more
than a year’s profitable ground in
sight. Central drifted off divs. last
month, though it had a bit of a
reserve to play with. But as George
Buchanan didn’t go into mourning there
wasn t the usual display of uneasiness.
Everybody is now anxiously waiting to see
if the crosscut at 1150 ft. gets values. If it
doesn’t, and there isn’t a mine at Daylesford
that has good values below 1000 ft., someone
will get a nasty knock. Bill Gullock and J.
Trevor are pushing on with development
work at the North Nuggetty and the Nug-
getty ; but there is obviously very little bloom
on the field just now.
* * * *
Chiltern Valley, Chiltern (Vic.), got off
the call list in February on the strength of
mining 17dwt. dirt. Veteran John Cock
battles along as cheerily as ever, though
he is up against labor shortage, crab
holes and an uneven bottom to the lead.
His consolation is that farther west he
should get out of the influence of the tribu-
tary. Then he will have better ' working
ground and the prospect of an enrichment
of the wash from gold brought in by the
sideline tributary. John Cock is now on the
far side of 80 and deserves a bit of luck
to keep him cheerful.
* * * *
A new application of the cyanide process
is reported from Victoria. The Bealiba
Alluvial Co. has attached a filter-press plant
to its puddling-machines to treat the
“slum” as it is discharged. Results so far
are not satisfactory. Mechanically the
plant works well, but the presence of free
acids in the “slum” interferes with the
action of cyanide. There ought to be no
difficulty in neutralising the acid ; it ought
to be done in the puddling operation. The
cyanide man is a familiar figure on all the
Victorian alluvial fields. The quantity of
old slum treated annually runs into hun-
dreds of thousands of tons, and although the
average recovery does not exceed ldwt. per
ton there is a good profit in the business,
handling costs being low and the consump-
tion of cyanide averaging under Mb. per ton.
* * *- “ *
“Bulla” : The scoop in Edna Centrals was
not the aftermath of Frank Savage’s visit,
as might be imagined from his I-told-you-so
smile, but was due to the discovery, 216 ft.
south of the No. 1 level, of a horseshoe-shaped
run of quartz, which got in the way of a
drive put out to secure cemented gravel.
The importance of the strike was dis-
counted at first because only a hand’s-
throw away No. 3 reef had shown itself to
be a duffer and there was every chance that
the new body would simply blend into it.
When 18ft. and 20ft. widths with 50s. to 80s.
values were reported, a puzzled public mopped
its head at the possibility of a twin Edna
May formation having been cut. Prices got
their final lift when the news leaked out that
some shrewd heads who rushed up from
Perth liked the look of the stranger. The
market by cutting its best caper helped the
erstwhile bilious-looking bulls to assemble
cheerfully for drinks in the old-fashioned
way over the chance of getting back their
shekels. On merits there isn’t much that
can be said of the newcomer, for 25ft. over-
head is wash. That it is big is correct, but
as to whether values are good is not dis-
closed in the official communique. The effect
of the find, so far, has been to wash the last
trace of bear-juice out of the market. The
danger point is that the south-east face
looks like breaking into veins. What has
to be settled is whether the V-shaped forma-
tion in the crosscut is not a vein branching
into the main shoot and enriching it at the
junction. Points like these will be settled
when the 150 ft. drive now being rushed
along discloses the constitution of the shoot
75ft. deeper.
* * * *
The woodline strike cut deeply into
Ivalgoorlie profits. Great Boulder at £11,935
represented a decrease of £12,548 compared
with December; Golden Horseshoe could
only manage to scrape up £603 as against
£6744; South Kalgurli weighed in several
hundred pounds light; and Boulder Per-
severance’s figures were too bad to make
public. Kalgurli and Associated, by drawing
on the bullion reserve, made it appear as if
nothing had happened. So far Lake View
and Star hasn’t spoken; but Ivanhoe, which
worked full time with the aid of what wood
fuel it had supplemented by Collie coal, was
not affected at all.
* * * *
Gwalia Central, registered in Westralia
but bossed from London, held a meeting of
shareholders in Leonora the other day. For
the past six years it has worked a number
of leases, including the Tower Hill in that
district, and what it didn’t spend in other
ways it splashed up in cabling gaudy pro-
gress reports to the Bull office. Latterly it
has devoted most of its time and attention to
the Trump mine, from which an occasional
good return has been obtained. The pro-
ceeds from one crushing, however, seem to
be swallowed up in digging out another, and
consequently shareholders get nothing. High
gold values at depth are talked of by the
Board, but so many exaggerated accounts
of the value of the co.’s ventures have already
been given that little notice is paid nowa-
days to what is officially said about them.
A shipment of 181 tons of blister copper
from the Government smelter at Ravens-
thorpe (W.A.) has just been sold in London
for £28,000. Since the end of 1914, when
Minister for Mines Collier assisted the in-
dustry by taking over the smelter, a heap
of copper has been treated and marketed
for the claimholders on the fiqld. In every
instance returns have been good, and after
advancing 90 p.c. of the value of the ore at
the time of treatment and allowing for all
charges the balance handed over to the pro-
ducer has been substantial. So there is at
least one State enterprise in the West that
is giving satisfaction. Anxious to give even
greater satisfaction by saving brokerage and
commission, Collier recently applied to have
the Mines Department enrolled as a member
of the Metal Exchange. His repeated appli-
cations to the Federal Attorney-General,
however, have been ignored, and at latest
he was still sending telegrams wanting to
know the reason why.
* * * *
Obit Lake View South. The deceased
struggled with an unprofitable area at Kal-
goorlie for some years and then tried its
luck at Kanowna and Yarri (W.A.). As
it did no good at either place it returned
to Ivalgoorlie, but was soon disheartened
and trusted to tributors for succor. Again
drawing a blank it fastened on to a Yukon
venture; but as this straw could not keep
it afloat it went under.
* * * *
Collie (W.A.) produced 286,666 tons of
coal in 1915 worth £137,949. Area
of field is now 29,897 acres, and the
number of men employed is given as
500. The contributors to the year’s out-
put were: Collie Co. 59,195 tons, Collie Co-
operative 63,500, Scottish Collieries 37,259,
Proprietary 73,781, and Westralian Co.
52,948.
* * * #
Speakman’s luck is still out. The new
manager recently predicted good prqspects
and got the battery going. Result, lidwt.
recovery over the plates, and the balance,
allegedly 7dwt., in the tailings. Slimy water
is blamed for the disappointing return, and
in order to determine for all time whether
the co. has a mine or not a further test
parcel of 25 tons is to be sent to Ivalgoorlie
for treatment.
Frank Moss, George Ridgway and two or
three other cute Westralian mining men who
got hold of the Lancefield last year seem to
be succeeding where Hoover, Govett and
8., M. & Co. failed. Tons of money were
spent on the show before the present crowd
stepped in, and finally it was bought for a
song by one of the creditors, who turned it
over to Moss and party a little later for a
few thousand and a small interest. Since
then the monthly profit has never been less
than £2OOO, and is now T up to £3OOO.
Broncho Horseshoe (W.A.) intends to get
a treatment plant and shake itself up. Its
admirers still get up steam about its great,
possibilities and gleefully point to the fact
that tributors recently took out some hun-
dreds of tons of 40s. ore. Pessimists say
that the tributors picked the stuff, and fierce
arguments on this point are still proceeding.
Water for treatment is reckoned to be a
big fly in the co.’s ointment, but if the values
are really in the mine it wouldn’t cost a
great deal to connect by means of pipe line
with the goldfields water main.
Belief in the resurrection of the dead is
growing in the West. The Lancefield, at
Beria, and the Light of Asia, at Cue, have
lately risen again. Westralia Mt. Morgan
is said to be following suit, and the New
Boddington at Goongarrie is coming on.
Two cwt. of specimen stone, described by
the local paper as “the richest seen for
years,” has lately been on exhibition at Kal-
goorlie, and a syndicate which has an option
for £6OOO cash and 6000 shares in any co.
formed already believes its fortune is made.
A whoop comes from the local paper
about the improved prospects of Yilgarn
(W.A.). Corinthian North is declared to
have doubled its value; Bullfinch is described
as looking better than it has done for years ;
Edna May Central, about which nothing too
bad could previously be printed, is given a
boost; and Burbidge, where the Great Vic-
toria, Broncho, Horseshoe and others hang
out, is said to have the makings of the
busiest centre on the whole field. Forres-
tania, on whose rocks the “great gold hunt”
split, is admitted to be under a cloud, but
as yet undeserving of condemnation.
Parker’s Range is referred to in glowing
terms, and Marda is tipped to buck up as
soon as it rains. Nothing is said about the
sick or the dying, or the inmates of Yilgarn’s
already well-filled cemetery, some of which,
according to the local rag, looked as well as
anything but a short time ago.
- - * * * *
The latest oiLtalk in Tas. concerns the
Langloh-Park district, near Hamilton, where
there is a big coal deposit on which one-time
Government Geologist Montgomery reported
most enthusiastically. Oil is said to have
been found there, but, if so, the discovery
is being kept very quiet.
* * * *
The Mount Lyell Co. has decided
that with its cheap water-power from
Lake Margaret it can turn out other
things besides copper at Queenstown. For
a start it is putting in a plant to make
caustic soda, and intends later to turn out
other chemicals. For some montlis past
the co. has been experimenting to see
whether it can save the sulphur fumes from
the smelters and turn them into‘sulphuric
acid. So far results are promising.
Though only a small affair compared with
Mt. Bischoff, or even the Briseis, the Pioneer
tin mine (Tas.) has been a good thing for
the shareholders. Its latest dividend of Is.
per share brings the total amount handed
out to £312,586. And •it is still going
strong.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A GOOD REMEDY FOR
SALLOW COMPLEXIONS.
Everyone may have a clear and bright
complexion, free from furrows or wrinkles if
they will only take a little daily trouble’ in
the following manner: Buy some Puri tol wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply every
night, lap a little cold water on the face ten
times with the hands every morning, allow-
ing it to get thoroughly round the closed
eyes, and pinch the skin very gently across
all wrinkles. Puritol Wax Soap, which
leaves a fine film of wax on the skin, should
be used; this acts as a protective during the
day. This treatment arouses the dormant
neives and blood vessels, and undoubtedly
accounts for many of the clear complexions
so much admired by those who do not yet
know the secret. — Advt. tq
