A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, February 2S, 191 G.
My Dear Arini, —
Six o’clock closing of hotels was acclaimed
by wopien as too good to be true. It was
only true for seven days. If Senator Pearce
had used his power under the War Pre-
cautions Act to shut the bars at six
o’clock till the enemy outside our gates
was beaten, a unanimous woman’s vote
would have made the drought permanent.
This is not merely the extreme view of female
prohibitionists or cranks of any color. It
is based on the opinions of Free Kinder-
garten teachers, district nurses and every
worker in the thankless vineyard of mean
streets.
Any approach to discipline, as the animal
is understood in barracks where there are
regulars, would have kept our young recruits
from asinine diversions. A wet canteen
under strict supervision might easily have
prevented the bloodshed in the late
uproar. Soldiers all the world over are
accustomed to have a reasonable amount of
liquor, but the war caught us on the hop,
before we had the military beer-mug in place.
Acute trouble with inexperienced young
men in khaki is only a passing phase. Civi-
lian Billjim, whose hard earnings foam over
in every city bar, is the person who wants
drying off. The woman’s view is that the
aforesaid toiler can drink quite as much as
is good for his health or his pocket by six
o’clock in the evening. Also, he can—if
he happens to be built like a tank —keep a
bottle or two of lager on his premises, just
to soak his thoughts in before retiring for
the night.
C aptain lirth, the State Governor’s new
young gentleman, has been pounced on by
our Second-best People. These are the
dressy crowd nowadays; and a Hero with
a bullet in his lung is just the fillip their
languishing spirits need in war-time. While
some our Best Suburbs are devoted to
Good M orks for the Cause, there remains
the frothy fringe which must have an A.D.C.
to play with.
A man who has much to say, from bitter
experience, of German prison camps is
harden S. Harvey. Just now he is with his
sister, Mrs. Percy Owen, recuperating from
his 16 months’ agony. She is a widow of
the late Bellambi (Wollongong) identity,
veais ago Mrs. Percy, when Miss Harvey,
was governess to the younger members of
the Owen family. Then she married the
widower and got on very happily with her
ex-pupils. Brother Carden is also in the
teaching trade. Before the war he pounded
English grammar into three of the Kaiser’s
sons. So he claims to know what the boss
enemy looks like both in peace and war.
Mrs. Kerr-Pearse, a relative of Lady Ede-
line Stiickland, had a closg call in the
lecent Tasmanian train smash. Fortunately
she escaped with nothing more distracting
than a shaking. Her husband is the Aide
who once supported Governor Strickland in
Hobart and Westralia. He is now in the
Fairgusons’ Help department.
The Darnleys’ only daughter, Dorothy
lg oh I s to one Daniel Peploe, of
the 20th Hussars. The girl’s mother was
Australian (her name was Morphy) who
married Ivo Bligh in the days when his
favorite recreation was cricket. Later of
course, Iwo decorated a seat in the House
of Lords (or should it be Lards?) as Earl
Darnley.
Australian Gaunt family has a knack
of getting to the fore when it’s worth while
being there. Captain Guy Gaunt R N
naval attache to the British Embassy at
U ashington, recently was persuaded to paint
hls sign. He’s a brother of
Jiteiaiy Mary, who has been a considerable
globe-trotter. The family luck found her
1 n m w . ar . broke out * so had
the chance of describing Ivan when he turned
ms back on vodka and moved south. An-
other brother, Admiral Ernest Gaunt, is the
Australian-born sailor to command a
division of the Royal Navy. Major C B
Gaunt, a fourth member of this strenuous
mcle, wears the gorgeous plumage of the
Dragoon Guards.
. p ercy Grainger, the S. Australian musi-
cian with the wavy, corn-colored mane, has set
the I\ew York musical critics purring
er „. a composition which he describes
as A Tribute to Foster, dished up
by Percy Grainger.” The orchestral
effects are colored by wet finger bowls
wine glasses and similar trick “instruments ’’
Instructions to players are conveved thus •
Louder ; bumpingly; chippy. Seems as if
Percy has side-tracked the classic and joined
the circus. J
A former Hawkesbury College boy was
killed in France a while ago. He was Lieut.
Harold Woodford, fighting with a Berkshire
regiment. Woodford senior used to be
British Resident Commissioner for the Solo-
mon and other cannibal isles.
The Verbrugghen and his just-arrived wife
have been handing round the ices at two
afternoon parties. Friday’s gathering at
Farmer’s was accompanied by the growl of
thunder and a fine, swishing rain tumbled
through the adjacent guttering as the musi-
cal items were marshalled. Fiddler Jenny
Cullen, pianist Iris de C. Rego and the
hostess were amongst the entertainers. Also,
tenor Philip Wilson, who is now on the Con-
servatorium staff. The Englishman is acquir-
ing more temperament—l suppose it’s in the
Australian air, though I never saw iff—
since he first pitched his voice here-
abouts. Mrs. Verbrugghen takes the
hostess business seriously, and uncorked a
tactful greeting to her guests. Some
day she hopes to learn the names of the long
procession of strangers who welcomed her to
Sydney.
Mrs. Verbrugghen, wife of the musical
Henri, is an Englishwoman with a silky
manner and a sweet, chirrupy voice. She
sings in a graceful, drawing-room manner,
and expressed her feelings the other after-
noon in a ballad or two. The plump and
pleasing lady had some of her domestic gods
badly broken on the voyage from London.
The new Conservatorium lady fiuuler,
Jenny Cullen, is a slim, spectacled young
woman from Clasgie. She is labelled
late professor of the Athenaeum School of
Music in that city of factory chimney-
pots. The Scotch lass has a fine warm
temperament and puts plenty of color into
her fiddling. She handles her bow with
the firm grip of a man. The importation
belongs to the Verbrugghen string quar-
tette.
E. E. Seton, a North Sydney man, recently
took possession of his best girl at an evening
ceremony in St. James’s. He is a great-
grandson of the late Lord Sidmouth, a one-
time Speaker of the House of Commons. The
bride was Madge Fitzhardinge, of Cremorne.
It was one of the quiet weddings of war-
time, and only relatives of the leading charac-
ters were asked to drink their health.
Cheques dropped in as light-ballast to the
usual fish-knife display.
Re wedding presents. Have you noticed
the slump in those old favorites, silver entree
dishes? Clocks are the mania of the moment.
When the modern bride settles down in her
new home she has about one timepiece and a
half for each room. In case these all stop
work a few wristlet watches help her
through.
The Benevolent Society had its annual
day out —in a huge marquee—last week.
A terrific thunderstorm put a wet tail to
the proceedings. An hour of preliminary
thunder didn’t stir the various speakers to
cut short their remarks; so the feminine
portion of the company fidgeted under an
acute presentiment of ruined millinery.
Governor Strickland took the best chair.
I resident Neville Mayman ran over the
debit and credit accounts ; and Chief Secre-
tary George Black replied warily to hints
from the committee for favors to come
Lnder the president’s tactful allusions
to the _ C.S. s “deep culture and broad
sympathies’’ Mr. Black’s smile was ex-
pansive. Mrs. Trefle, rarely seen in
public nowadays, was seated' near Mrs
Mayman amongst the elect. Dr. Fore-
nian explained the ethics of getting money
ci rom Government). Prof.
nd\ Stuait handed a bouquet to the presi-
dent as the man who had raised £6OOO for
the society. A parson or two gave the
final blessing. Then the heavens cracked
in several places and the company fled
through the rain.
A daughter of the late manager Vindin. of
the A.J.S. Bank, stepped fearlessly up to the
altar with .1. D. Sanderson the other dav.
Bride Maud s plumage w-s the orthodox
white. St. James’s Church. Turramurra,
was a-bloom with flowers for the event. Gor-
geous vegetation turned Ivuladubali, in the
same locality, into an exotic nest. Uncle
and auntie Vindin were the hosts. The
groom is a son of A. A. Sanderson, Combad-
ery station, in the AValgett sheep area.
~ -^ ie , ren ch Music Society’s concert, the
first of its kind in Sydney, liad a tropic night
for its musical fireworks at St. James’s Hall.
Ccntiary to our allies’ hopes the Conserva-
torium Hall was not available. Neither was
!ts director. Verbrugghen, in the final effort
of rounding up his flock for the first term in
the Botanic Gardens Academy, was unable to
lend any aid. He is anxious that the
blench community should clearly understand
that time the Wretch ! —pushed him out of

French chamber music is essentially mod-
ern. Some of it is full of weird tricks. For
this reason it has to be handled with care.
Debussy, brilliantly interpreted by Yvonne
( harvin. was the best offering on the pro-
gramme, though Debussy sometimes writes
smashing chords that arc not harmony. A
string quartet, with Staell as chief fiddler,
dragged. Society yawned furtively and
fanned itself with desperate vigor during
this long-drawn item. The Belgian singer
Lilliane Lore, a colatura soprano, was too
cold and academic in the trio of 18th century
French songs. Arthur Appleby was the male
vocalist. The William Cullens, the Ilolmans,
G. Karp and his lady, and the French Consul
Chayet bustled into the front pew in time for
the opening number.
The lace overskirt has come back for even-
ing finery. The Belgian soprano looked
like a fashion-plate of the ’Bo’s in one of
these frothy accessories the other night. The
white lace was rucked up on each hip, thence
it fell in ample lines to the hem of a lilac
understudy. There’s economy in this mode.
A veteran theatre frock would hide its
threadbare age under the graceful, lacy
upper terrace.
Dr. Worrall took an opportunity to urge
the necessity of getting a slice of the
Domain for increasing the area of Sydney
Hospital, at the recent annual plaint for
more cash. The public is likely to arch its
back if the frequent cuts at its green areas
continue.
The firemen and police carnival on Satur-
day was a huge success from an attendance
standpoint. Kings and queens were going
cheap. You could have bought them by the
gross for a pound or so in the big nroces-
sion. This wormed its way through gaping
crowds before noon. Then business com-
menced in earnest in the Agricultural
Grounds. Flora tius Johnston won the
Roman vehicle race. Did the early bush-
ranger advertise his profession in a flaming
red shirt? Anyhow, he was like a parti-
colored scarlet flamingo in the “sticking up”
incident at the carnival. Governor Strick-
land brought a couple of daughters to see the
events, which, as usual, included a swarm
of dancing youngsters in sweltering plaids.
About £7OOO will go to the clamorous hos-
pitals as the result of the show.
A Muswellbrook girl, Lilian Maynard, was
quietly wed to engineer Egerton Dennys the
other day. The bride wore her travelling
garb of biscuit-colored finery. The groom is
an Englishman, and his gifts to his bride
were something to inspire the devil of Envy.
Muswellbrook damsels will bear me out.
Lilian’s late uncle, Edwin Daintry, was a
well-known Sydney lawyer. The Egerton
Dennys doves are going to make their nest
in this harassed city.
Major d’Apice, who got a D.S.O. a while
ago, was in the S’African squabble with
N.S.W. Mounted Rifles. Now he is a bri-
gade-major somewhere at the Front, after
coming through the Dardanelles racket. The
d’Apice family was Italian and settled at
Hunter’s Hill years ago. The soldier’s
brother is on the Strickland staff.
Surgeon Herschel Harris, the Sydney man
who has done such brilliant work in France,
has been recuperating in a London hospital.
llis hands suffered severely in the blizzards
and frost of a Flanders winter.
The pretty Fred Dempster dame, from
Cowra, has deposited her wardrobe, motor-
car and husband at the newly-stocked T.
and G. flats. The lady carries with her
a a air of Rotten Row and Devonshire house
parties, but the English suggestion is merely
accidental. She originated in Westralia, and
her talent for making friends is entirely
Australian. , Her frocking genius belongs
to no territory, but is hers alone, and the
copyrights are reserved.
The Oliver Osbornes, of Jugiong, have
their newest she-babe Daisie
Willis, after Auntie Parberry. The infant
inhaled its first dawn at grandmother
Macfarlanes at Double Bay, and there was
much rejoicing when it took the rosy draught
iu spite of its sex and war-time theories
about the defender in the cradle.
After long months in warring lands, Clare
Gilchrist has come back, crammed with in-
formation, to her jumping-off place in
Macleay-street, Darlinghurst. In her smart
London-mades she looks as well as if she
had never left Sydney, and had remained
wholly ignorant of the feminine Sufferings
in Cano before the stern military command
drove the strayed doves back to* the home
fog.

uoman feel that she is going to be extin-
gmshed. A long coat with a wide flare, edged
S h f y nns th y .top terrace. Then comes

i, W h ls , e eet iler three-inch heels
will be like a lone herring in a barrel.

Numbers of Australian Nightingales have
been mentioned in despatches, a belated re-
cognition of their heroism in the early hor-
rors of the Flanders invasion. Miss Rear
and Sister Ada Gabriel, both Victorians are
included in a recent communique.
In the days when Australian bird-men were
regarded as freak-fliers who risked their
bones in public places, aviator “Billy”
Hart was in Class A. Now he’s off to soar
on active service. A send-off at the Aus-
tralia cheered him on his way.
The Women’s Horticultural and Home
Industries dames at a meeting the other day
balked at a suggestion that the Guild should
have an exhibit at the Easter Royal Show.
Yet the idea is good, and individual share-
holders are being given an opportunity of
saying “yes” or “no” to it. If a few mem-
bers of this long-winded Guild could prove
that they had only grown some carrots or a
high-class cabbage or two, the public might
begin to take an interest in its interminable
resolutions.
Onslow, son of a former M.L. Gov-
ernor, has recently been mentioned in des-
patches for his work in the Intelligence
Corps. He is still on the sunny side of 40,
and a gift of languages from Arabic to Rus-
sian has aided him to prominent notice
where that article is most useful.
Mrs. Florence Cooper, a Sydney woman, is
getting her breath in London after 10
months’ work in a Belgian hospital.
By the Rotorua, this week, Mrs. Pyne
( M.L.) and a brace of daughters go to chase
health in the danger zone. The party in-
cludes Phyllis Boyle, a daughter of that
Sandy Boyle who w r as racing and wool part-
ner with the late F. A. Pyne. Miss Boyle
goes to greet brother David, R.N., one of
the handy-men attending to the pursuit of
the baby-killers when the Gerhog guns tore
holes in Maoriland’s “gift” warship’s ensign.
He was officially noticed or recommended or
applauded at the time.
Sybil Macarthur Onslow, who recently
forsook her orchard and account books at
Camden for a few days’ gadding at Govern-
ment House with the Ferguson Exes., wafted
into Sydney in time to say “good boy”
to brother Jim on his return to home and
politics. Miss Onslow is very much the
man of the family just now, when almost all
the male members of the clan are out Hun
hunting, and she keeps her end up.
Millinery is suffering from an end-of-the-
summer expression. It wilts over one eye
ir it chances to be a last panama of the sea-
son. Or it surges iu front, under tall bows
that have a struck-by-poverty effect. The
buckle on all the-high black hats is'an obses-
sion. Nearly every woman’s headgear has
this decoration. it may be classed as a
necessary nuisance, twisted into black silk.
In May we are to have a Kiddies’ Exhibi-
tion in the Town Hall. This is not to be a
display of prize babies, but an effort to
show’ what modern pedagogues do for the
Y r oung Idea. John Mackay took the chair
at the preliminary meeting.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, February 28, 1910.
My Dear Yarrie, —
After finishing a round of country visits
to fill in time, the Governors have now taken
to visiting each other. State Ex. Stanley,
with Victor Hood as his Man Friday, has
led off by going to stay with the Fergusons
while the Stanley lady is away at Macedon.
The G.-G. handed out the prizes at Mel-
bourne Grammar School on Friday last, and
her Ex. came along wearing a happy smile
and royal blue silk draperies, topped off with
a. biscuit-colored toque and a nodding plume.
The Governor-General was surprised when,
instead of the usual silver and morocco
trifles, he was led to a mass of cardboard
strips. It appears the prize-money had
been patriotically pooled by the boys. They
howled and stamped with joy when the
potentate promised a silver cup for next
year’s sports meeting.
Doris Blackwood is very critical these days
about the appearance of the silver on the
vice-regal dining-table. She is gaining ex-
perience of domestic affairs daily, for she
helps to wash up plates and polish spoons
and forks at the lied Cross Rest Home on
St. Kilda-road.
Caulfield drew a great crowd on Saturday
in spite of the patriotic emptiness of the
vice-regal crate and the absence of many
who would throw themselves under a tram
if an Excellency went first. The John
Maddens did not appear, but the Frank
Madden dame came out with a spreading
crinoline-effect of black glace. One daughter
was an exclamation mark in purple. The
It. G. Caseys did a sprightly birdcage walk
after the success of Maharajah in the big
race. Redfern, the William Cooper knight’s
importation, went to the starting-post with
an illicit leg; but the Mills of Maffra had
very optimistic looks for Traquette. Their
hopes, were shortened by two lengths. Mrs.
Y\ iddis (owner of Patrobas) inhabited white
linen. The Clive Leonards rejoiced suit-
ably when Queen’s Challenger easily won
the crowded Stanley Steeplechase. Mrs.
Clive smiled in checked tailorings. Her
sister, Miss Reynolds, was smartly groomed
in khaki. Mrs. George Woodforde wore
heavy khaki silk, while Mrs. Thompson
hid inside a brown race-coat. Mrs. Norman
Wilson showed a dainty white cloth cos-
tume cut in real military style. Mrs. Sam
Mackay’s tussore was side-pleated to within
an ace of crinolining. Sir Walter James,
a well-matured Englishman who is careful
about his dressing, strolled out from the
city tavern which houses him to see what
Australian races are like. He seemed to
be favorably impressed. Mrs. Cecil Fisken
made a swirl of interest with a load of
ermine fur. It was a close day, but she
bore up. A grey-haired squatter brought
five girls; he escorted his flock to tea and
sat them in a grandstand row for
inspection. They wore wonderful hats,
and one fair one had a shot-silk dress the
color of the sea. Another wore peach blos-
som in her smart little toque. The third
held up a wide coon hat of striped blues,
and the fourth wore smart black roses.
Number Five was in a dream of plain white
cloth, hat and all. They were a joyous rain-
bow. One of the newest brides (thev are
so numerous now that they need identifica-
tion labels) did the lawn with a pink pro-
peller whizzing in front of her little brown
hat, while the breezes blew her champagne
frills about.
Ashmead Bartlett had a packed house for
his first lecture. The front rows were very
polite and distant and quite beautifully
upholstered. The back rows wore their rainy-
day clothes and a most earnest air. for they
had to strain their ears to follow the modest
mumblings of the lecturer, who seemed to
confide his best bits to his maps and to fling
ur.essentials over his shoulder to the audi-
ence. Points of great interest to Austra-
lians were hurried tl/ough casually, while
others, dear to an English heart, were
heavily emphasised. He seemed struck by
the fact that General Birdwood was forced,
by want of room, to live in a common dug-
out during the whole of his nine months’
stay on the Peninsula. “And he a General in
command of 50,000 men!” exclaimed the
lecturer. But the Australian audience took
ic quite calmly. Even a field-marshal in a
dugout wouldn’t have excited them.
Bartlett said he longs for the time when
the wonderful work of the British submarines
may be written freely. He casually men-
tioned that the rifle periscope, devised by
an Australian, proved a boon in Gallipoli
and has been sent along to the stormy West
Front. The fascination that new clothes
have for the Turk was touched upon. Usu-
ally poor and underclad, the artless infidel
thinks his new kakhi uniform a glorious pos-
session.
Mrs. James invited a party to meet Ash-
mead Bartlett at the Overseas Club, and
carefully saw to it that a number of military
men attended so that the guest wouldn't feel
lonely. General Stanley and Drs. Cus-
caden and Springthorpe sat with Alex.
McKinley and other newspaper men. Every-
body waited until the works nearly fell out
of the clock. Then some pleaded other
engagements and fled to keep them. The
guest at last arrived breathless in charge of
manager Tait, and found the Hennessy dame
and Mrs. James still waiting to receive them.
The break in the procession of events was
filled with tea and bun and sweet tenor sing-
ing by Mr. Brooks, accompanied by Banana-
land pianist Cahill.
Our Colonel Sellheim of yesterday—or the
day before —is Brigadier-General Sellheim.
He was originally “lent” by England to
Australia and soldiered first at Brisbane
and afterwards at Melbourne. He is cam-
paigning in sandy wastes now and gaining
a lot of popularity amongst our fighting
fellow-countrymen.
While Colonel Moon is away in France
directing his fleets of Australian motor
transport his wife is grass-widowing at
Sandgate (near Brisbane) with her father,
who is in his 90th year and about to build
a new house. That shows you how tired
people get in the North.
Mrs. Walter Tunbridge, whose colonel-
husband is whirling ammunition through and
across France, lately found her own house
too large to grass-widow in and rented a
Toorak bungalow for a few months. She
hopes to pilgrimage nearer the fighting-lines
some time in the future.
The Naval College Halls were recently
farewelled by Toorak at a tea-and-caking
hostessed by Mrs. Adams. Daughter Muriel
almost shed a tear at parting. It seems
that it is not Australia that Mrs. Hall would
like to use her maternal slipper on, but only
the Labor party and the Naval Board. The
rest of the continent would be given a free
pardon. Laborites and Board members will
be painfully interested to hear that a sooth-
sayer or witch-doctor has predicted the re-
turn of the family in two years’ time.
Percy Chataway, a young journalist who
was put out of action three weeks after the
Gallipoli landing, has been made as strong
as ever by skilful doctoring and nursing.
Instead of receiving his discharge in Eng-
land as anticipated, he was sent back to
Egypt, and there encountered younger
brother George wearing the stars of a
lieutenant.
Frank Ham, the rich young bachelor who
went to England to offer himself for active
service, died recently of laryngitis.
_ Mrs. James (wife of the Major) has been
visiting Mrs. Sydney Pickles (mother of the
aviator) at her house at Leura. in the Blue
Mountains (N.S.W.). Son Sydney is to
receive his captaincy soon and take com-
mand of a flock of Australian airmen in
England. He has had a long and painful
time nursing his injured leg, and spent a
good deal of the vacation on top of London
motor-’buses. The drivers at last got to
know him and used to call from passing jug-
gernauts, “This way to ’Endon !”
One more Hobart girl has gone to be the
guiding star of a fighting sailor. Her name
is Curran, and she is now on her way
to London, where one Walsh, of the Aus-
tralia, is waiting with the ring.
The Hoad widow has gone down to old,
familiar Queenscliff for a rest after her war-
time exertions. Her late husband did a lot
of peaceful soldiering there.
The feverish rush and whirl of Adelaide:
The Russell-Henderson wedding duly occurred at
All Souls’ Church, St. Peter’s, with a limited list
of guests. The bridegroom is one of the Frank
Russells of Salisbury, and a nephew of the implac-
able being who sees that we pay our taxes, while she
is the daughter of Mrs. Hall Henderson, of Tin-
tinara. The aftermath of tea and toasts was held
at Mrs. Koepen Wendt’s.
The work of providing comforts for the men in
the trenches is slowly forging ahead. Mrs. Lendon
was responsible for a meeting at which it was
decided that doctors’ wives throughout the State
and next-of-kin of the unit should look after the
A.M.C. The same busy worker is arranging a
Button Day for the Comforts Fund for the whole
of the S. Australian forces. The cash value of
patriotism was ascertained at Walkerville the other
evening when a Continental in Mr. McDonald’s gar-
den swelled a motor-ambulance fund.
Dr. H. A. Powell, who has been mentioned in
dispatches for his work at the Dardanelles, has been
promoted Lieutenant-Colonel. He left here at the
end of 1914 and has been in many troubles, includ-
ing the Anzae evacuation. When the last despatch
left he was in charge of the Ist Australian Station-
ary Hospital at Tel-el-Kebir.
The threatened paper shortage is stirring up in-
terest in the possibilities of local industry. The
Minister of Industry has received from the Director
of Chemistry samples of paper made from marine
fibre, and if the Government Printer approves of
them there seems a chance that local mills may
come into being. That, of course, is excellent.
What we literary girls want is unlimited paper to be
clever on. At present lam reduced to blotting-
paper or something like it.
Miss Earl, who had the hick to be in Germany at
the outbreak of the war, described some of her experi-
ences at the May Club as the guest of Miss Boothby.
It took her eight days to cover the ground between
Frankfurt and London, and she will never be hard up
for something to hold the floor with.
The marriage of Captain H. C. Nott to Marjorie
Lowe took place on Tuesday, in the St. Peter’s Col-
lege Chapel. Captain Nott is on a month’s fur-
lough, and the fortnight between now and the end
of his leave is to be spent in Melbourne. The
bride, who is one of our smartest girls, has a brother
in Flanders.
Mr. R. G. Nesbit, who has been appointed to the
presidency of the Licensing Court, is the eldest son
of our Paris. He is an old Prince Alfred College
boy, and from there went to the Adelaide Univer-
sity, being admitted to the Bar in 1902.
The Adelaide delegates to the Y.W.C.A. Conference
who were in the Tasmanian railway smash, got off
with a shaking—probably because they came from a
good place.
The will of R. Barr Smith, of Adelaide, has been
probated at £1,799,500. After providing for charit-
able bequests and personal gifts there is a trifle
left over of about a million and threequarters. This
goes to help the widow and children wrestle with
the cost-of-living problem.
Old Judge Boucaut’s £30,000 mostly goes to his
numerous sons, but there is a bequest of 20 guineas
for—of all unlikely purposes—the repair of West-
minster Abbey!
Esther, the ruddy-haired daughter of the
Charlie Fairbairns, has gone to join her
mother in Egypt. Brother Jim accompanied
as general luggage-shifter. His job should
be a busy one, for Esther took just as many
Saratogas as the Medina could find space
for after the coal bunkers were filled.
“Tommy'’ Reynolds, who married “The
Girl in the Taxi,” has given an aeroplane to
the Australian forces. The donor is living
snugly at Cliveden with his wife and their
new baby.
Mrs. Ambrose Pratt, having come back
to Melbourne, knocked at the door of her
Melbourne Mansions flat, but found her ten-
ants wanted it for several more weeks. To
oblige them she is camping in the flat vacated
by the Hoad widow. The hardships are
terrible.
Mrs. Sam Howell, killed in the recent
railway-smash at Campania (Tas.), was a
daughter of warehouseman David Beath, of
London and Melbourne. Sam Howell, who
went with his wife on the Hobart trip and
was considerably knocked about during the
derailment, owns Chocolyn, near Camper-
down, in the Western District. Raith, how-
ever, the Howell dwelling at Herne Hill,
Geelong, had been the family home for years.
Beath, the elder, in London, had booked his
passage for an Australian trip just before
the tragedy.
Edward Cunningham, a trifle weary in
Liberal well-doing, has temporarily given up
trying to settle the striker and stun the
fiscal issue with Argus leaders, and has
retired for a few weeks to Portsea.
Esther Fairbairn bandaged her last patient
at St. Vincent’s on Tuesday last, just before
she sailed for London by the Medina. All
the nurses are sad about it, but mother
wanted her.
Carmen Harris, who is about to sail to
Vladivostock to join her husband, endured
the appendicitis operation the other day. At
present she is looking as picturesque -as pos-
sible in a private hospital.
Dorothy Langridge said firmly and sol-
emnly last week that she was willing to
love, honor, and obey Charles Gerald
Hack. It happened at St. John’s Toorak,
and now they are honeymooning in Sydney.
The click of the needles is heard no more
at Menzies’. Julius Knight has closed
up his knitting bag, stowed away his
machinery and moved on to Sydney. Lord
bless him !
Women owners of speedy horses are be-
coming quite common. Just now Mrs. Dick
Hawker, who hails from Adelaide, is enjoy-
ing the sensation of owning a winner
(Cyklon) of one of Caulfield's first autumn
gallops.
Douglas Mawson’s wife has gone to
Sydney to meet her sister. Dr. Rika, who is
returning to Australia by way of America.
Mrs. Falkiner, who died some weeks
ago at the St. Kilda-road home of her
daughter, Airs. Fred Knight, was the mother
of the Falkiner racing men. She had many
relations —including numerous nieces and
nephews and grand-children—and she cut up
her money very generously amongst them.
The real business head of the partnership
with her long-since-departed husband, she
watched the station accounts and the expen-
diture while her husband put in most of
his time in the stables with his horses. The
sons nearly all inherited his sporting ten-
dencies, and the nearest local railway station
to the old homestead would occasionally be
asked on the ’phone to hold the train for
three minutes for one of the sons of the
house, then steeplechasing gaily across the
paddocks on one of the galloping machines.
At St. Ives, Malvern, the auctioneer took
the cash the other day for the household
effects left by the late Mrs. Rosetta Fox,
who died a little while before E. Phillips F.!
her brilliant painter-son. It was a quaint
assortment of goods, two conspicuous items
being a library of Hebrew works and another
of classical music. The Phillips Fox exhi-
bition, now on at the Athenaeum, and
arranged by his widow, is worth seeing.
The Frank Walkers raked in another 50
pounds at Ringwood by means of a moonlit
garden party. All the stalls were sold out
by closing time, and while the she-brigands
were operating. Morris dances and Maypole
prancings by children soothed the victims
Claud Hall tenored sentimentally in the
moonlight, and Beatrice Higginson and the
Janson lady trilled pleasingly.
The Returned Soldiers’ Association .fives
its first annual concert on Friday (March 3)
the arrangement of the programme being the
job of Maggie Sterling and others of the prin-
cipal musical pros. This show is to help
towards the Fallen Comrades’ Memorial
Hall, and the vice-regal Fergusons have pro-
mised their presence as well as their patron-
age.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
REASON FOR LENIENCY.
DEFENDANT (to magistrate who has just issued a maintenance order against him):
"Will your Worship kindly treat me as a first offender?”
“Y\ arre” : Has any fencer tried putting the
small end of split posts (which are better than
round posts) into the ground ? Have heard it
asserted that this method will increase the longevity
by 50 per cent. It looks feasible, because the big
end would act as a kind of roof, throwing off the
rain and dust, which would otherwise collect and
help to rot the wood.
“Bad Australian”: Away back about the
time the early Bulletin Chow arrived in Australia
I was kangaroo shooting in the Strathbogie Ranges
(Vic.), and one day I sniped a full-grown “black”
wallaby which was spotted worse than the pard. (I
never saw a pard, but this wallably could have given
him points.) The spots were perfectly white and ranged
from the size of a threepenny and up to that of the
change of the same coin in copper. Afterwards 1
killed thousands of the same species of marsupial, but
never another specimen with the native-cat markings,
although white faces and legs occurred occasionally,
and wallabies with a small white tip to the tail
were so numerous as to make it appear that the
white tip was the trade-mark of a distinct breed.
Now, about that spotted animal. Can anyone tell
me why or whaffor ?
* * ♦ *
“Moana”: Havelock North (M.L.) has
been indulging in a plague of eels. Pollution of
the Ngaruroro River has been the cause. Great
numbers of eels, many of them of immense size, have
been unable to get to the upper reaches of the river
as usual, so, to dodge certain death by poisoning,
they have bolted up the many creeks that run into
it, with the result that they blocked up these narrow
watercourses. Strawberry refused to cross the
streams or to go near them to drink for fear of
being swallowed by a water python, and the dairying
industry is threatening to die of thirst. When they
were last heard from the eels were starting to climb
about the landscape on each side of the creeks, and
as this cross-country job wasn’t agreeing with their
healths they were dying like flies and were decorating
the locality with a disagreeable, fishy aroma.
“J.”: Concerning that bush kiddy who
corrected headmaster Exley as to the family of Mrs.
Koala (B. 10/2/’l6). “P.F.” may be glad to hear that
Lucas and Le Souef, in their “Animals of Aus-
tralia” (1909), assert of the native bear, “The
female produces one cub.” So the kid was right.
* * * *
“S. S. Severn”: Talking of howling in
Papua. Going up the Tiveri River to the Lakekamu
in the old Bulldog was a load of boys signed on
for mining, amongst them being some women, one
with a dear, fat, honey-colored babe unclothed in a
string of beads. As we chunked sluggishly up the
river a native on the bank called out to Itow-bebe,
the mother of the babe, who thereupon lifted up
her howled. The other ladies joined in.
They put all their weight into the chorus, the re-
verberations whereof might have been heard in
Mars. “What is it ?” I asked. “That man been
say he see her brother,” they told me. “Him sick?”
I inquired. “No, him all right.” “What for she
cry?” “Him been say he see her brother,” they
reiterated. Apparently there was nothing the mat-
ter with her brother, “brother” being very often a
dimly distant cousin; the mere fact of a passer-by
having said he saw him being the raison d’etre
of this howling.
“Jaques ’: My old moke caught his fore-
leg in a barb-wire fence the other 'night and ripped
his hoof open from the fetlock to the shoe. The
tear is about three inches by one, and when he walks
the wound opens and shuts. I've tried bathing,
balsam, blasting and crystal-gazing, but without
any results that satisfy, the horse. Can any unem-
ployed veterinary surgeon tell me what next to avoid?
ANOTHER ARGUMENT FOR COMPULSION.
MISTRESS: “Your inferences seem very good, I must say.”
APPLICANT: “Sure. I always take fine care to get one to me liking before I leave the
house.”
“Barcoo”: “Itan” (B. 27/1/T6) is quite
correct as to the flying-fox being a particular pest
this season. This is noticeable because the drought
and consequent scarcity of wild fruit and berries
brings about a greater concentration of. num-
bers while foraging for a living. But “Itan”
is quite unreliable in saying that the foxes
migrate down the Australian coast from New
Guinea. The fruit-eating flying bat (Pteropodidce-
poliocephalus) is indigenous to the tropical coast of
Queensland and North Australia, and its breeding
grounds are in the dense scrubs in the tidal areas.
It is not generally known that there are four distinct
varieties of the Australian flying bat, and they are
all confused with the fruit pest. One of them is not
a fruit-eater at all, but lives entirely on the honey
of blossoms and pollen. This is a smaller bat than
the fruit destroyer, is scientifically distinguished as
Carponycteris minima, and is the most numerous
member of the family. It is black, with ashen
grey head and neck, whereas the black of the fruit
lavisher is relieved by a patch of brown on the back
of the neck. There is a smaller honey-eater, but it
is lare, and rarer still is the smaller fruit-eater with
yellow spots on the wings. The first-mentioned
honey-eater was much relished by the aborigines on
account of the fat and tender flesh, and white people
who know the difference in the species have no hesi-
tation in eating (particularly) the breast of the
honey-eater. Baked and converted into sandwiches

“Snell ’: The king simpleton has come to
ight. He was discovered digging postholes on the
common at Waddy, Vic., and charged with doing
deliberate damage to public property. His name
was Shrews, and he looked like a sample specimen
fiom an asylum. “I jist wanted a job, V I
thought I d sink those postholes,” he said. “Were
jou asked to do the work?” demanded the Banch
with severity. “Oh, no,” said Shrews, “I ]U t

up before horses or cows broke their legs in them,
le filled up the holes as ordered, and his next
appearance in court was as prosecutor. He was suing
the magistrate for his wages.
“I linderino” : On the beach at one of the
Palm Islands (N.Q.) there is a grave which is held
in respect by beche-de-mer fishermen and blackfellows
who occasionally camp on the island. Eight years
or so ago a New Guinea boy belonging to the crew
of a beche-de-mer schooner disobeyed some trifling
order given by the captain, a Japanese, who had him
tied by the hands and towed astern by way oi
punishment. On being pulled aboard later the un-
fortunate lad* lived only a few minutes. A course
was set for the Palm Islands and the body was buried
on the beach. A few days later, when the schooner
was anchored near Cape Grafton, some of the crew
cleared out in a dinghy, landed at the Yarrabah
Mission Station and reported the tragedy. Next day
a launch left Cairns with two armed police, and the
schooner was brought to Trinity Inlet with the cap-
tain under arrest. He was tried for murder, but
somehow escaped conviction. The schooner was seized
for debt and was sold to the Yarrabah Mission people,
who changed its name to Matubu, the name of the
drowned Papuan boy who sleeps at the Palm Islands.

“Musketoon”: I won’t dispute “F.C.’s”
contention (B. 13/1/’l6) that pine makes the best
posts for fencing, but a caution to him might not
come astray. That is, don’t use green pine posts.
Bill Wade made that mistake in fencing his farm at
Jacob’s Ladder, on the Yass-Tumut (N.S.W.) road.
Y ires were fixed in and sliprails arranged, when one
fine morning Bill discovered the fence had started to
grow. The pine posts struck root, and before
long all the cockies going through that run had to
get ladders every time they wanted to let down the
sliprails.

COMING HOME AT NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Darkness with her chilly fingers
Draws a sombre curtain round
Where the lonely wind, entangled,
Whimpers with a mournful sound ;
Harshly chafes the swollen river
Fretting on its gravel bars;
Coldly on the world beneath them
Stare the white, disdainful stars;
i'et a gleam on yonder hilltop
Makes the whole creation bright—
'Tis a signal in the window
When I’m coming home at night.
411 day long the team has battled,
Struggling through the sodden loam —
What a difference in the pulling
ft hen I turn their heads for home!
How they rush the slipp’ry rises,
How they rattle o’er the bridge,
See the willing shoulders straining
As we crest the final ridge!
There’s a something, strangely pleasant,
Makes the heavy going light,
And the miles fall off like feathers,
When we’re coming home at night.
Morning sets the birds a-warble,
But their music can’t compare
With the tinkle of the teacups
When there’s winter in the air.
Noontide’s glowing ardor coaxes
From the bee-enticing blooms
All their wealth of hoarded fragrance
Breathed in delicate perfumes;
But no flower that ever blossomed
Could waft odors that invite
Like the smell of dinner cooking
When I’m coming home at night.
All along the slushy roadways,
Like a vision on the gloom,
I can see the loved lips smiling
In the warm, familiar room,
See the laden table gleaming,
Hear the old clock’s dreamy tick,
Hear the coals .upon the hearthstone
Falling with a pleasant click,
While the golden flame above them
Dances like a jovial sprite—
All, the whole day’s choicest moment
Is the coming home at night!
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.


NERVOUS BREAKDOWN
MAY BE AVOIDED BY TIMELY USE OF
Hean’s Tonic Nerve Nuts.
MR. H. MARSDEN.
The wear and tear of modern business
life tends to deplete the nerve force of
the strongest folk. It’s WORRY that
does the harm, not WORK. Strain,
constant tension, the need of being
always on the alert against financial
disaster —these things sap vitality and
wear the nerves to a frazzle. The
power of throwing off anxiety is lost;
you “take the business home with you.”
Insomnia creeps stealthily on till it is a
habit, and the recuperative power of
sleep is lost. Then work itself becomes
a trouble, and nervous disorders “pile
up.”
Mr. Harry Marsden
known in sporting circles throughout
Australia as one of the “Famous Four,”

appreciation:

“N.R.”: To find north and south in
tropical latitudes when the sun is nearly vertical at
midday, mark on the ground the shadow of a fixed
point (the end of a bent twig stuck in the ground
will serve). Wait a quarter of an hour and have a
smoke or a bottle of iced beer. Then mark again
where the shadow of the aforementioned point falls.
This will be due east of the point first marked, or so
near it that the difference doesn’t matter. The
higher the point is above the ground, the longer, of
course, will the east-and-west line be.
“W.J.C.”: Passing along one of the old
coach roads out West recently I was impressed by
the tottering shells of one-time pubs whose frequency
bore eloquent testimony to the bygone drinking capa-
city of the baked inhabitants who are no more. I
passed along that road 15 years ago, and nearly all
the shanties were then in full curse—-I mean blast.
Now, when I look at the weather-beaten posts and
rafters I feel old and dry. Sometimes one comes
across a lonely grave adjacent to the ruins. Pro-
bably that one was trodden flat by beery hoofs
before the pub closed. That grave represents the
Last Drunk, the Final Jim-Jam, the Ultimate Fan-
tod. There are weeds on the sepulchre, and you
dream strange dreams if you camp thereabouts—
dreams of blue and green devils, and dogs that climb
trees tail first.
“Endezzer”: Any Bulletin reader like
to have the recipe for chicha de ma'iz, the booze of
Peru and Bolivia? First, collect the ingredients and
utensils. These are maize, an earthenware basin, and
half a dozen or more ugly old women, very dirty.
Seat them in a ring round the basin. Each oid
party takes a mouthful of maize, chews it and spits
the chewed mass into the basin. There it is left to
ferment. From this as a basis, with a few other
things suen as water and sugar, a charming drink is
made. No one with a maize patch and half a dozen
ugly old women on the premises need go thirsty.
* * * *
“Bad Australian”: Gidya may be all that
some Bulletin writers believe it to be, but for posts
gimme good, sound, medium-aged red-gum which
has been properly dried before putting into the
ground. I l-emember a fence which used to run
right across the Molcoan Swamp (Vic.). This fence
was erected some 40 years before I saw it, and many
of the red-gum posts were then as sound as a bell.
These posts had been subjected to as severe a test as
any timber is likely to be, as in rainy seasons the
fence was submerged and at other times the atmo-
sphere was as dry as a wowser’s christening at Can-
berra. I have also seen red-box and ironbarlc taken
out of the ground in a perfectly sound condition 27
years after being put there. But much, if not
everything, depends on the age of the tree and
whether it has been grown slowly or quickly. You
can grow two entirely different classes of timber
from the seed of any given eucalyptus, just by culti-
vating one specimen under natural rainfall conditions
and the other under irrigation, which is, in a way,
an unnatural condition. The difference in the
specific gravity and quality of the timber in these
circumstances is simply astounding.

“S.S.S.”: In Moviani village, New
Guinea, I saw a kikeni (girl) sitting cross-legged
on the platform of a house with an old savage
kneeling before her sticking flowers in her hair.
He studied the placing of each frangipani blossom
from every angle, and took nearly an hour in the
process. Neither he nor the lady took the slightest
heed of the white looker-on. The kikeni’s breast and
face were coated with oily-looking red clay—this
denoting that she was in the marriage market. The
ancient artist was her he-parent decorating her in
broad daylight in full view of the village. The
Papuan parent sells his girls to the highest bidder.
How much does he want for her? I asked. They
gave a list as long as my sins of armshells, sapi-
sapi feathers and trade. I worked it out that the
old boy valued his daughter at about 60 pounds
sterling.
* * * *
“John P.”: Here’s a shearing tally that
was put up by six men at Coronet Peak station
(M L.) recently. In nine hours they whipped the
wool off 1296 jumbucks. The best score on the board
was 239 and the average 216. It isn’t a world’s
record, but it is a mighty fine, even performance.
“Barcoo” : The greatest flight of birds to
be witnessed in Australia is at the rare intervals
when the northern portions of Queensland are
visited by the “flock pigeon”—so named for an
obvious reason. The flights are made from Celebes
and other large islands of the Malay Archipelago.
The male birjl is of a brown-slate color, with a few
very brilliant green- and pink-hued feathers on the
back of head and neck. The female is minus the
gaudy clothing and is of a dull drab-slate. These
birds are undoubtedly identical with the wild or
passenger pigeon “Columboe” (Ectopistes migra-
torius) of North America, and are most impetuous
and swift fliers, capable of sustained flight over enor-
mous distances. Specimens killed and examined
have been found to have their crops full of rice
from fields quite 1500 miles away. One Wilson,
an authority on wild-bird life, estimated a flight
in Manitoba a few years ago at 22 millions of birds.
Queensland has had two visits in 20 years, and the
% isitants came in only on the huge open spaces of
the Mitchell grass downs, as far south as the Barcoo
and Thompson rivers. Further north, in the Terri-
tory, the visitations are more frequent, although not
in such large numbers. The birds seem to be con-
tinuously on the wing and even drink while flying
just a couple of swoops at the surface of the water
and up again. The flesh has a splendid game
flavor, but requires more cooking than practically
any other game. It is hard to reason out their
countless numbers, as their egg-laying competition is
two at a time.
‘‘Mulga”: In a recent Bulletin mention
was made of the long distances travelled by a horse
in a day, but I would like to record the distance
covered on foot by a bushman. While shearing at
Talavera station, four miles out from Turat (Q.),
back in the ’9o’s, one of the jumbuck barbers,
Watty Robertson, regularly every Saturday after
dinner, took a constitutional into Yeulba to see his
fiancSe, and was back for blow-up time on Monday
morning. The total distance would be between 90
and 100 miles. Another time, the coach breaking
down just after leaving Turat, Watty started to ped
it to Yeulba, and was quietly quenching his thirst
there when the coach arrived.
* * * *
“Jim Carraway” : The liuthergleu fly pest
is giving orchardists and others a dose of trouble
in the irrigation latitudes served by the Waranga
basin channels (Vic.). The fly lights in myriads on
fruit, tree and plant, causing them to wilt and
wither. It is a cross between the smallest house-fly
and the mosquito. Smoking with tar fumes is the
only antidote, and this merely moves the pest on to
the next fellow s orchard j so that you are only
swapping consignments. Like the locust, the Ruther-
glen affliction happens or.ee in 10 years or so, but
it creates enough damage when it does come to be
remembered till the next time. The deadly night-
shade plant, detested by most insect pests, proves an
enjoyable titbit to the Rutherglen insect, which has
even been known to attack children when other
provender was lacking.

BLACK FISH: A SWAGGIE’S SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The long day’s over, the journey’s at an end,
I’m thinking to stay here by the river’s bend.
How can I fare better wherever I may tramp?
It’s all among the wattles I’ll fix my camp.
I’ve three black fish
On a hook;
I’ve three johnny-cokes
I must cook;
w Three little dilly-bags
Feel quite plump;
And the dashed old flour-bag
Dangles on a stump.
There’s no more worry, there’s no more wandering,
I 11 roll out the bluey as happy as a king.
It s cool here and pleasant, the journey’s at an end—
Ail among the wattles, by the river’s bend.
Three little black fish
Just been hooked;
Three little johnny-cakes
Nearly cooked;
Three little dilly-bags
Feeling plump;
And the dashed old flour-bag
Dangling on a stump!
VICTORIA.
LOUIS ESSON.
“Eddyson”: The climax of Smibert’s
adventure was witnessed in the police court. Smi-
bert commemorated his birthday in the usual way
that is to say, he filled himself to the neck with
cheap gin, gave three cheers for “Olengland,” and
curled himself up in the pub yard behind the dog
kennel. Duff, the baker in the adjoining premises
was having a new oven built in his yard, and pre-
sently six convivial soldiers took Smibert up and
deposited hint carefully in the brick oven. Smibert
awoke at the grey dawn, and after taking careful
note of his surroundings went clamoring for more
gin. He prowled round and round the pub like
an impassioned cat. He said he could come in
through the keyhole and take the inflamed gin if
he wished ; but he was desirous of acting as a square
and honorable ghost, so he threw a half-crown
through a ten-shilling window, and the publican
threw a bottle of gin back at him. On recovering
from the impact Smibert went back to his oven with
a series of blood trickles emanating from the dent
in his head and finishing in his boots. In the
morning Duff, the baker, found Smibert in his oven
consorting with gin, and a most interesting dis-
putation ensued, Duff wishing to remove Smibert
and Smibert resisting. Duff contended that Smibert
was a crapulous intruder and a disturber of Christian
peace; and Smibert wanted to know “If a man
ain’ tav bitter peace when he’s dead, when is he tav
bitter peace?” Smibert then quoted something
from the poets about being “lef’ ’sleep in grave
where Bri'n has laid ’ini,” and threw his gin at
Duff. He forgot to remove the bottle before throw-
ing the gin, and after Duff was found by his wife
he was labelled “Urgent” and sent to the hospital.
Then Duff’s dog found Smibert in the oven, and
affected great indignation as an excuse for biting
him. On tasting Smibert the dog lost all self-
ecntrol, and dragged the man out of the oven, and
pulled him through the slaked lime, messing him
about a great deal, and opening his pores so wide
that he could no longer contain himself. Smibert
then went into the hotel bar, and, acting under the
delusion that as a spirit of the departed he could
not be seen, pinched a bottle of square-face. Smibert

The Inky Way.
Ted Colies writes :
It is in a dugout on the side of a hill in the
gully known as Old Headquarters. With me is Dave
Barker, a Sydney man with some art experience
from the U.S.A., and painter Frank Crozier, well
known in Melbourne art circles. We are working
away quietly and happily at the production of The
Anzac Magazine, written and illustrated solely by
our own troops serving in the Peninsula, and to be
printed on dinkum surface paper with numerous
color-plates. War-correspondent Bean is the editor-
o.c. and chief hustler -and barracker for the project,
with the enthusiastic moral support of the entire
Army Corps staff, from the G.O.C. down to ll.Q.’s
cook’s offsider. Bean’s dugout, by the way, is on the
terrace below us. At present it is occupied only by
his youthful and kindly-tyrannical batman-secretary,
who is mad because the boss is doing some—to him —
entirely unnecessary dangerous pedestrian exercise
from far Suvla Bay to Anzac, instead of travelling
by the comparatively safer sea-route. Bean has
already suffered much physical damage in
following up charges in the past, besides having
once walked four miles with a bullet in his thigh
and arriving “home” in the dark hours without
awaking anyone, spent the rest of the night in his
clothes and a deep sleep that wouldn’t be denied,
leaving his wound to be attended to in the morning.
We are now off the peninsula and this is being
written amidst the verdant volcanic-tinted hills of
Imbros, in a building known as the War-correspond-
ents’ Camp. It consists of three small rooms
one reserved for eating and ■ writing pur-
poses. The tenants are Bean, English Ward-
Price (of the eyeglass) and “Pere” Nevinson,
representing respectively the London and the Provin-
cial press. Ashmead Bartlett has just hurried off
“elsewhere”; also, there is Malcolm Ross, the
Maorilander; and Lester Laurence, Reuter s man.
The Australian tenant is very rarely at home. He
could get most details of movement and progress from
headquarters here and only venture forth occasion-
ally for big events ; but being an unnaturally energetic
person he is usually in the front line seeing the real
thing for himself. The other tenants are but a little
less conscientious, so that our family is rarely gath-
ered at home together. Our cooking is done by a
small, greasy man who confesses to having been born
in Constantinople, but swears he is of Greek descent.
Another member of our household is the incomparable
Maloney. He is a stray Irish soldier who became
attached as Bean’s orderly. Standing six feet or
more, he is a humorous, good-natured, very worldly-
wise Tommy, with a stock of droll but very dis-
reputable soldiering stories and reminiscences on
which the career of another Kipling might be built.
He showed me a newspaper cutting with a portrait
of his “ould mother” surrounded by pictures
of his brothers —all “regulars” from India and else-
where. John has lost an arm. Pat is minus one
eye. Mat has sacrificed a leg. And Ned is a prisoner
of war in Limburg. “Ever think of getting home to
see her again, Maloney?” I asked, and this remote
possibility seemed to tickle him. “See her agin!”
he exclaimed. “ ‘Faith,’ she’d say, ‘which av th
divils ar’ ye?’ ”
Nevinson is a lean, peevish old gentleman, in
immaculate attire, with a querulous, pedantic speech
and a keen appreciation of all little discomforts and
inconveniences. He would break any batman’s heart.
Ward-Price is a big-built young man, with dark,
heavy Semitic features, perfectly parted and polished
hair, a lowering monocle; but in him the English-
man’s well-bred peevishness is neutralised by a
certain ponderous reserve. His costume also is sug-
gestive of the melodrama war-correspondent. Lester
Laurence is a perky, plump, pink-faced little man.
terribly short-sighted and wearing glittering pince-
nez, not unlike the conventional stage-johnny at
times, but in attire less assuming and in other ways
a real decent chap. As also is Ross. The batmen
(a Munster, a Maorilander, an Australian, a Dorset
and an unclassified “Terrior”) have given their ruling
that Bean and Ross are bonzer blokes. So no more
need be said. I did not become acquainted with
Ashmead Bartlett; but he seems to be a “hard case”
in a “gentlemanly sorter way.” Mainly on account of
his keen-sighted ness into things, and too caustic
criticisms, he found it best to transfer his activity.
.**-**
“K.”: Ashmead Bartlett, having com-
plained that he had to submit his Sydney
lectures to the Censor, Willie Kelly,
M.H.R., promptly rushed into print and
observed that the Northcliffe press (Bart-
lett was a Times man) had “earned the
contempt of England” by its criticisms of
running the war in a state of muddle.
Which was very characteristic of Willie.
In about 15 years he will wake up to dis-
cover that the Times ancl the Daily Mail
were the only papers in London that had
the courage to bell about 75 calamitous oats
and persuade John Bull he really had m
hand a job of importance. The attitude
of the other papers was trust the Govern-
ment, and six months’ experience was
enough to prove that a Government, shel-
tered behind a censorship, could be trusted
with the greatest confidence to be short ot
guns, munitions and men right up to the
disastrous end. And when Northcliffe cut
in and took Government and Censor
by their respective throats gave
three cheers, and the other fellows rag
became a drug in the market.
G. It. Sims has put. up a record. London
Referee has issued its two-thousandth num-
ber, and every Sunday without a single
break throughout 40 years Sims has weighed
in with his three columns of bright and
breezy gossip under the heading of “Mus-
tard and Cress,” and over the signature of
“Dagon’et.” Of course he has turned out a
vast amount of other sorts of copy during
these four decades, as well as plays, panto-
mimes, stories and contributions to other
journals. Sims has trained himself to do
with four hours’ sleep. He can work away
until two o’clock in the morning and is
ready to start again at 7 or 8 a.m.
Hence the prodigious amount of copy he has
been able to put into print. It is announced
that early in January his reminiscences will
start in the Evening News, the Harms-
worth London halfpenny supplement to the
morning Daily Mail. Henry Sampson, the
founder and first editor of the Referee, is
gone, but Sims has still a couple of col-
leagues who were with him in the first num-
ber—-Richard Butler, the present editor, and
Henrv Newton, who supervises the theatri-
cal department. Newton recalls theatrical
London as it was when the Referee started,
and incidentally mentions an oIU friend oi
Australia’s—Harry Rickards.
* * * *
H. A. Conant, after 19 years as sub-
editor of the Catholic Press, has subsided
into the editorial chair of its holy and aged
contemporary, Sydney Freeman’s Journal.
He was a Bulletin contributor in the
“Bohemian” days of Sydney, can pen a
good yarn when in the humor, and occasion-
ally brews verse.
“Harrison O.” : Henry Lucy, the literary
Jeames, who spends so much time dining
with “a certain very distinguished states-
man.” earls, dukes and such like who are
“well known to be on terms of re-
spectful intimacy at Court,” and then
tells the S. M. Herald and other
high-class journals all about it, has
resigned his job on London Paunch.
As Lucy would say: “I am in a position to
state this definitely.” The cabled message
made it clear. Why will the people at the
other end of the cable persist in the delusion
that Australia is tremendously interested in
Paunch? Lucy’s chief work for the paper
was “Essence of Parliament”; and it was
1 dull. To read the “Essence” that used to be.
contributed by Shirley Brookes is to under-
stand how Punch once acquired a reputa-
tion for brightness; but the only parts of
Lucy’s pages that scintillated were E. T.
Reed’s caricatures; and Reed was dropped
years ago.
“Dalriada’s” note :
Succeeding Carl Ludwig Pinschof, who has retired
from Melb. Herald’s Board of directors, J. C. David-
son, editor-in-chief, has been appointed. “Davy,”
or “Jack,” as he is variously known, presumably
because his baptismal name is James, is 49, but
owing to his habit of keeping himself fit by means
of boxing, looks a hard 37. A Scotch-American,
Davidson was born in Ann Arbor, Michigan, and,
fulfilling promise of a pugnacious disposition, has
punched a strenuous way through life. He gained
his early newspaper experience on the Detroit Free
Press, where Robert Barr (“Luke Sharp”) was one
of the stars. Having in him the blood that made
his brother an explorer, Davidson could not rest
from travel, and when he came to Australia he
preferred the saddle to the desk. But Fate dragged
him from the steers and the brumbies, and handed
him a job as secretary to Bigjohn Forrest and as a
reporter on the West Australian. Then he joined
the Argus and left it for the Herald. On the
latter paper he succeeded W. T. Reay as editor five
or six years ago.

“Who’s Who” for once is unreliable. The
1916 edition describes Prime Minister W.
M. Hughes as “High Commissioner for
Australia since 1915.” Perhaps it was a
tip that didn’t come off.
* * ♦ *
“Fount”: Here’s my modest entry for a
Hard Day Competition. I lived through it
when news-gathering for the Brisbane
Courier and the Observer, under Editors
Ward and Morley. Up at 6 a.m. Did a
double execution at Boggo-road Gaol—one
column for the evening Observer. At 10,
Government rounds, including two deputa-
tions—another column. Meeting of credi-
tors in one of the largest businesses in
Bananaland at 2 p.m.—four columns (I
had a junior to help in transcribing). After
tea the first night of a big dramatic combi-
nation. Like most first nights everything
late, and 11.15 p.m. before I crawled up the
Courier stairs with a column and a half to
write about “The Second Mrs. Tanqueray.”
Just laid down my wobbling pen after 1
a.m. when a fire broke out in Red Hill. The
acute sub. (Barton) twigged it through the
windows of his room and caught me as I
was going yawning to the stairhead. A
cab, a rush, a few words with the Fire
Superintendent, a rush back in the cab, and
a quarter-column of serious fire at Red Hill
before the machines started clacking. Then
at last home to bed.
* * * *
The paper famine may have its compen-
sations. One of the chief anxieties of the
world is paper—cheap paper over which the
cheap soap advertisement sprawls and the
cheaper soapy article spreads its degenerate
slop. With a shortage of printed matter
there need be no real reduction of news.
A foolscap sheet would often contain ali
that is decently interesting or worthy of
note in the whole 16 pages of the double-
royal sausage wrap that befoozils the
public and cqnfuses its thinking. 'phe
number of daily or weekly papers on this
planet that might not with advantage be
cut down in size could be counted on the
fingers of one hand. Of course, a large
number of pressmen of ability and parts
might be urgently in need of a drink if the
cutting process went too far; but the gen-
eral mass of readers wouldn’t be greatly
hurt. The first effect of the diminished
press output would be to drive the crowd
to the libraries. Then the bookshop and the
second-hand bookstall would have their
show. Billjim would discover that books
which he might have read 20 years ago
gave him more to talk about than the lost
“final” of the Evening Bloodstain or the
departed “extra final” of the Splurge, if
the trouble lasted long enough the breaking
of the paper drought would be the most
remarkable'event in the history of the press
With a blare of trumpets and display of
black headlines the old matter would be
rushed in its former volume upon a new
world. And the public would hold the great
blanket of inanity at arm’s length and
snort its disdain. The calamity would be
permanent; for in the absence of the daily
irritant the public would have learned to
read.
“Copabella” moralises from behind the
counter
“Dry Shave” strikes the nail on the head (B.
3/l/’l6) when he says the Australian manufacturer
isn't keen for more trade. In spite of the iniquitous
Wages Board, the Socialistic unions, and capital
falling over itself to remain in the country, many a
manufacturer is doing so comfortably these days that
he smiles at the cry for more local manufactures.
His travellers tell us with pride that they cannot fill
all their orders. Invoices reach retailers with item
after item marked “making,” “to follow,” “tem-
porarily out of,” and so on. Some lines we wait
weeks for ; some we never get—in fact, I’ve a shrewd
suspicion the manufacturer doesn’t start to make
some things until he actually has the order for
them. We try other firms, and so fill our stock-lists
somehow'. Very healthy—apparently. But is it?
Let us examine from the retailers’ point of view—
which though he doesn’t know it, is the national
one, too.
There are a few Australian lines that may be
termed “national.” They are household words. Most
of them are on the drygoods side. I can recollect a
packet tea, a starch, a soap or two, two or three
cure-alls, a porridge oats, a milk—a dozen lines at
the most —I must stock ; because customers insist on
them and will not accept a substitute. They are
good goods, well advertised. (By the way, there are
a hundred imported lines in this category.) Offset
against these national ones there is a host of Aus-
tralian manufactures thsrt don’t count. Perhaps a
dozen little two-by-four firms are making the same
things in lean-to sheds decorating suburban back-
yards. Even if all the Australian firms making some
lines w’ent bung nobody would notice a gap. The
public never heard of their goods as Australian
specialties, and the retailer would always be able to
get an imported line to replace the goods. We re-
tailers sell the local products because they are cheap
or the profit is good, or because the traveller for
them is a sport with a lot of good stories under his
hat, or it’s the first brand offered us, or because Mrs.
So-and-So’s husband is foreman at the factory, or
because we were mates with the managing-director
at the Yackandandah rush back in the battling
days—never because the public demands them.
Take boots. I’ve sold a mountain of boots in my
time, but never yet (with one exception) has a cus-
tomer bowled up to me for a “Wedgwood” or a
“Highbrow” or a “MacFurphy” as they would for a
Yankee “Comstock” or “Partial.” (“Partials” are
now made, and sometimes demanded, in Australia,
but the demand was created when they carried
“imp.” after their name.) The exception was a hob-
nailed watertight very popular on the land and the
mining-field 10 or 12 years ago. I haven’t handled
it for years now, but even to-day an old shellback
asked for a pair. That maker, had he not been con-
tent to tap out a few pairs in a back room in some
mean street, might by now (and systematic pub-
licity) have scooped the whole Australian trade. The
stuff was right; the public was willing to buy, and
the retailer ready to sell it. It’s dead: as the other local
brands will die —and a Yankee competitor’s advertis-
ing manager will read the prayer over the grave! So
with hats. No one asks automatically for a local
Denton as he does for the Italian “Maraschino. ’ In
piece goods, what tailor offers Marrickville to the
hesitating customer as casually as he does “Boxs
serge”? How many buyers of “Laager” or “Japon
underwear ever heard of —now what is the name of
the Collingwood brand ? The Australian manufac-
turer is afraid to dare anything—a little overstock, a
few extra hands, a new machine. He will never be
accused of “dumping” on the other fellow’s market,
he never has anything over to dump. Some
of him will not even open up for the busi-
ness knocking at his door. Sometimes he is a pifflen
who doesn’t see beyond the end of his nose,
who doesn’t try to make himself count; and if the
job of making Australia a manufacturing nation ha
to be left to him we’d never get beyond the
stage of the village cobbler and the roadside smit y
and the red-nosed saddler travelling round from farm
to farm.
The stereotyped excuse (when it isn’t the u»ua
“We are a producing, not a manufacturing, coun
try”) is that Australian wages render world-compe 1
tion an impossibility. We’ve not got as far as "or
competition yet. We haven’t supplied Australia. n
fact, we omit to supply the little one-horse store ll
the Backblocks with all the boots and unders 11
and softsoap and currants and salacetic table s n
wants! I never heard chat particularly low
were paid in U.S.A., anyhow—so we should be a
to make nutmegs and raspberry jam-seeds and c 1C
food and fly-swatters against the world.
There’s an up-to-the-minute instance of o
“push” in mustard. Tire local mustard manu a
turer was given a mild uppercut in recent Bulleti
lie counter-moved, last week! He wrote to his
paper explaining very mildly that his produc
really very nice mustard! Anyone else wou
seen the pain wanted a poultice in anothei P
altogether. There are a thousand oversea ines
goods waiting to be “outed” ; there are retai
to help “out” them, and there’s a public wi in
buy anything that’s recommended to them
enough; yet, since the war started, how m an -V
local products have been getting a push! a
a shelved tariff and a sleeping Morgue o - a
tures the Teuton will be able to walk righ in
old stand before the peace signatures aic if
QUITE A DIFFERENT MATTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Good Lord ! What muck this is !” he saio,
And held his editorial head,
While scratching with a vicious pen
The pars sent in by writing-men.
“Their facts are nearly always wrong,
And ev’ry blankv thing’s too long!
These fools can’t write!” he cried with groans ;
“They ought to be out cracking stones!”
His fierce pen scratched and slashed each page,
For he was in a cold, grey rage,
And worked like one who thought he could
Write with his feet stuff twice as good.
In spite of his immense distress
The paper duly went to press,
Was published, sold and even read!
“Don't like this issue!’’ someone said.
“Don’t like it?” yelled the editor,
And scared the critic. He was sore.
“If you had brains you wouldn’t say
Such things ! You give yourself away !
“The brightest paper that we've gd*!
In it you’ll never find the rot
There is in others by the mile.
Don’t like it! Well, you make me smile!”
The critic did not speak again,
But turr/ed and ran to catch a train.
The editor puffed out his cheeks,
And scolded him for several weeks.
N.S.W.

Here are some Facts about the Plant as it is to-day.
It is the largest automobile manufacturing plant in the British Empire, and
one of the largest in the world.
Builds the famous Canadian Ford for Canadians, and for all the British Empire
outside of the isles themselves.
Built more than three times as many cars this year as any other automobile
factory in the British Empire.
I urned out more than 3600 cars in a single month this spring breaking all
Its own previous records.
Has more than nine acres of floor space.
Has sold more than 50,000 Canadian Fords to date.
Has more than a thousand machines of various designs and for various purposes
in its machine shop, having a combined valuation running into the hundreds of thousands
of pounds. Most of these machines are designed especially for the manufacture of
the Ford Car.
Bought its material this year for a minimum production of 40,000 cars — 160,000
wheels, 200,000 lamps and other materials in proportion.
Bought more than 15,000 tons of steel this year for its output. Most of this
steel was the world=famed vanadium and was heat=treated in the Ford way.
Employs approximately 2400 persons at the home office factory, nine branches in
as many principal Canadian cities and at its branch at Melbourne, Australia.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Conscription, or proscription?
Acting Prime Minister Pearce, in a mani-
festo to N. S. Wales Premier Holman: “My
Government does not feel,” etc. It’s nearly
time there was a little less of “my” Govern-
ment and a bit more of Australia’s Govern-
ment.
■ The other day Westralian Premier Scad-
dan suddenly thought of something new to
say. In a sorrowful voice he wailed that
the State was too large for him to' manage,
and harangued for a new set of Parliaments
and an imported Governor for the nor’-
west of the continent. If Scadd-an were an
advocate for Unification; 'which* he is not,
there might be something On his utterances.
As it is there isn’t; 5 Still it serves to keep
people quiet till' he can bolt to London as
Agent-General. Wjiat about a census of
Agents-Genefal in London?
At a dinner given to Aslnnead Bartlett in
Melbourne the other night a note of prac-
tical patriotism was sounded by Tate, Minis-
ter for Education. He remarked that Bart-
lett’s descriptive articles about the Austra-
lians in war had done much to make the
public proud of the soldiers and grateful to
them whilst the row was raging, but the
trouble in the future would oe to keep the
memory of their service green in the public
mind. To which end the Department was
doing its best to bring State School children
to a full knowledge of the cause of the war,
and of the great interests at stake, so that
the old soldier of the future would not be
a matter of indifference to the men and
women of the time. Australia is incurring
liabilities which will have to be shared by
the next generation and the one after that,
and if the children are shown the way to
think about these liabilities —-especially if
the way runs parallel with a towering
tariff wall —all will not have been in vain.
QUITE LIKELY.
AUNTIE: “You’re a lucky little girl to have
a letter written to you. Nobody writes
to me.”
LITTLE JANE: “Poor Auntie! Perhaps if you
ask President Wilson he’ll drop you a
Note.”
An oft-repeated tragedy was mentioned by
S. M. Herald in its biographical note on
the death of John Winthrop Hackett: —
Nine years before he had declined knighthood, but,
in the meantime, though late in life, he married, and
that made all the difference.
“He married, and that made all the differ-
ence” —it might be chipped on the tombstone
of many a man who started as a good demo-
crat and finished as a good husband.
“O.K.’s” dissatisfaction: —
In the interests of society at large, I rise to pro-
test against the hush-it-up policy of the Adelaide
coroner and police authorities in the matter of the
late L. Cohen’s tragic end. Cohen, who admittedly
had many enemies, was found dead or dying in his
pyjamas under conditions which indicated that he
had dropped from the window of his bedroom in the
hotel where he was last seen alive. The allegation
that he was quite sober wuen he retired to his bed-
room makes it impossible to understand how he
could have met his death as he did, unless he jumped
or was pushed out of the window —a fate which
might befall any of us. If the Labor party is quite
satisfied with the scandalous official eagerness to
accept the pleasantest possible explanation of the
cause of Cohen’s death I wonder how different would
be its attitude if Joe Cook or Iceberg Irvine were
found dead under similar circumstances, and the
authorities agreed to dispense with a public inquiry
into the cause of death.
