L. F. Giblin, who was a member of the
Tasmanian Assembly till it was dissolved
the other day, will not be a candidate for
the next Parliament, lie holds rank as a
captain in the Intelligence Corps and has
offered himself for active service. Had lie
not broken an arm he w r ould long ago have
robbed, the Tasmanian Parliament of the
distinction of being the only one of Aus-
tralia’s seven borrowing agencies that has
nor sent a member to the Front.
“Bystander": When Steve Barker, sec-
retary to the Trades Hall Council of
Victoria, went up to the Senate James
Munn filled the old man’s shoes at the hall.
Presently Munn was found
drowned in Hobson’s Bay, and
Charles Gray succeeded to the
secretaryship. After a time
Laurence Cohen, by trade a cut-
ter of letters on stone, w r as made
assistant-secretary. Now Cohen
is dead, killed by falling from
a window at an Adelaide hotel,
where he was staying on his way
back from an attempt at inter-
vention in the Broken Hill
trouble. Cohen was a leader
in the Victorian Labor move-
ment ; but he was an “advanced”
type of Laborite. Since he was
president of the Political Labor
Council and held a paid job at
the Trades Hall he was paraded
by the anti-Labor press as a
fearful example; and he figured
too often as the author of senti-
ments which caused pain and
vexation to better-balanced toilers in the
cause. Cohen stood twice for the City Coun-
cil, and polled well on the first occasion.
The second time he tried he ran third, and
never tried again. Mrs. Cohen is a sister of
Jack Lemmon, M.L.A.
After prolonged cogitation over the
inspiring questions related to hotels and
I'lossie, Tasmanian Royal Commissioner
Ewing recommends that legislation be passed
forbidding a publican from serving liquor to
the frail sisterhood, while protecting Bung
from the legal consequences of a mistake
either way—that is, whether he serves a lady
who should not have any, or refuse drink
to a woman of exemplary character who
doesn’t look like it. But Ewing says nothing
about protecting the vendor from the
consequences if a lady whom he takes
for what she isn’t lands him one with a
handy bottle.
“Billy Blue”: “Chasuble’s” story of the
attempted stampede of the Pope from Italy
to Spain (B. TO/2/T6) is similar to one
told on the same subject in the just-
published Life of John Hay, the American
statesman. Hay relates that in 3<SUb he
was having a conversation with Dr. Smith,
Professor of Theology at Fide Cqllege, Rome,
when Smith told him that some few years pre-
viously. when the Curia was much perturbed,
Pius IX. said to Lord Odo Russell, the
English envoy, “I suppose if I am driven
out of Rome you will let me come to Malta,
will you not?” Ixird Odo consulted the
British Foreign Secretary (Lord John Rus-
sell), xvho immediately answered. “Whenever
his Holiness desires it, an English man-of-
war will be at his service at Cavita Veechia
to take him to Malta.” This despatch still
exists, and Dr. Smith says it is the only
document that has passed between the two
Governments on the subject. Thus it ap-
pears it was to Protestant England the Pope
looked for a refuge.
Writing on the coming general elections in
Tasmania, Hobart Mercury lowers this
down :
Labor rule or German rule, it is all a matter of
degree, and with very little difference even in that.
We are not speaking of the German rule of an in-
vaded country in war time, but of the regimenta-
tion, the hobbling of minds and the oppression of
spirit which are part of their system. Liberal prin-
ciples are based on freedom, and are incompatible
with anything else. It is not possible to conceive
of the people of Tasmania accepting with eager
willingness German rule- on the ground that German
business methods are good, or that money is
more easily obtained! from German banks, or
because German administrators would do so much to
develop the resources'of the State. But in its e sen-
tials Labor rule is German rule, a fact which has to
be taken most seriously into account.
That’s the kind 'of thing party politics
breeds.
The A.W.U., always the sanest of Aus-
tralian unions and the one that has suffered
least from the demoralising effects of pene-
tration by the frowsy adventurer with a
machine-made pull, was asked at the recent
conference of delegates in Sydney to turn
down arbitration and make “direct action”
part of its programme. The proposal was
thrown out, and wisely. Any other decision
would have split the organisation into frag-
ments within a week. A month’s trial of
direct action would smash the remains into
little pieces and undo beyond repair the
hard, laborious work of a quarter century.
Arbitration has never been regarded as the
end of the section for Labor, though it knows
if to be defective. But it is a darned safer
foothold than a bladderful of hot air.
There was a heartbreaking appeal in the
Sydney papers the other morning for the
lend of a second-hand blacksmith’s forge, an
anvil and a hammer, a cart and a horse.
And in case anyone with a spare gimlet
might imagine the implements asked for were
wanted by Kitchener and rush along with his
borer it was explained that they were re-
quired for the use of volunteers who are pre-
paring land upon which to settle Billjim
when he comes marching home again. So
it looks as if grappling with the great influx
of population that is going to arrive later on
is not going to be so difficult after all.
FORM AT A GLANCE.
A Totalisator Bill is to be introduced into Parlia-
ment with Government sanction. —News item.
N.S.W.: “ Note, what’s he ringing in?”
Any victory which either side in the war
manages to secure is promptly followed by
explanations on both sides as to how it
occurred. The losers usually try to make
the point that the victors succeeded by
vastly superior numbers and a massing of
guns, while the victors point to the superior
dash and gallantry of their soldiers. In
the recent Russian success at Erzerum,
however, all the explanations, allied and
enemy, agree on one point: that lack of
railways for the transport of supports was
mainly responsible for the fall of the
fortress. Which is an admission that has
a message of particular point to Australia
and to Australian politicians who are
howling for a railway merely from Dead
Dog to Leggermuttongobilly.

A CHANGE IMPENDING.
HE: “If you accept me it will make another
man of me; but if you refuse me I shall
never be the same man again."
“Leo Lear”: Among multitudinous ex-
cuses advanced by the Victorian Government
experts to explain why Portland should not
get a share of this year’s wheat export is
a hint that the port needn’t expect any con-
sideration in the future. One of the “ex-
perts” lately let the hen out of the coop
when referring to the new railway north
from Hamilton, which is to tap the wheat
area, with the much-advertised intention of
bringing cocky’s produce to its natural port.
This line when completed will —so the ex-
pert said —give Portland a much closer aver-
age mileage to the wheat country than Mel-
bourne. “But, as the line is being laid with
light rails, it will still be cheaper to take
wheat to Melbourne,” etc. Why the thunder
are heavy rails not used, when the authorities
know that the traffic for which the line is
built requires them? The Maroona to Mel-
bourne line, via Geelong, was built for wheat
carrying, and heavy rails were of course put
down. Why ? Well, because the business
pointed direct to the same old congested
centres where votes are thick. Preferential
rates have gone overboard ; Preferential rails
do the trick instead.
“Nobby” : The recent rise in wages to the
Newcastle miners has not made them much
richer. The coal trade of the port has
dropped to less than half its usual volume.
One pit has worked only six days in six
weeks, and another six days in four weeks.
If the trade gets any worse some of the pits
that are nearly worked out will close down
for ever. The men in the district are asking
for relief work of the usual futile kind out
of loan money. If the Government would
ship from Newcastle the wheat now hauled
over a hundred miles to further congest the
railway and loading facilities in Sydney, j
would save the country thousands of pounds
and would allow the rolling stock to mo\e
a lot of the wheat that will probably rot a
wayside stations. There are plenty of sllP . l .J
available, and the coal cranes could n
wheat as easily as they do the coal. 1 11,
would give useful employment to man}
the men who are asking for doles.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Before another issue of The Bulletin is
produced the Australian Cup will be a blessing and
a curse of the past. Two and a half miles require
getting over, and there are not many horses in the
business worth backing to see the job right out.
Patrobas, winner of last Melbourne Cup and V.R.C.
Derby, was under a cloud for a few days lately, hut
the Bar Vons took no risks, and kept him a firm
favorite, a position Mrs. Widdis’s champion is likely
to hold at the start. Patrobas ran two good races at
the V.A.T.C. meeting just concluded. Carlita, who also
has a V.R.C. Derby' to her credit, is sure to see the
long journey through, and will be going faster than
most others in the last four furlongs. Eric Connelly’s
fine-looking hurdler Grand Fleet, erstwhile Klinger,
is another which should be there. These three look
like providing the winner, which may be Carlita if
it isn’t something else.
* * * *
Newmarket Handicap, run down the Flem-
ington straight six furlongs next Saturday, is one of
the hardest prizes on Australian turf to win, and
Saturday’s streak is as open as any of the preceding
streaks over the same locality. The defeat of Cyklon
and Redfern at Caulfield on Saturday gave hundreds
of punters a pain, and both prads have passed out of
the calculations of eleventh-hour bettors. One nag
that must have a great look-in is Eurobin, despite his
failure in Oakleigh Plate when favorite. Eurobin
was badly away in the start for the shorter race and
didn’t have the best of passages subsequently. But
Eurobin had recently been first past the post in six
successive races. Another that ought to have some-
thing to say in the argument is Amata, and others
likely to be in the deciding line are Woorak, .Burra-
badeen and Merrimee.
* * * *
The mystery of Redfern was the topic of
the hour at Caulfield last Saturday. In the first
place Redfern was said to be a goner for all engage-
ments. The nag that cost £5300, or thereabouts, had
fallen down twice in his box whilst being taken to
the course and was more or less injured, it was said.
But he walked to his stall in the “birdcage” (No. 13
stall—“unlucky for racers”) looking little the worse
for the upset, though there was a nasty cut below
the knee and small gashes in sundry other places.
Nevertheless Redfern when his wounds had been
dressed looked as capable of galloning as any un-
damaged nag on the ground. He didn’t look like a
bargain at £5OOO all the same, nor did he*give one
the impression of being wound-up to win the Futurity
Stakes, with the Newmarket Handicap to follow. Just
a nice, “classy” sort of horse, good-tempered and
game. Not the flier he would need to be to wiu a
Newmarket with 9st. 91b. Yet Redfern had certainly
been backed down to a short price for the (joining
event, and Ike Foulsham had talked of running first
and second with the English my'stery and Woorak.
Seeing that Redfern’s wounded leg didn’t fail, the
owner and trainer decided to let him have a go in
the Futurity. There was no rush to back him. He
went as high, or as low, as 8 to 1 in the betting
whilst the other English horse Cyklon was quoted
firm at 2 to 1 on the strength of his easy defeat of
Traquette a week previously. And the race! Neither
Redfern nor Cyklon was ever in it, though the for-
mer did get away smartly enough. Traquette made
the running as usual, with the long-expected Mahara-
jah close behind. Eventually the colt (7st. 71b.)
ran away from the mare (9st. 121 b.), and Carlita
came third. Cyklon’s had running was only to be
accounted for by roguishness. Redfern’s performance
left the crowd a choice of four conjectures. The
costly animal was feeling the effects of his fall, or
he is not acclimatised and fit, or he has lost his
English “form,” or he was not bustled on account of
recent accident. Anyhow, he seems to have blown
out as far as his Newmarket chance is concerned, but
the writer reckons that Redfern may be quite equal
to a startling reversal next time he runs. He looked
well and happy when rubbed down after the race.
In the last race of the day—a six-furlong flutter
—Woorak (lOst. lib.) seemed to hold everything
safe until the favorite, Amata (Bst. 81b.) came along
and beat him. And in the Woodcliffe Handicap (11
furlongs) Flash of Steel beat a dozen triers and
non-triers, thus proving that the big, strong, English
horse should alwa3's have been trained for long
journeys, rather than pitted against the sprinters
and milers. Flash of Steel, with only 6st. 121 b. up,
won the Futurity Stakes last year. He then looked
to be a colt of stamina rather than speed.
* * * *
“0.1 v.” : The in-and-out running at Caul-
field messed up all the early theories about the
Newmarket Handicap. Any" one of 15 horses
might be reasonably tipped at time of writing, and
if Maharajah starts favorite the odds “bar one”
should be fairly tempting. The top-weights (includ-
ing Redfern, if he is started) are bound to be
fancied. Cyklon meets Traquette on much better
terms than when he beat her, but the great mare
appears to be coming on. Woorak meets the danger-
ous Amata on better terms than when the latter
won last Saturday. Burrabadeen meets Tullia on
better terms than in the Oakleigh Plate, and Blague,
last year’s winner, carries only 71b. more this time.
In a multitude of chances there is no wisdom in
fancying anything very keenly. The writer prefers
two that are about the best handicapped on their
best form—Eurobin and Red Signal. The latter ran
respectably behind Maharajah and Traquette in the
Futurity Stakes, and will have a big. pull in the
weights on Saturday. A punter might do worse
than follow Red Signal through the meeting.
One Christian Moses didn’t have turkey for
last Sunday’s dinner. Sequarious landing Rosehill
Handicap the previous day was the cause of the disr
aster. Barney Allen, one of the brethren, owns the
nag and sent a wire to Rosehill offering a tenner on.
The bookmaker who made the bet laid his comrade
£2OO to £lO in his own book. Somebody else knew
about the horse’s chances and accepted 100 to 6, 100
to 7, and even 100 to 8, while 100 to 4 was going at the
death knock. Sequarious will soon be 10 years old.
He became Allen’s property as far back as 1910 when
in the New Stakes at Randwick he put in such a
paralysing finish that Allen rushed in ahead of scores
of others and bought the brute for £l5OO. Sequarious
has been a disappointment ever since, and cost his
owner a hatful of bullion. He (the horse) must be
near the end of his tether now.
* *■ * *
Two years ago Wallalo won the Aus-
tralian Cup, but the inconsistency of the perform-
ance compared with one just previous, struck the
stewards so forcibly that the horse was ordered into a
paddock. Provided Wallalo is still sound his name
should shortly be appearing in the entry lists again.
He won his Cup in hollow fashion, and was a solid
favorite.
* * * *
La Carabine —sometimes nicknamed “the
feminine Carbine” —a famous racing mare a few
years back, died three months ago. She won the
Australian Cup, Sydney Cup, Champion Stakes
(twice) and other long-distance races. Everyone
expected that she would be a mother of excessively
fast sons and daughters, but she proved an utter
failure at the stud. Rupert Clarke, her owner, was
very proud of La Carabine.
* * « *
“Mujik” : The vocabulary of the turf and
the boxing ring is in each case about the most
expressive on earth. The racing man has phrases
and terms into which meaning is packed in large,
comprehensive lumps. The pug can express in a
dozen words—sometimes in one —as much as the
average man can convey in a column. Yet by some
singular sequence of events it happens that sporting
literature is as dull as the record of a hen-laying com-
bat, even though its acolytes have found the dic-
tionary. inadequate for their purpose and have coined
new words by the score to put a coating of gilt
on their dope. To read a Yankee account of a fight
is like tackling a new language. Thank Heaven
Australian sport, even though its literature be of
unexampled dulness, has kept itself free from the
spurious devices by which American sport is given
exaggerated and eccentric importance. A queer
example of this was given in one of the Melbourne
dailies lately' when in the account of a scrap the
disastrous experiences of one pug were attributed
ARCHIBALD
YUILLE,
Hon. Treasurer,
T.R.C.
to receiving a succession of “enervating rights” from
the other chap. There is a compelling simplicity
about this term for welts on the jaw which does
infinite credit to the man who dug it out of himself.
Superior people speak of the enervating climate and
the enervating influence of Socialism, hut could any-
thing be more enervating than a punch on the nose
from Darcy or “Knock-out” Brown ? If so I don’t
know what it is, unless it may be waiting from six
o’clock at night for the pubs to open next day or
watching the prad one has backed at even money
toiling painfully along at the wrong end of the race.
“Trot”: That scheme (B. 17/2/’IG) for
dividing owners and horses up into classes and fixing
jockey’s mounts by ballot misses the innards of
things. A far better system would be to make all
trainers civil servants and vest the nags in the
Crown. The result would be to ensure the horse
getting a competent valet and absolutely hamstring
P. Enterprise, particularly if the price of yearlings
was fixed by a Board of Control. The business could
be worked this way: On Reefer buying a colt he
would at once proceed with the anlm .l t he office
of the Minister for Punting, who w t.l 1 demand
particulars of what the owner required. L, for in-
stance, he wanted to run the animal a couple of
byes as a two-year-old and then bottle him up for
the Newmarket, this would be nlaced on record, the
animal handed over to a State trainer and the
owner’s wishes faithfully obeyed. When the colt
ran at two years he would be nominated as a stumer,
and anyone who backed him would be protected
from winning by disqualifying the jockey if the
brute came to light. Then when the Newmarket
happened along the colt would be delivered at the
post by the State accompanied by a sworn declara-
tion that he had undergone a thorough preparation,
was fit and well, and had in a trial six months
before trotted the distance in lmin. 371 sec. If he
won the race the State would take a percentage of
the winnings. If he didn’t, the owner could have
him back, but in no circumstances would the
animal be allowed to compete again except when
attached to a milk-waggon. There are sreat advan-
tages in a scheme of this kind especially to poli-
ticians who could get the strength of things first
hand, and I hereby make a present of the not on to
whoever is behind the gun in NJSAV. as something
Holman might incorporate in the Tote Hill.
“Carfax”: Purely in the interests of scien-
tific research something should be done to investigate
the physical condition of the person who makes
loud remarks at races and matches and boxing con-
tests, and other pastimes, the object being to dis-
cover the kind of bacillus he is afflicted with. At
last Randwick meeting the writer stood next to an
individual who appeared to have backed a horse in
the steeplechase whose rider’s name was But.
“Steadv, Bill!” he roared from the stand at the start.
(The animals were half a mile away.) “Steady, pie
hoy. Ah ! That’s it; that’s it! Hold him 1 Then
a wild burst of “Lettim go, Bill!” smote the air as
the starting gate flew up, and from that on William
was pursued with copious advice about the treatment
of his nag and the strategy of the occasion. Bill and
his dromedary arrived last into the straight, and
while the crowd was cheering the finish there were
frantic yells for the fate-comer to hurry up and
“make a name for himself.” This class of pest is
frequent at football matches, howling dementedly to
Sam and Mick' and Dodger to leap over the goal-
posts, or something, and groaning horribly if some-
one on the opposing side does the leaping. At the
temple of stoush the prayers of the loud-mouthed one
drown the stroke of the gong. T’other night at
Sydney Stadium there was a voice coming from
somewhere clamorously urging O’Connor to wipe Gil-
more off the face of the earth, and it prevailed like
a circular curse all through the fray. “Go it 1
blared the noise, regardless of what was happening
or likely to happen. “Go it!” “Go it!” “Go it!’’—
the hideous reiteration, pointless and fatuous, sounded
like the nightmare howls of a demented auctioneer.
It got so badly on O’Connor’s nerves that he didn’t
go it worth tuppence.
* * * *
The luck of the game! A country woman
went back home with a new bonnet and a smile
after a recent visit to Sydney. When she got on
the course she had ss. in silver, a 10s. and a £lO
note. The coins melted in the first race, and as
she knew something with a chance for the second,
she rushed into Israel with what she thought was
the scrap of paper for 10s. and had a flutter at 8
to 1. The nag* won easily, and when Ike weighed
out £9O she nearly fainted. She had put the
tenner on in mistake, but she didn’t hand the
winnings back. Neither would Ikey had he won.
* * * *
“Musketoon” : Having discovered the only
sure method of dealing it out to Ikey a punter at
Forbes (N.S.W.) sailed into Sydney on February 19.
The idea was to have £1 each way on the last-
quoted individual horse at Ascot pony races, taking
the Herald quotations ,4s a guide. By the end of
the fourth race the punter was 255. behind, but
Snip, in the fifth, made the credit balance £2 15s.
as he ran third and started at 15’s. The sixth
brought the balance down to 15s. again, and the
seventh put him 255. behind. After that Ikey got
it on the neck, for the eighth race saw Rainbow,
quoted at 20 to 1, finish third, balancing things
again and leaving £3 15s. to the good. Agnes
May added another £2, and in the last Clifford fixed
everything up by starting at 20 to 1, winning, and
being the last horse quoted meant £25 for a win
and place, showing a profit on the day for the
punter of £3l ss. If McShortodds can be persuaded
to bet on this system he gets walloped every time.
(Later. —Ikey said it was a swindle and refused to
pay.) The scheme would have accumulated £39
ss. at the trots on the 21st, while at Rosebery ponies
on the 23rd Ikey’s bag would have been plundered
of a further £7O.
* * • * *
“The Oldest Inhabitant” : About the Pear-
sons, of Ivilmany Park (Vic.), whose girls are going
to marry titles or something equivalent thereto. The
founder of the tribe was William Pearson, son of a
naval captain, and born in 1818 at Kilmany Parish,
which is in Fife, Scotland. He went off to sea and
had reached a command when at 23 he landed at
Port Phillip and decided to give the ocean a rest.
He was the first settler on the Mitchell, and soon
afterwards took up a block which he called Kilmany
Park, at the junction of the Mitchell and Thompson
Rivers (named after the Surveyor-General and his
deputy, N.S.W., of which Gippsland was then a
part). He was a good pioneer, and as population
came along he acquired a string of racehorses and
bred and raced largely, being practically the founder
of the Sale Turf Club. He was successful on the
turf, but on one occasion lie had a disastrous tilt
with the press. The newspapers charged him with
making his own handicaps and letting the Kilmany
Park horses in easy. When Pearson drew attention
to these statements a half-hearted apology was given.
But the sporting writers were not satisfied, and
when the particular race they had been writing about
came off there were but three starters, two of them
being Pearson’s, which ran first and third. At that
meeting, a three days’ one, Pearson won four races
with three horses and ran second with three others
and ran, also, into a third place. He took in stakes
nearly a thousand guineas, which' was not a bad
haul on a country racecourse. The newspapers
howled again and Pearson issued a writ for
libel against the Australasian— claiming £5OOO.
The newspaper offered an apology and paid
£5O into court by way of amends. It
would have paid Pearson in cash and peace of mind
to have accepted the apology, and he knew it when
before Ireland, who led for the newspaper, had con-
cluded his cross-examination. The paper won. Pear-
son became a legislator.

The Fernand Quendreux-Leo Coyle affair
at Melbourne Stadium on Saturday night was a dim, i
assortment of clinch, smother, grin and monof™
The Frenchman was suffering from a severe attart
of embonpoint, and, in spite of the crowd’s exhnrtiL
of “The Marseillaise,” could neithei k e p
Coyle s loft off his cheekbones nor get the nethL
side of the smother. At the end of the wearing
Coyle was reckoned the lesser bore.
* * * *
Charlie Campbell, right-crossed and outed
for all time last week, was one of the biggest
hearted men boxing or any other sport had known
in Sydney. He flourished in the davs before the
Mclntosh started tile Stadium. The Golden Gate
Brickfield Hill, and the Gaiety in Castlereagh-street
were the Campbell headquarters for the unloading
of stoush. In those days unless bruisers knocked
lumps off each other they handled no money. Camp-
bell was quite illiterate. Once he told a' gatheriiL
that there were “humors about this fight bein'
crook.” If such proved to be the case Charlie pro-
mised his patrons “two more bouts and an exhibi-
tion of cutlery-drill by some naval lads in the hall"
Thousands of people have reason to remember the
dead man with gratitude, for he dipped into his
pockets to help the needy whenever an appeal was
made to him. Campbell was a true son of Ireland.
He had a great superstition about the infallibility
of the green, and flaunted an emerald scart’pin and a
harp on his watchchain. The writer remembers him
backing Francis Foy’s The Irish Horse at Randwick
in the only race that imported prad won, and anv
pug. seeking a match under Campbell’s management
and wearing a green tie was sure of a hearing.
* * * *
One of the reasons why the Vic. Hoofball
League isn’t too keen on strictly amateur football this
coming season floated into open air the other day.
South Melbourne doesn’t want to play, and will only
perform as an amateur team if at all. South’s assets
are £1750; its liabilities nil. Per contra, Carlton,
last year’s premiers, owes a bank £1650, guaranteed
by officers of the football and cricket clubs, and the
institution has just demanded that this amount be
promptly and considerably reduced. There are other
clubs in a similar bole, but not quite so deeply. Of
course, while things were booming the batik’s interest
was paid regularly, and it didn’t worry; but no club
provided any sinking fund, and when the call came
for good, reliable cash there was nothing in the till.
Carlton’s misery is only a hint of what is coining in
other directions presently. Incidentally, Melb. club
will not enter a team for the premiership until the
war is over.
* * * *
“Cog” : Concerning spectacular catches.
How about one on the Brisbane ground when the
last English team was playing an Australian XI. The
oval is encircled by an asphalt cycling track. Craw-
ford, who went in seventh wicket down, was just on
the thirties when Douglas wheeled up one for him to
hit. Crawford accepted the invitation, and the ball
went so high that I thought they’d have to send a
balloon after it. But Mead knew better. He moved
backward about 25 paces, and then found himself
against the asphalt. He balanced himself on his toes
on the edge of the grass, shot both hands high above
his head and took the drop magnificently. The shock
of the ball, however, upset his balance, and to recover
it he brought one foot back on the asphalt. The
scorer started to chronicle the eighth wicket score,
but, to the scorer’s, Crawford’s and particularly
Mead’s astonishment, the umpire held that Mead took
the catch while over the boundary line, and gave
Crawford not out. The Englishmen were dissatisfied,
but when Crawford went on to 105 they were real
wild.

An heir to a big cricketing name arrived in
Sydney during February, when the stork visited the
house of M. A. Noble and handed in a son.
* » *
Sixteen-year-old Frank Carberry tried to
obliterate the 100yds. professional swimming record
at Sydney Domain baths last Saturday. He sped over
the distance in 61 l-ssec., just four-fifths of a
second too slow. The lad intends having another
kick shortly.
* * *
The fourth annual swim-through-Adelaide
was again a good thing for the long-start men. The
course, one mile 800 yards in the still water of the
Torrens Lake, was covered in 35min. 39sec. by the
winner, Pratten, a boy hardly 16. Moon, tf om
scratch, beat this time by little more than 2imn.,
and he was giving away 9min. Nine-minute men nlie
the first three places. There were 64 starters, welufl-
ing one lady, and most of them finished the course, lne
best time was made by Suhard (30min. 49sec.), ninti.
The race was upstream this year, which —there win-
now nothing worth calling a stream —is clearly
the contestants having the broad lake instead ot tn
bottle-neck up by the Zoo. Of course, boats we
allowed to get in the way—an unnecessary privilege
when the high banks provide a natural grandstand.

More Triumphs of Voluntaryism.
A good of the rottenness of the
voluntary way of doing things, if any more
illustrations are needed, is afforded by the
ride-club system in country places. In large
centres there are generally enough enthu-
siasts to keep a decent amount of virility
in a club and see that business is carried out
in a fairly respectable manner. But in
sparsely-populated places the matter is dif-
ferent. Usually the initiation of the club
depends upon a few energetic enthusiasts,
frequently upon only one man, and the
majority of the members only join at all
because they are cajoled into it. If the
energetic minority can inspire enough inter-
est to build up a solid gang of workers, all
may go well; but it is a business requiring
not only enthusiasm and activity, but tact,
and in many cases the best endeavors only
result in ill-deserved failure. When the
initiation is confined to one man he may
be obliged to leave the district, whereupon
the movement gradually shuffles to the ceme-
tery. In any. case, it often happens that,
after all the initial “dead work” has been
carried out and the club placed on an
apparently satisfactory footing, the majority
of members take no definite interest in the
proceedings, and all the hard graft has to
be done by a few. Unless they run about
the district urging membef's to put in a
little time on the range, arrange about the
marking, see that targets are looked after,
rifles cleaned and put away, empty shells
and clips picked up and returned, and gen-
eral organisation and supervision kept up
to standard, nobody worries about them,
and a condition of dry-rot sets in which
soon reduces the club to a body of no prac-
tical value. If it is of practical value for a
country like Australia to possess a popula-
tion trained in the use of the rifle—and
there are few nowadays who would decry
such a proposition—fhe sooner this volun-
tary and haphazard system is supplanted
the better. In many districts where rifle
clubs exist there are young men with plenty
of time on their hands, and the makings of
fine riflemen in their physique and tempera-
ment ; but they are not sufficiently inter-
ested to attend the range regularly, and are
apathetic and indolent when they do
attend ; and the trouble is that nobody has
any legal power to grasp them firmly bv
the hair and shake them into disciplined
activity. If the ability to handle a rifle is
a useful asset to the State, the State should
take steps to see that everyone capable of
developing that ability does develop it with
neatness and despatch, and see. too. that
all take their share of the hard work as
well as of the easy part of lying on their
stomachs and firing a few shots, and then
sitting around on logs smoking, or going
home to dinner, while someone else pulls
the targets down and puts the rifles by and
generally acts the perspiring slave to keep
things going. To get all this done effectively
and efficiently it will be necessary to heave
overboard a few more old British ideals of
liberty and freedom and consequent jumble
and chaos, and make rifle shooting a com-
pulsory matter, and see that the compulsion
is properly applied by giving money to suit-
able individuals to boss the others. At a
time when a heavy splash about every other
week denotes that another British tradition
has been thrown overboard, and when the
local kid is already enmeshed in the toils
of a compulsory training system that very
few people have a word to say against,
despite the dreadful and dismal prophecies
that split the air when it was installed, it
would not be amiss to see if a sound and
inexpensive system of training the adult
male to shoot straight, and submit to enough
discipline to considerably improve his shoot-
ing as well as his civic conscience, could
not be devised and installed speedily. The
present system entails large expenditure
for a minimum amount of practical result,
while tending to discourage manv who-
deserve to'be encouraged for their endeavor
to do good work for a nation that takes
very little trouble to see whether its work
is good, bad or indifferent.
“Homo”: I’m not prepared to give an
offhand solution of “Edward Howler’s”
query (B. 17/2/T6) : “How does a man
know that he is dead?” but some data on
hand seems to have a bearing on the matter.
On the evidence against John Jackson for
the murder of Constable McGrath at the
1 rades Hall, Melb., the Law dealt with him
at the end of a rope. Everyone was made
fully aware of what was being done, and the
newspapers gave a liberal supply of details.
But, if there w y as any lingering doubt as to
the Law having well and truly carried out
its part, or John being under any misappre-
hension as to the result, it has since been
dispelled. In three successive weeklv issues
of the Vic, Government Gazette (Feb. 2.
D and 1G) appears a good half-column of
statutory testimony from a Government saw-
bones and nearly a dozen assorted gaol
officials, a like number of pressmen, and two
constables that they
this day (24/l/’l6) witnessed the execution of the
said John Jackson at the said gaol, and that the
said John Jackson was, in pursuance of the said sen-
tence, hanged by the neck till his body was dead.
So it seems reasonably safe to conclude that
John was settled. What took place ought
to be a warning to him, anyhow.
Rag”: One result of the riot amongst
soldiers in Sydney is that over a thousand
men have been dismissed, and the incident
is being spoken of as a beautifully swift,
definite ard satisfactory solution of the
trouble. To me it all looks dangerously
casual and expensive. If a soldier needs
only to make himself a nuisance in order to
obtain release from the army there can be
no doubt of continuous leakage. Moreover
every recruit in training costs something like
£1 per day, counting his wages, clothing,
food, accommodation and instruction. Thus
the thousand men dismissed last w r eek, who
averaged but a month in camp, meant a
monetary loss to the Commonwealth of over
£30,000. Obviously it should not be possible
for recruits to riot themselves out of the job,
after involving the country in much expense.
Another thing is that these thousand men
could be found useful toil somewhere where
they could be backed at long odds not to go
on strike before time was up,


Piccadilly Circus, London
The Directors of the largest hotel
in the Empire, or even in Europe,
a magnificent hostelry completed
and opened in June last, ordered
from us a Beale Horizontal Grand
Piano for the principal reception
room. We have no London show-
rooms, but had supplied an order for
pianos, also quite unsolicited, for
their other large hotel, the Strand
Palace. Instructions were sent to
us from London in 1914 as follows :
“ We wish you to make, for use in
“ the Regent Palace Hotel, now in
“ course of erection at Piccadilly
“ Circus : One full-sized Concert
“ Grand Piano in burr walnut, latest
“ model, with only exterior ornamen-
“ tation sufficient to be dignified.
“ We think it unnecessary to give you
“ any instructions as to quality—•
“as you will know to supply an
“ instrument of the best.”
We have since received letters ot
strong commendation from con-
noisseurs of high reputes upon the
instrument.
Even in time of war, this handsome
hotel, whose splendid reception
rooms are finished as examples of
strictly classical architecture, is
crowded daily with visitors. Any-
one sojourning in London who omits
to call at the Regent Palace, and
everyone goes to Piccadilly Circus,
misses one of the finest sights of the
great Metropolis.
There are about 1200 bedrooms, and
the whole of the arrangements are
modern, hygienic and comfortable.
The Beale Pianos^were awarded at
the great Franco-British Exhibition,
London, 1908, Grand Prize and three
Gold Medals for their points of excel-
lence, being the highest award given
for any pianos.

The Manly Council after a lot of trouble
has succeeded in obtaining fi-om the Port
Jackson Ferry Co. a rental of £lOOO a year
for the use of the wharf at the village used
by the co. This rent, the monopoly com-
plains, is dreadful, but the Council has now
received an offer from the proprietor of a
merry-go-round to lease a chunk of the beach
end of the Corso for 10 years for £lO,OOO,
or £lOOO a year. As the Ferry Co. extracts
a profit from every tripper to Manly and
the merry-go-round only gets a profit from
the small proportion enticed on to it, the
figure offered by the showman has taken the
Council’s breath away. The local policy has
always been to turn a deaf ear to these pro-
posals ; but one has to be very deaf not to
hear someone else offering to hand up £2O a
week. So the matter is being given earnest
attention.
Adelaide University has made Chief Jus-
tice Murray its Chancellor in succession to
the late Chief Justice Way. There is no
special reason why the two offices should be
held by the same man; some people think
the job of sawing off law —with the occa-
sional boredom of Lieutenant-Governorship
—quite enough for one man’s time. Murray,
however, has been connected with the Univer-
sity from boyhood, has held most of the
offices connected with it, and was already
Vice-chancellor. He would pretty certainly
have been Chancellor w T hether he w T as Chief
Justice or not. Law and education being
the two great interests of his life, he' can
probably do justice to both.

IN BANANA LAND
Shortage of labor threatens to seriously
affect the cutting and milling of the cane
crop this year. Mackay district expects a
record harvest, and everywhere except
in parts of the Bundaberg district the
crops will be heavy. Two new mills
—the South Johnstone and Burdekin
—will turn their rollers for the first
time, and expect to put through about
150,000 tons between them, whilst the Gov-
ernment Babinda mill, which crushed 51,000
tons last year, anticipates handling from
125,000 to 150,000 tons this season.
“Daniel” : When Labor lost the Wide Bay
seat, chiefly through mistakes of the
State Government, the usual crop of Tory
prophets rushed to point the sinister writing
on the wall. How well their prediction is
likely to be fulfilled was illustrated on
Saturday in the biennial Metropolitan Water
and Sewerage Board election. The Labor
leagues nominated four candidates for the
four vacancies, and all were successful. The
Labor bunch polled more votes than the other
eight candidates put together. And this on a
roll restricted to property owners and house-
tenants. In the face of this re-
sult it is up to the aforesaid seers to re-read
the stars.
. “Itan” : Fruit-growing is going to be a
big thing in Queensland, For banana land
in the Blackall Range country over £lOO an
aefe has been paid. Choice fruit at times
brings Bd. a dozen in the markets, while 2d.
pays. The pineapple men are also net-
ting' much coin, chiefly as a result of their
having supplied the demand for the Mac-
gregor variety, which is rapidly supplanting
all others. The Macgregor pine seems to
have had its genesis at Bulimba, but no one
seems to have traced its origin further back.
Most probably it is one of the seedlings of
the Acclimatisation Gardens. An acre of
these pines will yield 2500 fruit, averaging
4Mb. weight, which keeps well and never
blackens at the heart. That’s why it is
named after Rob Roy.
Dave Bowman, who passed out last week,
was one of Labor’s pioneers, and was a con-
temporary of Lane, Mat Reid, Mabbott and
other early democratic prophets. Bowman
was a Bendigo boy and commenced life as
a bootmaker. Drifting to Queensland he
entered Parliament as Labor M.L.A. for the
Warrego, and after sustaining a couple of
defeats settled down as representative for
the Valley. When the big split took place
in the Labor party in 1907, and some 17
members were practically expelled in a
bunch, Bowman was chosen leader of the
straight-out remnant, a position he retained
until Ryan succeeded him. He owed such
political success as he attained to his un-
doubted goodness of heart and his genuine
belief in the Labor movement; but some of
his views, such as his bitter opposition to
any form of immigration, and his habitual
and uncompromising denunciation of the

employing tfhlasS, were narrow and con-
tracted. 'No politician had more personal
friends in Brisbane —or deserved more. He
-was 55 when he passed out, after having
seen his party experience every variety of
Fortune’s mutations.
“F. Paul”: Here’s an example of how a
Labor Government is assisting the small
farmer. The Babinda Central Mill, run by
the' Government, recently issued this notice:
Owing to the unexpected rapid development of the
area for cane-growing purposes, it is anticipated that
the present cane supply will exceed the mill's crush-
ing capacity. You are hereby notified that before
undertaking the preparation 6f all further areas for
cane-planting that approval must first be obtained
from the mill management.
1 his is how it works out. There are four
cane-growers in the Babinda district whose
aggregate cultivated areas are considerably
over one-half the total area under cane.
There are also several others with compara-
tively large areas. Not one of the four culti-
vate the land personally, or even live near it;
they each have a business in Cairns, 40 miles
away. Their areas for this year’s crushing-
run from 500 to 800 acres each. There is no
doubt the cane supply will exceed the mill’s
crushing-capacity, and it is hopeless for any-
one to look forward to any fresh cultivation.
The consequence is that the small cane-grow-
ers, some of whom only planted 10 to 12
acres last year, and anticipated extending
their cultivation this season, will be hard hit.
Some of them will have to sell out, and it
would not be surprising if the whole district
eventually passed into the hands of about a
dozen monopolists.
“Bill”: Mayor Reed, of Maryborough, has
found some use for the rabbit-skins which he
recently acquired with his new job. Several
of his friends wagered he was not plucky
enough to decorate himself with the furs.
However, Reed conducted his first council
meeting in full regimentals, and the local
hospital has tendered its thanks for an un-
expected £!).
“Wig”: Pressure has apparently brought
the Government to the verge of d 'iug some-
thing with Police Commissioner t thill, who
earned the bitter enmity of Labor during the
big tramway strike. Cahill’s billet holds good
until 1917 by special enactment, but it looks
as if an abridgment were probable. After
all Cahill only did what he was appoint< d
or ordered to do, and that’s more than can
be said for some of the Ministers who would
like to drop him overboard.
“Itau": Premier Ryan is, perhaps, the
most finished artist in Australia in the hand-
ling of deputations. His plan is to get a
speaker to make a statement, and then cross-
examine him on it. With the information
gained he proceeds to cross-examine the next
man on the same item. Almost invariably
there will be a difference on a material
point, and that’s where Ryan applies the
Iv.O. The other day the beef kings went
along to have a talk to him about the com-
ing killing season. Most of them arrived
111 motor-cars and shepherded by a solicitor,
who had been briefed for the occasion. Ryan
nailed each speaker to one point: How much
did he want for his beef on the hoof?
►Scarcely two views were the same, prices
varying from about 4fd. to sid. But the
average agreement was that the all-
round price should not be less than
4gd. per lb., as against previ-
ously agreed upon. “So,” "said the
Premier, with a smile, “this deputation is
not asking anything in the way of cheaper
meat for the men in the trenches?” Letting
that sink in he added that he had to con-
sider the Imperial authorities, Empire inter-
ests, the men in the trenches and local
consumers. H e was just about to unfold
the Lmon Jack when the deputation seized
its hat, bundled into its elaborate car and
rushed for a reviver.
The Psalms.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No more they lift the anthem sweet
In yonder temple grey ;
The God who once was throned there
No longer holdeth sway ;
11 is shrines are still —His praise is done,
llis worship past away.
’Tis true a quavering psalm at times
The passer-by may hear;
Some devotee may lift the note
And wipe away a tear;
'That lonely treble only makes
Desertion doubly clear.
Away on yonder Eastern plain
And yonder Gallic hill
.Another psalm by night and day
Swells up with giant thrill ;
Doubt not that they who chaunt it there
Must worship with a will.
In endless line from North to South
How roll the battle-cars;
And what a fervor fills that song
That smites the ruddy stars —
So sings Mankind whene’er from Christ
It turns to bloody Mars.
QUEENSLAND.
F. HORN.

('apt. “Bob” Chambers, of the 9th Bat-
talion, who has been twice wounded but lias
now rejoined his unit, is engaged to an Eng-

Enid Maealister, second daughter of the
James Macalisters, of Milton, murmured the
usual "I wills” to Claude Anderson, of Red-
cliff station, llughenden, last Wednesday. The
bride wore a modern, quite-short frock and
a veil. Her two bridesmaids, one of her sis-
ters and a daughter of the squatting Hoods,
wore pink. A small girl and a. boy in white
satin were also included in the procession.
After the wedding breakfast Mr. and Mrs.
Anderson left for the South.
Another matrimonial contract which will
have been fixed up by the time you receive
this gossip is that of Miss Annis Biganto,
youngest daughter of ,J. Siganto, who owns
much property near the Coomera River and
is known locally as the “King of the Coom-
era,” and a Brisbane man. Alfred Acton.
L. C. Groom, of the Toowoomba Chron-
icle, has gone into camp. lie is the fifth
Chronicle man to get the khaki habit.
Tolmie, M.L.A., is also khaki-clad these
days. He has been appointed to the com-
mand of a transport.
The Women’s Mutual Service Club fare-
welled its secretary, Mrs. Macfarlane, on
Monday night, and handed her a few items
representing their good wishes. She is leav-
ing for N.S.W., where her other half, Cyrus
A., is pushing a journalistic quill.
Nora Collison’s play, “To the Day,” has
now been published. Anyone who buys a
copy gives the Red Cross a helping hand, and
also obtains several speeches that might be
recited with the minimum amount of danger
to the performer, at concerts and such-like
dissipations.
Thanks for Christmas billies still pour in.
One Billjim concluded with this threat:
“I’.S. —I am 24 and dark.” Another wrote
in a somewhat injured strain to his lady of
the billy to the effect that he still had two
feet, and that only one sock was included;
also, he reproached her for sending a “Made
in Germany” pocket-knife. A knife of British
steel, two socks and a full apology are
already on their way to calm him.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
‘'Bill”: After three generations of the
family had kept the anvil ringing, Campbell's
smithy at Ipswich has put up the shutters.
In 1842 Donald Campbell landed in Ipswich,
then called Limestone, and undertook to
shoe anything foaled. In 1857 the concern
passed to the famous Hugh Campbell. From
the Condamine to Brisbane almost every
horse had given Hugh its paw. Bullockies
and racing men reckoned he had no equal
in the world. Police horses have been shod
by the Campbells for the last 70 years.

MALTED
MILK
A squadron of the Australian Flying
Corps is hovering on the verge of
departure for- England, where it is
understood the outfit will be attached
to the Royal Flying Corps for in-
struction. There are men in the squad-
ron, from all States, each flier having some
special knowledge. Particular interest
attaches to C flight owing to the fact that
a number of wireless operators are attached
to its personnel. When he goes aloft
the wireless operator will be accompanied
by a pilot and an observer. By virtue of his
mixed job he wears an arm-badge showing
streaks of white lightning enclosed in a
pair of white wings. The squadron will
probably be located at the Hendon aero-
drome for about four months, by which time
it is expected that most of the men will
have sprouted a gold star in the customary
place.
Just to show that Australia can do some-
thing beyond strike, a record was estab-
lished in Sydney the other day over the load-
ing of the P. and O. Co.’s Medina, which
was discharged, loaded, coaled and made
ready for the homeward voyage in five days.
Similar operations usually take 1G days;
but the Medina was required to get away a
fortnight earlier in order to cover a hole
that the Hun had made in the co.’s sched-
ule. The vessel arrived on Friday; after
discharging some cargo she went into Wool-
wich dry-dock at noon oh Saturday; came
out, in 24 hours, cleaned and painted ; was
coaled by Monday; and ready for sea on
Wednesday. Yet many of the unionists who
helped probably think less of their achieve-
ment than they would of a smoke.
“J.P.”: Re the Bosch magneto CB.
20/1/Y6). It is good to hear that British
and Australian makers are now able to turn
out something as good as the Bosch. It’s
about time they did. But. to give the devil
his earnings, the Bosch high-tension mag.
was a wonderfully efficient device. It solved
the ignition problem as far as the internal
explosion motor was concerned. Bosch did not
invent the magneto at all. One Simms
had a lot to do with it, but the intricate
machine was perfected by a humble
mechanic from Maoriland. He got the idea
that made a high-tension magneto possible,
and Bosch stole his brains and then dropped
him. A year or so back he was again earn-
ing a crust at The Bluff.
Ringing up San Francisco from Noo Yark
on the new transcontinental telephone line
is an impressive business. It is the longest
call in the world, 3400 miles, and the cost of
three minutes’ conversation is £4 6s. 3d.! If
the talk overflows that limit an additional
28s. is demanded for each extra minute.
Whether the talker has to go on doling out
28s. a minute when he is cut off in the middle
of his yarn and has to spend £2B for 20
minutes consumed in getting him connected
up again isn’t stated. Probably it is only in
Australia that the ’phone acts that way.
An ingenious individual na.med Godward
in Invercargill (M.L.), where the pubs are
dry, has an idea that a new type of car-
buretter he invented for internal explosion
engines is a gold mine. The ordinary car-
buretter has always been looked upon as a
crude sort of affair that only turns about
half the petrol, that goes through it into
vapor. The other half reaches the cylinders
in a liquid state, fails to ignite, furnishes
no power and is blown out through the
exhaust. Godward’s idea practically vapor-
ises all the petrol long before it gets to the
engine, because it breaks up the spirit at
the start and atomises it before it reaches
the gas-making stage. So nothing but dry,
cool vapor comes from the carburetter and
is delivered to the engine, where combus-
tion is perfect. The device makes any
car easy to start, gives uniform efficiency
and quiet running at all engine speeds, and
gets nearly all the energy out of every ounce
of petrol. It has been exhaustively tested
on 21 different makes of cars, and in
every instance is said to have increased the
distance that can be run by them on a given
quantity of petrol from 25 to 100 per cent.
The inventor is backed by a syndicate, and
there are stories that Noo Yark is fran-
tically howling for him to put his own price
on the rights for Wilson’s country.
I CAN’T AFFORD TO DIE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, not for me the glory call for Country and for
King!
I dare not let my spirit soar when calling bugles
sing;
For I am not a care-free youth—l have some kids
around,
And I must keep my mental feet firm-set upon the
ground ;
So when the battle standards stream and wild
cheers split the sky,
I tell the heroes as they pass, “I can’t afford to
die!”
I can’t afford to die! Dear me, I am a married
man !
Let young men for the Present bleed —l for the
Future plan !
Oh, I must fret and I must sweat and take things
for my cough
Lest civil Death should pounce on me and bear me
swiftly off;
I’d love to live one crowded hour and see the
splinters fly,
But reckless deeds are not for me; I can’t afford
to die.
The posters pasted on the walls disturb my peace of
mind ;
But, bless you. I don’t lose my head, for I am not
that kind ;
I pass them by as I have passed Black Ruin’s gilded
brink
And ads. that call on me to be a man and take to
drink ;
I daresay I could drink and charge if I might have
a try;
But I am battling now with Life—l can’t afford to
die.
Though I have often sighed for rest —deep rest that
knows no end,
I have to wake and rise at six to get to work, my
friend ;
And I’ll go on as best I can until my strength is
fled,
And die of something lingering, ingloriously a-bed.
I hate the undertaker with his speculative eye,
And I’d love to be a hero—but I can’t afford to die.

FAST EATING CAUSES DYSPEPSIA.
Few people chew their food sufficiently,
and to this fact is ascribed a good deal of
dyspepsia. The first action of the digestion
of food occurs in the mouth —mastication
and the mixing of food with saliva. Too
often food enters the stomach in a more or
less unbroken state and remains undigested.
A famous London doctor used to bluntly tell
his patients that the stomach is not a giz-
zard and resents being put upon. The late
Sir Andrew Clarke made his patients count
their bites. He said that every mouthful of
animal food required 32 bites, and he made
them count accordingly. Persons who have
been careless in their eating, or from some
other cause are subjects of dyspepsia, should
try the tonic treatment for their complaint.
They should avoid haste at meal-time and
take a course of I)r. Williams’ Pink Pills to
restore stomach tone. They act directly on
the blood, and the first response from the
stomach is a better appetite, freedom from
distress after eating, and an increase in am-
bition and energy generally. Try Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills as a stomach tonic and see
how your general health improves. These
pills are sold by all medicine dealers. —Anvx.
A BILIOUS TONGUE.
When your tongue is coated evenly, all
over, with a whitish-yellow color and your
breath is bad the chances are that you are
bilious. If you also have indigestion, con-
stipation, headache and a general ill feeling
there is scarcely a doubt about it. Your
liver needs attention.
Harsh cathartics which purge violently
and then produce a re-action only aggravate
the trouble. Gentle assistance is what nature
needs to establish regular habits of health,
and Pinkettes, the little pink laxative pills,
free from harmful or habit-forming drugs,
meet the requirements most admirably.
Send for a free trial sample to the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney, mentioning
The Bulletin. Pinkettes are Is. a bottle
at all medicine dealers. —Advt.

BOOTS have come back again and no mistake.
But such dreams of boot 3, up-to-date,
dainty, delightful, fit accompaniment to the
flounced taffeta frocks of the day, -which
combine smart modernity with old-world charm.
Paris and London both are now tripping to work
and play in short-fronted, high-heeled, suede-
topped boots like the Bostock style here illus-
It is a smart little boot, there is no doubt
about that, and every bit as comfortable as it is
smart. The beautifully fitting last on which it is
made sees to that, and lots of women, who have
never imagined they could wear a short-fronted
style, will be amazed to find how comfortable it
is, how jolly it makes their feet look.
Then for those who want something different,
who prefer shoes perhaps, there is a fine range of
styles to select from, all as up-to-date and charm-
ing as this model, and all safe to wear well and
feel as peculiarly one’s own as if they had been
made to measure.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Maoriland Railway Department doesn t
stand round with its hands in its pockets
moaning about soldiers refusing to pay then-
fares to the nearest pub. Instead it pro-
ceeds to make the irregular business a
mighty expensive one. One cove in khaki
who recently decided to try the great Aus-
tralian dodge and refused to part up the
cost of a ride from Trentham to Wellington
(M.L.) was told by the beak that the bill
would be £3 9s. sd. instead of Is. 4d.
* * * *
“Midchannel” : An institution which calls
for "the application of the hobnail boot, or
a rise in salary or something, is that pait
of the P.M.G.’s Department in Sydney to
which dutiable parcels are sent. When
there is reasonable belief that a parcel
contains dutiable articles the addressee is
requested by circular to attend, so that it
may be opened in his presence. \\ hen he
attends this is the process: Hand the notice
in. Interval of half an hour. Meek inquiry
if there is any chance of getting the business
through before peace is declared creates
another interval of five minutes. Clerk
then disappears to find out the pos-
sibilities. He returns after more in-
terval to remark that it will be
through “in about an hour.” lliere
are usually 20 people waiting while halt a
dozen officials stand round exhausted by the
repose. Under the circumstances Webster
ought to add a line or two to his circular
warning the person addressed that the value
of his parcel can in no way compensate for
the amount of time he will have to w r aste in
getting hold of it.

“D.S.F.” : The n<*ed for refineries of pot-
ter's raw material (“Dry Shave,” B.
10/2/’10) is indeed urgent. Local clay
invariably contains iron, which in the fui-
nace turns nasty and runs all over the
china, making it as streaky as a tote com-
mission’s reputation. Samples of white
day from Stawell (Vic.) were recently
handed to importers of potters’ goods, with
a view to placing the dirt on the. local
market. With local refineries still in the
clouds and freights to overseas refineries
soaring out of reach, it had to be turned
down. The only present demand for the
clay is a small one by the paper mills and
Stawell will find an atom of comfort ip the
war if it causes a leap in their output.
A draft of nearly 40 mules has been sold
in an Adelaide sale-yard by Norman Rich-
ardson, of S.A.’s far north-west. The ownei
announced that he could never have earned
on his mail contracts without the mule. (He
has plenty more at home.) The brute does
not eat as much as a horse, finds its own
food more readily, recovers quicker when
knocked up, and lives much longer. 1 had
one that was still going strong at ob, and
doing useful work, when poison ended its
career.” The draft sold readily, up to about
£l5 a head.
* * * *
Maoriland put a monoplane under the
auctioneer’s hammer the other day. lhe
machine was a Bleriot imported by a French-
man who was going to start a flying school
in the Isles, but the Tricolor caught linn in
its folds, and he rushed home to
be killed while flying over the Hun trenches.
Was an air machine ever sold at auction
before? Bidding started at a fiver, and the
Bleriot, with a 50-h.p. engine, was knocked
down for £62 15s.

“Moana” : Four years ago a Gerhog com-
mercial traveller called on a well-known
Christchurch (M.L.) manufacturer in quest
of an order. The Maorilander intimated
that he made things instead of importing
them, and added that such goods as he had
to get from afar he preferred to be British
Then poor old Berlin lost his temper and
blurted out: “Veil in 1914 der Kaiser vill
speak about dis prohibition of Sherman
goodts from behind ’is Sherman guns !” The
threat disappeared in a waft of laughter at
the business-getter who couldn’t keep his
temper. But when war and 1914 arrived in
company it was remembered. Now, did the
peddling commercial just howl out the right
year by accident, or was he a friend of the
Kaiser’s? Perhaps he teas the Kaiser.
WHEN THE BILLS COME IN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wo had ham and tongue and turkey,
We had puddings, cakes and pies ;
All the kids were (like the Cratchits)
Sage and onion to their eyes ;
There were bottles in the ieechest,
And the cupboards looked like bars,
And the house was filled with fragrance
From some extra-fine cigars.
We went picnics in the country,
We made parties to the play,
We went boating up the river
And excursions down the bay;
In a tiptop car we jaunted
Many a dizzy, day-long spin!
Oh, we had a boshter Christmas —-
Till the bills came in !
Oh, father had, a Christmas!
There were bulges in, his hat,
And we found his boots and waistcoat,
Filled with prawns, upon the mat;
He brought mother home a crayfish
And some mussels in a tin ;
But she never lost her temper
Till the bills came in.
Now he dines on sodawater,
And his gloomy brow is knit
O’er a mighty pile of dockets
Ending briefly, “Please remit,”
While he bids the prattling infants
Cease their (mustn’t-say-it) din;
For poor father’s rather nervy
Now the bills come in.
When the dolls have gone to pieces,
When the drum is beaten flat;
When the big tin trumpet’s broken
(Though we scarcely grieve for that),
When the gay balloon is busted,
All the weary months begin
With an overloaded postman —■
Yes, they still pour in.
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.

Rexona Girl.
Miss JEAN
ROBERTSON,
from a photo, by
Kenelm Stump.
Miss JEAN ROBERT-
SON, one of the most
beautiful 'of the bevy of
Rexona girls, has never
had to toil up from the
lowest rung of the Stage
Ladder. She jumped into
a big part on her first
appearance with the Wil-
-loug h b y management.
Among her many striking
successes during a two
years’ stage experience
have been her Lilian Mait-
land in “The Silence of
Dean Maitland,” and her
Princess Venetia in
“Under Two Flags,” with
Ethel Buckley, another
Rexona girl, in the lead as
Cigarette. Miss Robert-
son, who is an Adelaide
native, writes: —
“Years ago I heard of your wonderful Rexona from a friend of
mine who could not find words to give it praise enough. As I was
needing something of this kind for an injured hand, I purchased a small
pot, and I congratulate you on your wonderful remedy. It is certainly a
most marvellous healer, much in advance of anything I have ever come
across before. lam also going to try a cake of your Rexona Soap, for
I hear that it is splendid, and am going to see for myself if it is in
keeping with Rexona. Rexona and I shall always be friends in the
future, and I will never lose an opportunity of telling my friends about
it-” (Signed) JEAN ROBERTSON.

THE RAPID HEALER
This Week’s TOILET HINTS
INTERESTING SELECTIONS FROM THE WORLD'S SMARTEST BEAUTY ARTICLES.
SIMPLE RECIPES MOST EFFECTIVE.
How to Discard an
Unsightly Complexion.
“Toilet Club Notes.”
How many women exclaim as they behold
their ugly complexion in the mirror, “If I
could only tear off this old skin!” and, do
you know, it is now possible to do that very
thing V Not to actually remove the entire
skin all of a sudden; that would be too
heroic a method and painful, too. I imagine.
The worn-out cuticle comes off in such tiny
particles, and so gradually—requiring about
teu days to complete the transformation—it
doesn't hurt a bit. Day by day the beautiful
complexion underneath comes forth. Mar-
vellous! No matter how muddy, rough,
blotchy or aged your complexion, you can
surely discard it by this simple process.
Just get some ordinary mercolised wax at
your chemist’s, apply nightly like cold cream,
washing it off in the mornings.
Why Have Grey Hair?
When a simple, old-fashioned and harmless recipe
will correct it.
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes. A
very old, home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
an ounce of concentrate of tammalite and mix
it with four ounces of bay rum. Apply this
simple lotion to the hair for a few nights with
a small sponge, and you will soon have the
pleasure of seeing the greyness disappear.
This recipe is perfectly harmless, is neither
sticky nor greasy, and has given perfect
satisfaction for many generations to those in
possession of the secret.
A Strange Shampoo.
“Cosy Corner Chats.”
* * * I was much interested to learn from
this young woman with the beautiful glossy
hair that she never washes it with soap or
artificial shampoo powders. Instead she
makes her own shampoo by dissolving a
teaspoonful of stallax granules in a cup ol
hot water. “I make my chemist get the
stallax for me,” said she. “It comes only in
ilb. sealed packages, enough to make up
twenty-five or thirty individual shampoos,
and it smells so good I could almost eat it.
Certainly this little lady’s hair did look won-
derful even if she has strange ideas of a
shampoo. I am tempted to try the plan
myself.
Blackheads Instantly Go.
The new sparkling face-bath treatment gives instant
relief.
A very simple, harmless and pleasant pro-
cess is now used to remove blackheads and
correct greasiness and large pores in the skin.
You have only to drop a tablet of sty mol,
obtained from' the chemist’s, into a glass of
hot water and bathe the face with the liquid
after the effervescence has subsided. The
blackheads will then come right off on the
towel. The enlarged pores immediately con-
tract to normal and the greasiness disap-
pears, leaving the skin smooth, soft and cool
and free from blemish. But to make sure
that this desirable result is permanent, it is
advisable to repeat the treatment several
times at intervals of say about four or five
days.
Is Powder Necessary?
“Practical Suggestions.”
I say emphatically, No! There is a simple
lotion which can be easily and cheaply made
at home, and it is at the same time both
effective and beneficial to the complexion.
Cleminite is a splendid substitute for face
powder, which is at the bottom of many
complexion troubles. Get about an ounce
from the chemist’s and dissolve in four table-
spoonsful of water. The result is a fine clear
liquid, which instantly gives the face, neck or
arms that peach-like bloom of perfect health.
There is nothing to equal it for greasy skins,
and the result lasts all day long under the
most trying conditions. [4

The Ocean Ways.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ra iHE ocean ways are wonderful, the
'I ’ sailing: tracks are wide,
A We steam in with a fair wind and
slouch out with the tide;
And ’tis “Drive with a blunt bow and stag-
ger to the rise
At the beck and call of Commerce and a
pair of pretty eyes !”
Forsworn to fetch and carry down the roll-
ing roads of Trade,
Our pretty babes are cradled and the ringing
keels are laid ;
A thousand bonny painted boats are moving
down the slipfc,
For the building of a Nation is the building
of its ships.
And the wide seas, the grey seas, are free
for us to roam,
Till homeless we can twirl the spokes and
make the ports of home ;
’Tis hey! the drumming sea-rain, and the
gallop of the gales,
And the sheeted mists of ocean, a-marching
past the rails.
The sailing roads are casual, the sailor
ways are slack,
But I’ve a tidy w'ife at home and so must
hurry back ;
An’ ’tis kiss me lass and hug me tight and
slip from off m’ knee,
For the packet’s at the pier-head and she’s
trimmed for open sea!
By all the music in the note the streaming
scuppers raise,
The skirling of a stalwart wind within the
funnel stays,
The roaring of a racing screw, and all the
magic rhyme
Of Ocean’s joyous Spring Song when the
engines beat the time ;
By kiss of mouth, by sob of babe, by cling-
ing of a hand
We learn the message in the call which rings
from land to land ;
And ’tis outward! and homeward! the
course is straight and plain
Though ’tis homeward! and outward! to
quest the seas again.
Oh, all the life we may not live upon the
open seas
We cast off with our mooring lines upon the
busy quays.
And the long roads, the green roads are
leading down and down,
Until, a blush upon the sky, we drop the
lights of town !
The bushland odors on the breeze steal out
and out in vain,
Like jealous fingers from the shore to draw
us back again ;
But ’tis “Johnny Bowlegs, turn you home,”
and “Johnny, get you gone,”
For our souls are under hatches and we’ve
snugged the covers on.
Oh, East and West the roads lead out, the
trading tracks are wide.
But we’ve the road of all the roads to fetch
the harbor-side ;
And ’tis swing to the long roll and drive
through the spray—
We only know the worth of Love when Love
is far away.
The sailing tracks are casual, the outward
way can wait!
A little bale of sailor-love is mixed up with
our freight.
’Tis Homeward and Homeward, to wake a
woman’s smiles ;
For the heart is on the log-line and ’tis beat-
ing out the miles !
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
The Dragon and the Globe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAVE often wondered what was the
origin and ultimate resting-place of a
queer piece of metal-wmrk shown to
me by old Sarfonetti, who ran a trading
schooner in the Pacific thirty years and
more ago. He was a strange customer, Sar-
fonetti—a Sardinian, who had worn the red
shirt and knew more about the troubled
politics of his country half a century back
than most of the people who w y rite about
the subject. And he had all the Italian’s
traditional love of music and of some of
the other finer things of life. He was clean-
tongued and clean-souled, too, though had
an Australian wowser heard some of his
stories of life up and down the world that
wowser would have turned grey at the lis-
tening. Yet it was seldom of women that
he spoke. A man of strong impulse and
nery temper before Time had sprinkled his
hair with grey ; but in the days I speak of
slow in judgment and deliberate in speech
and action.
Never once did I see him stripped of this
quality of reserve; not even when Bueletta
told him about the Greek mate. Bueletta
was a Hue woman, and the Greek —well, he
had a long tongue and the fervent Hellene
heart, and Bueletta despised him as pro-
foundly as she loved the Sardinian. You
see, w T hen Sarfonetti acquired Bueletta from
that Eurasian at Hainan he took her with
his anchor ready to break after * -wift,
silent combat w'hich left the sharks Tint a
bloodless meal. The Greek only talked,
whispering of something he had that would
make him rich. Bah! So she told Sar-
fonetti of what might happen that night,
and when she finished he slipped off his
shoes and wmnt out on deck. Bueletta sit-
ting at the little table went on quietly sew-
Vm' > ’working at some price-lists,
In 11 guess there was anything wrong, for
they had spoken in the jargon of Cambodia.
1 morning when I, not seeing him about,
made casual inquiry concerning the mate,
the woman answered: “We spik him no
more. But he was spoken of —and that
very night.
"bile Bueletta was cooking the midday
meal Sarfonetti came aft with a sea-kit. the
r \ ee i S - Beckoning me below he spilled
Out the contents on the table. He picked
the sorry-looking rubbish up, one article
after another, and tossed it back into the
bag. A shirt or two, a pair of shore-going
dungarees, a cap, and so on. Then as he
lifted a silk handkerchief there was exposed
a small box of sandalwood, studded with
nails around each of its six sides. Looking
idly on I noticed that Sarfonetti stared with
uplifted hand as a man might who had sud-
denly found a pearl or a skull. But in a
flash he whipped up the box, wrenched it
open, and sitting down on the locker, turned
into his hand from the box a dull, silvery
globe, about four inches in diameter. He
rolled it slowly over, pulled it with a polish-
ing movement along his thigh, then went
behind the bamboo curtain which screened
his berth, and the rattle of keys followed.
The kit and the rest of the things went
over the side soon after.
That Sarfonetti was worried soon became
clear. Onfie for over an hour he sat on
an upturned dingy lashed across the hatch,
looking steadily in one direction. Three or
four times before nightfall I saw him go
to his berth and heard the rattling of a key.
Bueletta watched him with the feline
patience of Asia. But Bueletta never knew
what ailed Sarfonetti that day. Perhaps
she never cared after the sun had sunk.
“Come, my friend, I have not smoked this
day”; and as we sat on the break of the
little poop in the moonlight Sarfonetti spun
me the yarn of the box and the globe. I
knew it would come, for I knew that the
old pirate loved me well, though I was not
of his race and was young enough to be his
grandson. For had not I, a stranger, broken
the arm of a drink-maddened coolie who
had stabbed him once and was driving the
knife home again one night in Batavia?
“This,” he said, drawing the sandalwood
box from his pocket and opening it, “has
been looked for by me, by the agents of
the owner and of people you may have heard
of —searched for how many years? I do
not know—but fifteen by me. Once I saw it,
but I never wanted to see it again, for I
fear it may.... Well, it is like the Mafia —
In morte ,” and the broad shoulders touched
the speaker’s ears.
“I tell you how it was. Igo to Garibaldi.
With him I am at Calatafimi, Castel-fidaro
and Vulturno. There I was wounded and
there this thing began for me. Amongst our
prisoners was Pelledino, a Neapolitan and
old, whose daughter had married one of the
Perugio, once friends of his house, for it
was a Perugio -who had married his father’s
sister when Murat was king ; but they had
gone no one knew where. If the children or
grandchildren of the vanished pair could be
found, then the Perugio estates would pass
to them, for that was the direct line. But
this Perugio who had taken his daughter!
Curse him for an upstart who came in by
the side-door —a cousin’s son, bah ! He died
reviling the new Perugio (for the old man
had no son, and the girl’s husband would
win on that hand, too), but rejoicing that
a brother had sons to
“In time it is good for me to go to sea
again, and I drift away, now here, now
there, and one day buy this schooner at Sam-
bawa. I trade for a year or two anywhere
—away to the Ladrones, the Marshall
Islands, and when Bueletta came aboard we
went as far as Manahiki. Then to Singa-
pore again, and there a Spaniard came' to
me and we bargain for a run to Manila with
cargo for the Jesuit priests. He paid well;
was very anxious for me to go. On the
night we arrived I knew why. A boat came
alongside and there stepped aboard two sons
of the Church —one an old man, wearing red
on his neck. A long talk we had together,
he and I, alone.
“ ‘My son,’ he said, ‘stories come to us
sometimes of a man living somewhere in these
seas who avoids the world. We would like
to find him. If you can find him your re-
ward will be generous.’
“It seemed a mysterious thing this to look
for a man who did not want to be found.
I had other things to do, and said so. The
answer of my visitor was that all he wanted
was for me to listen on my trading trips,
and ask questions and let him have any clue
I might discover.
“‘And of what name is this man?’ I
asked.
“ ‘Perugio.’
“ ‘Any relative?’
“ ‘My elder brother.’
“A familiar name, and in a minute or two
I remembered —Vulturno and the Pelledino
who died. Of him I speak to the priest and
of his sorrows. ‘The same family,’ said the
Jesuit, and tells me more —that the Perugio
of Murat’s time was his father; that the
young noble had been obliged to seek
safety in Spain; that there he and his
brother were born. Something had happened
(it did not matter what) and the brother had
disappeared. He was sought now for two
reasons —first, to discover whether he had
left legitimate issue, and. secondly, that he
might yield up an heirloom which the father
had carried from Italy in his flight. This
was a silver globe, of much value and coveted
by someone of a very exalted station.
“And so he left, and back we went to
Singapore and wherever a dollar could be
■earned —even to Fiji, Tonga and the Mar-
ouesas, anywhere. Then one day at Vavau
the next page was opened. A cutter came
in from Penryn that night. Its native crew
pulled alongside and there climbed aboard an
old man.
“He was seeking passage on his way to
the Gilbert Islands. Ilow far should I take
him? ‘All the way,’ I told him.
“A strong, bold-faced man this, who, so
far as I could judge at the time and believe
now, preferred the semi-savage life to civi-
lisation. He showed little interest in such
things as I could talk about. Had I been to
Manila? Was there a chance that I had
heard of a Monsignor named Perugio? He
was well? He sought answers to these
questions and made no comment. When I
tried to draw him into conversation he
quickly slid off on a fresh tack. So I let
him alone and could see he was thankful.
On the day we hove-to to land him he
beckoned to me.
“ ‘Sarfonetti,’ he said, ‘next time you are
in Manila go you to the Monsignor Perugio
and tell him you have seen this’; and he held
before me this thing I have in my hand now.
‘And tell him, too, that one day it will go
to him.’

Not always'on account of age, for
many old people have straight backs

backs.. They are bent with the pains
of kidney disease, lumbago—uric
acid in the muscles of the loins, con-
gestion of the kidneys—the pains
that make the young feel old, and
the old feeble and shaky.
Rest in bed may ease a bad back,
but it cannot cure it. Only a medi-
cine that reaches the cause can do
that. Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills
are solely for- weak and ailing
kidneys. They help the kidneys to
throw off uric-acid poison, which is
the cause not only of backache and
lumbago, but of gravel, stone, rheu-
matism, dizzy attacks, irregular
heart, and that heavy, drowsy- feel-
ing. Doan’s Pills regulate the
urinary system like ordinary medi-
cines regulate the bowels; they
release the accumulated water in
dropsy, and relieve bladder weak-
ness, which is so common in children
and the aged.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are
pure; and cannot injure the most
delicate patient.
