‘‘Thus he went, and I, instead of laying for
Singapore, worked to Manila. The Mon-
signor paid me well. ‘You did right, my
son,’ he said, when 1 told him that I had
resisted an impulse to bring his brother to
him. ‘lt would have been a great scandal.
We shall find him now.’
“They tried hard. They failed. To me
he was but a memory.
“Six months ago there came to me at
Singapore the Greek Asolsofa, bearing a
letter and a package. The letter stated that
the writer, being about to die, had entrusted
the bearer with a parcel for delivery to me,
and that he hoped this would be carried to
Monsignor Perugio. The Greek’s story was
that he had been mate of a Batavian
schooner, and at the Ladrones was asked to
visit a native village. There he saw a white
man, and from him received the letter and
package, besides a substantial sum of money
for himself. - The white man had said very
little, according to the Greek, except that
he would not live more than a day or so,
perhaps only a few hours. So I brought
Asolsofa aboard here and sailed again to
Manila. The reward was good, but it came
not from the old Monsignor, for he was
dead. The head of the Order promised to
send the parcel to the relatives of Mon-
signor. To-night for the first time I know
one thing and suspect another —one that
Asolsofa took the only thing of apparent
value from the parcel made up for
him at the Ladrones; the other that
the drink in the Malay whose kris
reached my shoulder that night in Batavia
was a Grecian’s gift. Now I have this
thing what shall I do? Look at it.” And
Sarfonetti went into the little cabin.
There he put into my hands the globe, the
work of a fourteenth-century silversmith.
It represented the world as it was then
known, with Africa cut off short, with the
coasts of China and Japan straggling away
to nothing, with the Americas so absurdly
portrayed as to be scarcely identifiable.
Yet, strangely enough, Australia was on the
globe—or. at any rate, a large island was
there. But what riveted my attention most
was a two-headed, dragon-like creature with
its body across Central Europe, one fantastic
mouth being stretched in the direction of
Italy, the other neck carried across
Asia Minor towards what at the time the
maker of this queer thing had lived was the
unknown land afterwards called Australia.
Did this mean anything, or was it merely
a piece of artistic license? Sarfonetti
shook his head. “I do not know; but it is
beautifully done.” And so it was—exquis-
itely-—the idea being wrought into the metal
with a wondrous craftsmanship.
“And what will you do with this?” I
asked.
“Ask me some other time. I must think,”
he said, and locked the thing away, not to
speak of it again until the day I received a
letter which brought Sydney very close. “I
must go by the first steamer,” I told him.
“Why?”
And I told him that too, and he answered :
“Yes, and when you go you shall carry that
globe. I have it still.”
This, then, is how it happened that a
small box was deposited with a Sydney
bank, which held it untouched for seven
years. Then one day it was claimed and
carried to an office in the city, where two
men who had arrived the previous afternoon
from Singapore were waiting. One of these,
addressing the other as Sarfonetti, paid over
a sum of £2700, and placing the box in a
handbag said he would catch the Melbourne
express that night. The list of passengers
by that week’s outward P. and O. steamer
contained the name of Dr. Torfette. Three
months later Sarfonetti came to Sydney
from the coastal settlement he had decided
to end his days in with an Italian paper six
weeks old. It had an account of a sensa-
tional murder at Naples, where Dr. Torfette,
who had just returned from a pleasure trip
to Java and Australia, and was about to
marry a daughter of the Perugio, had been
knifed a few hours after arrival.
Now what was the meaning of all this, and
why was Sarfonetti so careless? And, above
all, what was the meaning of the later news
that one Pelledino was proved the murderer?
And of the stories published in American
papers that the crime was planned by mem-
bers of a society having agents in Manila
and the Straits Settlements and Australia?
Sarfonetti? Did he through a foolish Greek
work a double on the Church and hold on
till the market ripened? Perhaps. He said
nothing. He only shook his head, and,
maybe, it was because she never asked that
he grew so fond of an Australian bambino
and left her his money. She knew him only
as a white-haired retailer of queer stories
about two-headed dragons that sometimes
come out of the sea to swallow careless
people ; and he had a strange habit of call-
ing her Bueletta. I know this because the
bambino, a woman now. is my daughter.
Yes, he was very simple was Sarfonetti.
So much so that he never offered to tell me
why he was paid for the dragon-infested
globe, nor "did he express the least inclina-
tion to know what became of it. Yet I, who
saw only the ripple on the pool, so to speak,
would give something handsome to-day if I
could find out where that globe is now, and
why it was so prized.
MUJIK.
Amongst My Own People.
THE END OF LOG PADDOCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOME time back I wrote that Oow-
coclcyism and Cow were the end of
Log Paddock, the District and My
Own People, but I know now that they
weren’t. For the other Sunday I read that
Log Paddock had been leased by a syndi-
cate and was to be turned into a stud
farm, and "it was hoped it would give a
great impetus to racing in the Mudgee
district.”
They have thought and dreamed and
talked of and bred and trained horses for
little else but racing in the Mudgee district
for as far hack as my memory goes, and for
the last 10 or 20 years the younger men
have thought and dreamed and talked of
nothing else —unless it be girls and dancing.
And in other districts as well, I suppose.
Draught and oilier useful horses had to come
by chance —and take their chance. Race-
horses, with their clothes, were ever on the
roads and are ever on the trains. They’re
amongst the earliest memories of my child-
hood. So I don’t see what “impetus” racing
needs in the Mudgee district. Nearly
every boy’s ambition was to be a jockey,
when it wasn’t to be a mounted trooper —or
a bushranger. Jockey came first.
Opposite Log Paddock and across the
“Guv’mint Road” stands what used to be
“Our Hill,” with slopes or “sidings” above
the frost-line in the severest winter, and
nooks or “pockets” which were always warm
in the sharpest weather; where the cows
used to go to calve, and the cattle camped at
nights when it was bitter cold on the flats.
I suppose the old selection will be part of
the stud farm, and stables built up there for
brood mares—for warmth in winter and
coolness in summer. And the old house
(where- the local papers say I was born,
but I wasn’t) —where we fought drought
and ruin and death for so mani years—
will be a camp for the cigarette-smoking,
spitting weeds they call jockeys and trainers.
I was never on the sacred soil of a race-
course in my life —except perhaps to pick
buttercups and daisies at Mudgee, when we
were children, and the crusty old jockey-
caretaker seemed to look on even that as a
sacrilege, and would shoo us off. Later
on, when I went there to shoot hares, I
was shooed off again—because my gun
might upset the nerves of a brood mare or
two who boarded at the back of the grand-
stand. Even the offer of a hare now and
then couldn’t bribe the caretaker. So it was
a good sanctuary.
There was a lockup, or log or slab strong-
room or “dead-house” at the course, for the
convenience of the mad-drunks who were a
natural result of a combination of horse-
racing, bad liquor, mixed drinks and mixed
religions. Before that they used to be hand-
cuffed to a bullock-chain stretched between
two stumps, ten or a dozen at a time. Good.
jolly old Father O’D used to go out
every race meeting to “keep the boys in
order” with his buggy-whip; but they said
with, a wink that he mostly had a horse
running, too. The cemetery is quite close.
Across Log Paddock from Our Hill, and
across the “crick” are the bare, scalded
slopes where Germany grew the vine long
years agone and made money when the
“rushes” came, maddening the diggers with
new wine and making them throw away
the gold they'd come so far to win. Often
they threw away their lives and souls too.
But Germany also grew wheat and fruit
and pigs and sheep and cattle. There is
little, or few-, or none of these now. Those
slopes grow nothing now —not even the
native bush or grass; for, uproot them as
you will, those old vineyards have spoiled
the soil for anything else for long to come
—maybe a hundred years or more. There
are vineyards on the upper flats now, where
wheat used to grow, and the abundant,
heavy wine is ruining local youth and com-
pleting the ruin of local age.
I don’t know whether there’s truth in
wine, but I know there was madness in
our kind, and there is plenty sad proof of
it. Beer may be bad enough in cities, but
I know the effects of that cheap, new or
“doctored” wine in our districts. I sampled
some of my native vintage at a “wine” shop
(there are many of them) on my native
heath, so to. speak, when I last set foot on
it on a visit some holidays past, and the
effect was startling no less to myself than
to my friends. I’d arrived on good Aus-
tralian beer, happy but sober. It took
three days and several gallons of bottled ale
to repair the damage.
But sporting will have a worse effect on
Australia, as a whole, for it leads to the
neglect of the healthy vices as well as the
healthy virtues; and, like healthy virtues,
when healthy vices are neglected too long
they perish. And when both perish a nation
perishes.
So we take a last look at Log Paddock,
where nothing grows now but a little
“loosin’’ and other hay—for the keows
mostly, in time of drought.
And at the Last Shaft, which I wrote
about. some time ago—which two of mv
father s old mates struggled so bravely to
bottom” (one of them died last year as a
result of that struggle—and the* other is
hoeing grape-vines now). The shaft is a
well now for the benefit of the cow I
suppose it will soon lie a well for the benefit
o.i that other curse of ours.. horse-racin°-
Let me see. In the first place they 5 felt
they had to exterminate the kangaroo I
saw the process of extermination. With the
kangaioo (and the cattle later) went the
useful or hunting dogs that might have kept
down the rabbits. So the rabbits when
they came got rid of the farmers—and,
somehow, of the hares, too. Then someone
imported starlings and sparrows and other
birds, so the people will soon have to net
their vineyards and orchards. Then some-
one imported the bony, useless carp and
they came up the “cricks” and are extermi-
nating the edible native fish. (That was the
only real, healthy recreation near Log
Paddock—fishing.) Somebody put down a
bore in order to drain the paddock under-
ground and get at what little gold might
bo left, and the cool, pleasant evergreen
surface springs dried up; and the creek
for some reason of its own. has shrunken •
the clear, cool, she-oak. which shaded lilv-
deeked pools of my old Eurunderee Creek,
lia\e vanished, with the pleasant green
banks and sedges., the reeds, the water-cress
and the water-lilies. The very water, what
there is of it. seems dead—stale, unhealthy
and yellow, like the dead face of a dyspeptic
So this is the end of Log Paddock, of the
early settling days, the “roaring” days and
the farming days. The ’possums have gone
the magpies,.the bell-birds, the butcher birds’
the rosellas—all the native birds. Even the’
scents of the bush. And mv own people are
going toff.
HENRY LAWSON.
